Contents


Chapter One: The Night I Broke the Only Rule That Mattered
Kira
The first rule of the Veil Nights was also the only rule, because it was the only one you needed: stay inside.
Every door in Vreshka was locked by sundown. Every shutter latched. Mothers had been telling children for generations that the Veil Nights were not a time for curiosity, not a time for bravery, not a time for anything except sitting very still in rooms with thick walls and pretending the sounds outside were just the wind.
I knew all of this.
I was also the only person moving in the lower city of Vreshka. I had either made a calculated decision or a catastrophic error, and I was currently telling myself it was the former.
Drev had my money.
That was the beginning and the middle and the end of my reasoning, and I stand by it: Drev had my money. He’d also conveniently forgotten to be anywhere in Vreshka that I could reach him. For four days he’d been a ghost, and I’d been patient, because I am a patient person when patience is strategically useful.
Patience had stopped being strategically useful approximately six hours ago, when I’d spotted him ducking into an alley off the Saltfish Market and realized that he’d been in the city the whole time. He’d just been inside, because he knew what tonight was. He’d been counting on me being inside too.
A reasonable person would have been inside.
*   *   *
The lower city of Vreshka was built for fog. In daylight it smelled of woodsmoke and fish and the river mud that got into everything — into your clothes, your hair, the cracks in your boots. At night, the fog came off the Vres River and filled the streets to knee height, and the gas lamps on the main thoroughfares did nothing except make the fog glow a sickly amber, arguably worse than darkness.
Tonight the gas lamps were out. Every one. Someone had gone through and extinguished them before sundown, because light attracted attention, and attention was the last thing Vreshka wanted on the first night of the Veil Nights.
I moved by memory and ambient gray. I knew this part of the city — every shortcut, every dead-end, every shop that stayed open late for the wrong reasons. Drev’s place was four streets south, in the canal district where the buildings listed and the rents were low.
The city was not quiet.
That was the thing that had been sitting wrong since I’d left my room two hours ago — not the emptiness of the streets, but the texture of the silence. Vreshka was never truly quiet. Tonight there was none of that. It was the silence of a city holding its breath.
Everyone in Vreshka knew what the Hollow did during the Veil Nights. The descriptions were always wrong. The reality was: the world softened at its edges. Sounds arrived from the wrong direction. Distances became unreliable. And for six hours, something ancient moved through the streets.
Tonight every street felt like those streets.
The cold that came with this particular brand of wrong was different from winter cold. Winter cold was honest — it bit at your ears and your fingers and it was trying to. This cold was incidental. It was the cold of something very large moving through the air nearby, leaving a wake the way a ship left a wake, not intending the disturbance but creating it anyway.
I kept moving. I turned up my collar and kept moving, because Drev had my money.
*   *   *
I found him in the cellar of a boarding house on Tanners’ Row.
This took me forty minutes, two wrong turns, and the services of a woman named Ora who ran numbers for six different crews in the lower city and owed me a favor from last winter. Practical woman. I respected her enormously.
The boarding house cellar smelled of mildew and old wine. Drev was sitting on a crate with my sixty silver marks in a leather pouch on his knee. He had the expression of a man who had expected this conversation and had rehearsed several versions of it, none of which were going to work.
The problem had appeared.
I sat down on the crate across from him and waited. I was comfortable with silence. Drev was not.
“Kira,” he said. “I was going to come find you.”
“Were you,” I said.
“Things got complicated.”
“Did they.”
He shifted on his crate. The bottle moved in his hands. “The merchant — Petrov, the one you got the ledger for — he came asking questions. Wanted to know where I’d sourced it. I had to go quiet for a few days until it blew over.”
“That,” I said, “sounds like a problem you have. Not a reason I don’t have sixty silver marks.”
A pause. He was recalculating something behind his eyes — I could see it, the way I could always see it, the little internal machinery of a person deciding which angle to try next. I’d had this conversation, or versions of it, more times than I could count. I was very rarely surprised by where they went.
“I can get you the marks,” he said finally. “Not tonight. I’d need to access the warehouse, and tonight—” He gestured vaguely upward, indicating, presumably, the entire situation happening above street level.
“Tonight works for me,” I said. “I have flexible hours.”
He stared at me. “Kira. It’s the Veil Nights.”
“I’m aware.”
“The Hunt—”
“The Hunt is for people who are outside after the bells,” I said. “We’ll be quick. The warehouse is four streets from here, which means we’ll be back inside before the hour turns.” I paused. “Or you can give me your keys and I’ll go myself, and you can trust me with access to your warehouse, which I notice you’re hesitating about.”
He was quiet for a long moment. Then: “You’re insane.”
“I’ve heard that,” I said. “The keys, Drev.”
He gave me the keys.
We went to the warehouse. He counted out sixty marks into my hand with the mechanical speed of a man who wanted this transaction to be over as quickly as possible. I counted them again, because you always count again, and the number came to sixty, and I put them in the inner pocket of my coat where they’d be uncomfortable for anyone who wanted to relieve me of them.
Then I told Drev to go back to his cellar and stay there, and I headed for home.
*   *   *
I should have made it.
My room was twelve minutes from Tanners’ Row by the route I took — through the Saltfish Market, up the Weavers’ Steps, across the small bridge over the canal that smelled of brackish water year-round, into the neighborhood called the Warrens where the buildings were older and cheaper and the streets barely deserved the name. Twelve minutes. I’d done it in eight when motivated. I was very motivated.
I had made it through the Saltfish Market and was halfway up the Weavers’ Steps when the city changed.
It happened between one step and the next. No warning. No transition. The street was empty and then the street had a man in it.
And somewhere to the north of the city — far away, then less far, then no longer far at all — something that was not quite sound began.
Every person in Zemya knew about the sound of the Hunt. Not frightening the way a human threat was frightening, not the spike of adrenaline that came with danger you could fight or run from. This was the fear that came before thought. The kind that lived in the oldest part of the body, the part that had been afraid of the dark long before it had words for dark.
I stood on the Weavers’ Steps and I listened to the sound of the Hollow Hunt moving through my city.
And then I turned north.
Not toward home. North, toward the sound, toward the part of the lower city that pressed closest to the river where the Hollow bled through worst, and I’d taken exactly four steps in that direction before some functional part of my mind caught up with my feet and asked, with considerable urgency: what are you doing.
I stopped.
I stood very still on the fifth step of the Weavers’ Steps and looked north, at the direction I’d been walking, at the direction the sound was coming from. The sound had not gotten louder. The not-color in the sky was shifting, moving, pulled in some direction that had nothing to do with wind.
My feet wanted to go north.
I had been ignoring inconvenient impulses my entire life, and I was quite good at it, so I noted the impulse and set it aside the way you set aside a tool you don’t have a use for right now. What I had a use for right now was the twelve-minute walk home, which had become urgent in a way I was trying not to examine too closely.
I turned south.
I walked.
*   *   *
I made it three streets.
The Hollow did not stop me. I want to be clear about this, because the stories made it sound like the Hollow acted on you. It did not act. It simply changed the terms of the space you were moving through, and you adapted or you did not.
The logic of this particular shadow was that it was moving against the nonexistent light source, which meant it wasn’t a shadow at all but something else wearing a shadow’s shape.
I took the alley to my left instead.
The alley brought me out two streets over — not the direction I wanted to be going, and when I tried to correct back toward the Warrens the streets did the thing that the lower city streets did on Veil Nights, which the old scholars called membrane-drift and which practically meant that the city’s geography became slightly negotiable. Not impassable — nothing moved, nothing changed visibly — but the internal logic of the space shifted, so that the route I’d taken a thousand times felt different underfoot in a way I couldn’t account for. Like walking a familiar room in the dark and finding the furniture had moved two inches.
I stopped. Breathed. Thought.
The sound of the Hunt was closer.
I had sixty silver marks in my inner pocket — not helpful, and a knife at my hip, which was also not helpful but felt better to think about. I had the Saltfish Market behind me, the river somewhere to the north and west, and the growing conviction that the twelve minutes between Tanners’ Row and my room had become theoretical.
Every child in Vreshka was taught what to do if the Veil started while you were outside: get indoors. Any indoors. Lock the door. Do not look out the window. Do not open the door until the bells ring at dawn.
I was, in fact, a person. This was going to be very difficult to communicate through a locked door.
*   *   *
The sound changed.
I had been moving — carefully, keeping to the walls, trying to navigate by feel toward anything that looked like a main street where the buildings were sturdier and the doors less likely to be the hollow wooden kind that didn’t actually stop anything — when the Hunt shifted from background to foreground. Not louder. Oriented. It had a direction now, a specific vector, and the vector was this street.
I did not run, because running was how you fell, and falling in a situation like this was something I didn’t want to think about. I moved faster, which was different. Fast and controlled, close to the wall, watching the street ahead and behind simultaneously with the particular awareness that came from ten years of moving through places I wasn’t supposed to be.
The shadows at the far end of the alley shifted.
I stopped.
The shadows weren’t moving the way shadows moved. The air around it was the not-color I’d seen in the sky. The temperature in the alley dropped another ten degrees.
Then it stepped out of the shadows and it was a man.
Except that it was very obviously not a man. It had the shape of one — tall, dark-coated, moving with the economy of something that had never in its existence needed to hurry — but the shape was the beginning and the end of what it had in common with anyone I had ever met. Silver. Not pale gray — silver, the metal, cold and deep and without warmth.
Those eyes found me immediately.
I had my knife out. I didn’t remember drawing it. My hand was steady, which I was grateful for, because the rest of me was conducting a very rapid internal debate between the part that wanted to run and the part that knew, with absolute certainty, that running was not an available option.
The not-a-man looked at the knife.
Then it looked at me.
It said nothing. It moved nothing. It simply stood at the end of the alley with the particular patience of something that had been alive long enough that patience was not an achievement but a state of being, and it watched me with those silver eyes, and I understood — in the part of my mind that processed things my conscious thought hadn’t caught up to yet — that I had already been caught.
That the moment its attention had oriented toward this street, it had been over.
That the knife was ceremonial.
I kept it in my hand anyway. On principle.
“The Veil Nights began an hour ago,” it said. The voice was low and unhurried, and it carried the feel of something that had been using language for so long that it no longer needed volume to be heard. “You knew this.”
“I did,” I said. My own voice came out steady. I was surprised, and then I was not surprised, because I had been in bad situations before and steadiness was the thing I defaulted to when everything else failed.
“And yet here you are.”
“And yet here I am,” I agreed.
The silver eyes did something that was not a blink — a slower movement, deliberate, like recalibrating. “You are aware of what I am.”
“I am aware of what you are,” I said. I was also aware that my heart was doing something very unsteady that I was going to deal with later, privately, when I was not required to be functional.
“And of the law.”
It moved then — not toward me, not yet. Just a shift in weight, a small reconfiguration of that enormous stillness. Up close, the cold was extraordinary. Not the cold of the air, not even the Hollow-cold I’d been feeling all evening: this was the cold of something ancient, the particular temperature of a thing that had existed before the concept of warmth.
“Then you understand what happens now,” it said.
I understood what happened now.
The knife was absolutely ceremonial.
I lowered it anyway — and I looked at the thing with the silver eyes that was the Lord of the Hunt and the oldest of the Vorath and something the world had been afraid of for longer than the world had had words for fear, and I said:
“I want it noted that I had a very good reason for being outside tonight.”
The silver eyes looked at me.
Something in the long, still face shifted — not much, barely at all, in a way I wouldn’t have caught if I hadn’t been watching very carefully.
It was gone again before I could name it.
“Noted,” it said.
And then its hand closed around my wrist, and the alley went sideways, and the world I had lived in my entire life disappeared.
I did not let go of the knife.


Chapter Two: What the Hunt Finds
Aleksei
The Hunt-sense arrived the way it always arrived: not suddenly, not gently, but with the patient inevitability of something that did not concern itself with readiness.
The membrane dissolved at the third bell. Dissolved was the correct word — not thinned, not weakened, but ceased. There was no clear line between before and after. There was the Hollow on one side and Zemya on the other, and then there was only the one place where both had been, and every living thing in that place pressed against my awareness simultaneously.
I have ridden the Hunt forty-seven times.
It is not easier, the forty-seventh time, to hold that much of the world in your mind at once. What you acquire, instead, is precision. A fox in the pine forest east of the city, wary, watching from the tree line. Three birds startled awake by the change in the air, already settling back to their branches. The river Vres, cold and fast, the fish in it unbothered by any of this. A cat on a rooftop in the merchant quarter who registered the membrane dissolving, flicked its tail once, and looked away.
I respected the cat.
Six other Vorath rode with me tonight — their signatures in the Hunt-sense distinct as different instruments playing in the same key. The others at the membrane’s edge, ensuring the dissolution was clean, no tearing, no complications. The Veil Nights were largely administrative. The stories did not reflect this, but the stories had never reflected this.
I sorted through the rest. There were mortals outside — there were always mortals outside, forty-seven Hunts’ worth of evidence to confirm it. Someone returning home too late. Someone who had not heard the bells, or had heard them and made the calculation that they had time. It was simpler than the law. The law, once invoked, was not simple.
Then, in the lower city, threading through the narrow streets near the river market:
A mortal woman, moving north.
*   *   *
That was longer than I usually spent on any individual mortal in the Hunt. Home was south. She was moving north — quickly, with the specific economy of someone who was not running but who was making the calculation about whether to run and arriving, repeatedly, at not yet. Threading the membrane-drift as if she could feel where it was thickest and was moving around it.
She was moving toward the sound of the Hunt.
Not away. Toward.
Radovan’s signature shifted to my left — a wordless question. Do you need me.
I did not need him. I left the others and went after her alone.
*   *   *
Moving through the Hollow’s surface register was not walking. The membrane made distances negotiable and directions approximate. Following a mortal through it required attention of a specific kind.
The Hunt-sense threaded her trail ahead of me like a line cast into moving water.
She was good at moving through a city. The route she had chosen was not the fastest or the most direct, but it was the one that kept her off the main thoroughfares where the Watch might notice a woman moving with too much purpose.
Her mortal signature in the Hollow’s awareness was unusual.
Mortals moved through the Hollow’s surface register dimly, even during the Veil Nights — muted by the layers of ordinary living that separated them from the realm beneath. Like voices heard through a heavy door. She was not faint. She was vivid in the Hollow’s awareness in a way that I did not have a framework for and noted without conclusion.
She made it farther than most.
I found her stopped in an alley off the Weavers’ district. She was against the wall. Her eyes were moving — the gap at the alley’s far end, the door to her left that was locked, the wall she could not clear in useful time. She had already arrived at the conclusion.
She knew there were not enough exits.
She was not panicking. She had run the options and found them insufficient and was standing in the result of that with a steadiness that had nothing to do with calm and everything to do with discipline.
I stepped out of the shadows.
*   *   *
She did not scream.
In forty-seven Hunts, I had seen three responses from mortals in this situation: the scream, the run, and the blank freeze of a mind that shut down its higher functions when confronted with something outside its categories. The blank freeze was the most common. The scream was second. Running, I had found, was a decision that the body sometimes made before the mind had finished the thought, which I understood as a survival mechanism even when it was unhelpful.
She looked at me and drew a knife from her hip in one clean motion and squared her shoulders and waited.
I stopped.
The knife was not going to be useful. She knew the knife was not going to be useful — I could see the knowledge of it in the set of her expression, the way you could see a calculation behind someone’s eyes when they had already run it and arrived at an answer they did not like. She held it anyway. Deliberately. Chin level, weight even, knife hand steady.
I revised my assessment of the situation.
Up close, the Hunt-sense registered her more completely than it had at a distance. Her pulse, rapid and deliberate, the specific rhythm of someone who was afraid and had decided not to be governed by it. Her face arranged in the expression of a person who had chosen a course of action and was committing to it past the point of revision.
The steadiness of her knife hand was, I acknowledged internally and only internally, impressive.
I said nothing. The situation was apparent to both of us. I let her assess it in her own time.
She looked at the knife. I watched the calculation run. Then she lowered it — not dropped, not fumbled, lowered with the precise control of someone who was making a choice and demonstrating that it was a choice. She looked up at me.
Her eyes were brown. Most mortals could not look at a Vorath directly for any duration without experiencing an instinctive response to the wrongness of it — the pressure of something very old in a space that was not designed to hold it. She held the eye contact with what appeared to be no difficulty whatsoever.
“The Veil Nights began an hour ago,” I said. “You knew this.”
“I did,” she said. Her voice was even. Steady in the same way the knife hand had been.
“And yet here you are.”
“And yet here I am,” she agreed.
There was something in the agreement — in the specific flatness of it, the absence of apology — that I found myself paying attention to. It was not bravado. Bravado required an audience and a performance, and she was not performing anything. She had simply stated the fact. She was here. She knew what that meant. She was going to deal with it.
“You are aware of what I am,” I said.
“I am aware of what you are.”
“And of the law.”
A pause. She did not move. Intelligent — she had correctly assessed that stillness was her best available option, and she was holding it with the patience of someone accustomed to waiting out situations that required waiting. The knife was still in her hand. Still lowered. Still ready, at the level of a body that had trained itself to be ready without announcing it.
“Then you understand what happens now,” I said.
She understood. I could see that she understood. There was nothing left to say, so I crossed the alley and I took her wrist, and the Hollow shifted around us to accommodate the transition, and the law prepared to acknowledge what had just occurred.
*   *   *
And then the binding activated.
I have felt the law engage before. Forty-seven times, in various configurations, the ancient covenant has acknowledged a catch — a formality, a registration, the sensation of legal machinery completing a cycle. I knew exactly what it felt like. It felt like nothing. A notation. A door latched.
This was not that.
What moved through my Hunt-sense in the moment my hand closed around her wrist was not the routine acknowledgment of an ancient law. It was something older than the law. Something that had no interest in the law’s opinion, that was not asking the law’s permission, that had been waiting — I understood this with a certainty I could not account for and did not yet know what to do with — for this specific moment.
The sensation was not pain. It was not magic, not in any system I recognized. It was the sensation of something locking into place. A key in a lock. A gear catching. The specific, unmistakable feeling of a mechanism completing its function after a very long period of waiting.
I went still.
The alley was still around us. She was still. I was still. The Hollow’s transit had paused. I had paused it, which meant some part of me that operated faster than conscious thought had decided we were not moving yet.
I looked at my hand.
My hand was around her wrist. This was not remarkable — I had caught mortals in the Hunt before. I had physically detained mortals in the Hunt, briefly and with purpose, any number of times across a thousand years of Veil Nights. There was nothing remarkable about this specific contact.
The binding said otherwise. It was not settling into the background hum of legal obligation the way the law’s acknowledgment settled. It was fully present. It intended to remain fully present. I understood this with the certainty of something stated plainly.
I looked at her.
She was glaring at me.
Not afraid — I had already established that she was not producing fear in the standard configuration. What she was producing was something more specific and considerably more direct: the unimpressed, unintimidated expression of a person who had been caught in a situation she considered avoidable and was going to deal with it, and who was looking at the cause of the situation with the particular focused displeasure of someone who did not waste anger on abstractions but was very precise about where she placed it.
She had placed it on me.
She said: “I want it noted that I had a very good reason for being outside tonight.”
I was still looking at my hand. The binding did not feel like obligation. This did not feel like obligation. It felt like something else, something I did not have adequate language for because I had not had occasion to develop language for it in forty-seven Hunts across a thousand years.
It felt like recognition.
She was still glaring. Waiting, with the patience of someone who had asked a question and expected an answer, even if the question had been rhetorical and the answer was probably no. The knife was in her other hand. She had not sheathed it. On principle, I suspected.
I looked away from my hand. I looked at her.
“Noted,” I said.
And I took us into the Hollow.
*   *   *
Radovan was waiting at the edge of my territory when we arrived.
He looked at the woman. He looked at me. His Vorath-signature carried nothing I could read as a specific emotion — Radovan was old enough to keep his own counsel when he chose to — but there was a quality to his attention that I recognized as something he was choosing not to say.
I did not require him to say it. I was already thinking it.
In forty-seven Hunts, the binding had engaged the acknowledgment of law and nothing more. In forty-seven Hunts, I had caught mortals and the law had noted it and settled.
The binding was not settling.
I took the woman into the Hall. I gave her a room. I told the Hall’s small presences to leave her alone tonight, and I went to the far end of Nochval where the silver trees ran oldest and the ground remembered the first world, and I stood in the cold open dark for a long time.
I looked at my hand.
It told me nothing I did not already know and nothing I yet understood.
But underneath the nothing, in the register where old things communicated with patient things, the binding was paying attention to what it had caught. Not to her. To what she carried. And what she carried was not something I had a name for yet.


Chapter Three: Ancient Law and What It Means for My Immediate Future
Kira
There is a moment, when you arrive somewhere completely new, where your brain performs a very rapid triage: immediate threats, available exits, distance to the nearest other human being. I had done this so many times in so many bad situations that it happened automatically now, without input from the part of me that was supposed to be in charge of decisions.
The Hollow failed the triage in every category.
The immediate threats were not things I had words for. The available exits were not visible. The nearest other human being was, by all available evidence, not human at all, and was standing six feet to my left with his hands clasped behind his back and the patient quality of something that had been waiting in this particular space since before my civilization had invented the concept of space.
I breathed. I looked.
The Hollow was — I want to be precise about this, because imprecision would not do it justice — impossible. Not in the way that things were sometimes called impossible to indicate that they were surprising or difficult. In the way that this place operated by rules that had not been included in my understanding of how reality functioned, and the gap between what I had understood and what was currently in front of me was wide enough that I had to actively resist the urge to sit down.
The sky was the deep cycling dark of something that had never held a sun. Not black. A moving darkness that cycled through blues and greens and a purple that pressed against the eye.
The air tasted like winter and something I didn’t have a name for. Something that predated winter.
I was still holding the knife.
I sheathed it. Partially because it remained ceremonial and partially because I needed both hands to be functional right now, and gripping a knife was giving one of them a job that felt productive and wasn’t.
“All right,” I said. My voice sounded strange here — too crisp, too present, like speaking in a room with very particular acoustics. “Explain the law.”
He looked at me with those silver eyes that held the Hollow’s ambient light the wrong way — gathering it rather than reflecting it, so they appeared to produce their own dim luminescence. In the Hollow, in this light, he looked more like what he was. Less like a man in a dark coat, more like a man-shaped arrangement of very old darkness that had decided to present itself as a man for practical reasons.
I found this significantly less comforting than the alternative.
“The law,” he said, “is as follows.”
*   *   *
He told me the law the way I suspected he did everything: completely, efficiently, and with the professional remove of someone delivering information rather than having a conversation. No apology in it. No warmth. No visible awareness that this particular information was, for the person on the receiving end of it, something other than routine.
The law was as follows.
Anything caught outside during the Veil Nights belonged to the Hunter who caught it. Not a negotiation. Not a punishment. An absolute, with the weight of a natural law, built into the world’s grammar during the first separation.
“Remain,” I repeated.
“In my territory. In the Hollow. Until a full turning of seasons has passed, at which point you may petition for formal release.”
A full turning of seasons. A year. I was being told, calmly and without any particular inflection, that I had just been acquired for a year by an ancient being who lived in a realm that operated by different physical laws, because I had made the objectively correct decision to go retrieve sixty silver marks that were owed to me.
I thought about the sixty marks in my inner pocket. I thought about whether they had been worth this.
Behind me, in the alley I had just left, something shifted. Not a sound — a quality. The air had changed register, as if the street itself had noticed I was carrying something valuable and had opinions about the carrying.
I decided that was not a productive line of thinking right now.
“The memory option,” I said instead. “If you wipe the evening and return me — I go back to my room in the Warrens, and the Veil Nights just happened, and I woke up this morning and didn’t go out, and none of this—” I gestured at the impossible sky, the silver trees, the ancient being in the dark coat “—is something I know about.”
“Correct.”
“And there are no other effects. Nothing left over. I just don’t know that this happened.”
He paused. It was a very small pause, barely a pause at all, the kind of pause that I would not have noticed if I hadn’t been watching him with the attention I reserved for situations where missing things had consequences. It lasted perhaps two seconds.
“No other effects,” he said.
He said it with the even quality of a person stating something accurate, and I filed the small pause in the place I filed things I didn’t have enough information to interpret yet — a very crowded place on an average day and was filling up rapidly at the moment.
“And the third option,” I said. “Remaining. What does that involve, practically?”
“Safety. Shelter. Food.” He produced this list with the economy of someone who had covered the essentials and considered them sufficient.
“And?”
“And what.”
“Those are the conditions of a decent boarding house,” I said. “They’re also, I notice, about a third of what I’d want to know before agreeing to spend a year somewhere.” I held up a hand, mostly so I could count on my fingers, which helped me think. “What is this place, specifically? What are the rules here? Can I leave the immediate area? Can I communicate with anyone in Zemya? What does the binding—” I had not missed the word binding, said as though it was a technical term with a specific meaning “—actually entail? What are the other residents of this territory, and are they going to be problems? What does ’petition for formal release’ mean in practice? Is a year fixed or is there—”
“This place is my territory within the Hollow,” he said. “It is called Nochval. The rules are that you do not leave Nochval’s boundaries without my knowledge. You may communicate with Zemya through means I will provide. The binding means that I am aware of your general location and wellbeing at all times, and you will have a corresponding awareness of mine.” He paused. “The other residents of this territory will not be problems.”
I counted. “That was four questions. I had eight.”
“A year is fixed,” he said.
“Five. I still have three.”
Something moved in his expression. Not a smile — I was confident it was not a smile. More the way a muscle moved that was adjacent to where a smile would be, a different thing entirely, and it was gone before I’d fully registered it.
“Ask,” he said.
*   *   *
I asked. He answered, occasionally, which was less useful than it sounds.
The petition for release at the end of the year was a formal request to the law — not to him specifically, though he had input. He could deny it, though he did not say under what circumstances he would. He would not, he said, know my precise thoughts. He said this with the flat certainty of a person stating a fact and not an assurance, and it landed differently than an assurance would have.
He did not explain how a mortal was meant to survive in a realm that was evidently not designed for mortal habitation. I asked this directly. He said: “You’ll survive.” I asked how he knew.“ I asked what that meant.” A pause. “Most of them.”
I processed this. “Most of them,” I repeated.
“The exceptions are avoidable,” he said, in the tone of someone who considered the matter settled.
I noted that he had not actually explained how the Hollow had decided I was manageable, which implied that this was unusual, which implied a number of things I did not yet have enough information to interpret. I added it to the pile. The pile was architectural at this point. I was going to need a better organizational system.
“The memory option,” I said again. I was not, at this point, making a decision — I was testing the edges of things, which was how I always worked. You felt the shape of something before you committed to a position on it. “If I choose that. You can do it right now? Return me to my room, no memory, and this conversation never happened?”
“Yes.”
“And you’d just — have caught a person tonight and released them, and that would be the end of it.”
Another pause. Longer this time. He was looking at me with an expression that was not readable and that I was going to need considerably more data to decode. “Yes,” he said.
“Would that happen often?” I asked. “Catching and releasing. Is that normal.”
“It has been done before.”
“That’s not the same as normal.”
He said nothing. The silver eyes held mine without apparent effort. The Hollow made a sound around us that was not wind — something deeper, something that came from the ground rather than the air, a note that I felt in my sternum more than heard with my ears.
I had been caught by the Hollow Hunt. I had three options: death, amnesia, or spending a year in the territory of an ancient being whose name I still didn’t know.
I found a loophole.
*   *   *
I understood the shape of it now. Not the mechanisms, not the politics, not the thing they called the Claim that I could feel building in the place below language.
But: I was bound by ancient law to a dying realm. The law had teeth. An immortal keeper watched me with an attention that had nothing to do with duty. And somewhere beyond his territory, other immortal powers were beginning to notice that the realm’s long decline had stopped — and to ask what had changed.
What had changed was me. I needed to be ready for that.
“The law says anything caught outside belongs to the Hunter,” I said carefully. “But I wasn’t outside in the usual sense. I was in the process of returning inside. There’s a meaningful distinction between someone who willfully leaves shelter during the Veil Nights and someone who was caught in transit between one inside and another. I was heading home.”
He looked at me for a long, flat moment.
“You were heading north,” he said. “Away from home.”
“I course-corrected,” I said.
“After four steps.”
“Promptly,” I said. “The point is that my intent was to be inside, and intent is a relevant factor in most legal frameworks, and if this law is as old as you’re suggesting then—”
“Intent is not a relevant factor in this law,” he said. “The law is predicated on physical location at the time of the bells, not on what you meant to be doing.”
“That seems like an oversight.”
“It has been the law for several thousand years without significant complaint.”
“People who are released to the Hollow are dead,” I said. “People who have their memories wiped don’t know to complain. You’ve had a very convenient absence of feedback.”
Something happened in his expression again. The same almost-not-a-smile, slightly more definite this time. I was beginning to think it was the version of amusement available to beings who had been maintaining dignity for a millennium and were not going to compromise it now.
“The law stands,” he said.
“Fine.” I had expected this. I moved on. “The binding. You said you’re aware of my general location and wellbeing. Is the awareness constant, or only when you actively—”
“Constant.”
I did not love this. “And I have a corresponding awareness of yours.”
“A directional sense. Distance. Broad states — if I am injured or in significant danger, you would be aware of it.”
“Would I want to be?”
He seemed to consider this as a genuine question. “Probably not,” he said.
“Wonderful.” I thought about asking how I was meant to feel about having the general location and wellbeing of a Vorath lord constantly present in my awareness, and then decided that I knew how I was meant to feel about it and that asking would not change the situation. “You said the Hollow has already decided I’m manageable. Who made that determination before tonight? I’ve never been to the Hollow. I’ve never had any contact with the Hollow.”
“Have you not,” he said.
It was not quite a question. I looked at him. He looked at me. He did not elaborate, which was, I was beginning to understand, a primary characteristic of this particular ancient being: he said the things he chose to say and the space where other things might have gone was simply empty. Not awkward. Just empty, with the character of a room that has been deliberately cleared.
I filed this too.
“Second loophole,” I said. “The binding awareness goes both directions, which means we are technically in mutual contact. Most legal frameworks treat mutual contact as implying mutual obligation, which means I should have equivalent standing in any formal proceedings about the terms of my stay. If I have standing, I can petition now rather than waiting a year, because—”
“You do not have standing,” he said. “The binding is asymmetrical. My awareness of you is a function of the Hunt law. Your awareness of me is incidental.”
“Incidental,” I repeated. “I have a god in my head as an accident.”
“As a consequence,” he said. “Not an accident.”
“What’s the difference?”
He seemed to find this question more interesting than the loopholes. “An accident implies that something occurred without mechanism. A consequence implies that the mechanism produced this result specifically.” He paused. “The binding produced this result specifically.”
I stared at him. “You make the binding sound like it chose to do this.”
He held my gaze for a beat that was a fraction too long. “Ask your other questions,” he said.
*   *   *
I asked my other questions. He answered a precise subset of them. By the end of our conversation I had a map of the situation that was functional, though incomplete in a number of places that I suspected were deliberate.
What I knew: I was in a territory called Nochval, in the Hollow — a living realm adjacent to but not the same as the world I’d come from. He would ensure my safety and basic needs. I was not imprisoned in any physical sense. I could not leave Nochval without his knowledge.
I would know, at all times, roughly where he was and whether he was in danger.
I had not yet decided how I felt about this last one.
What I didn’t know: why the Hollow had apparently been paying attention to me before tonight. What the small pause before “no other effects” had meant. What the binding’s consequence — as distinct from its accident — implied.
The not-knowing was not comfortable. It was the particular discomfort of a woman who survived by understanding her situation completely, standing in the middle of a situation she did not understand completely, in a realm where the trees made their own light and the ground remembered things that had happened before memory.
I was going to stay. The death option was ineligible on principle. Which left remaining, with the significant practical advantage that a year spent inside the Hollow learning everything I could about it was a year spent on the correct side of the information gap.
Also the loopholes I’d found so far were both closed, and I needed more time to find one that wasn’t.
These were my reasons. They were sound and practical and said nothing at all about the fact that I had looked at the man-shaped ancient darkness in front of me and found it — him — significantly more interesting than I should.
“I’ll stay,” I said.
He received this with the same flat attention he’d given everything else. “I’ll show you to the Hall.”
“One more thing.” He had already turned. He stopped. “Your name,” I said. “You know mine. The binding means you know mine.”
A pause. He turned back. “Aleksei,” he said.
It was said flatly, the way he said most things — but there was a quality to it, a weight to the way the word landed. Like it was a real thing, given, and he knew it was a real thing, and he was choosing to give it anyway.
I filed this in a different place than the other things. A better-organized place. “Thank you,” I said.
He said nothing. He turned and walked toward the silver trees, and after a moment I followed, because the alternatives were worse, and because the Hollow was watching with the sense of something very patient and very old that had been waiting a long time for this specific sequence of events and was now watching them unfold with the particular satisfaction of a thing whose plans had come to fruition.
I did not know this then. I only knew the cold, and the impossible sky, and the name I was turning over in my mind the way you turned over a coin to see both sides.
Aleksei.
The silver trees moved around us as we walked, and I noticed that they moved slightly toward me, leaning at the edge of my peripheral vision, and that he noticed me noticing, and said nothing.
*   *   *
He gave me a coat at the boundary of what he called the Hall’s grounds.
It was not offered. It was stated: “You’ll be cold. Take this. Here. This is useful. Take it.
I looked at the coat. I looked at him. He was in shirtsleeves now, apparently unbothered by the cold that had been affecting me since we arrived. The Hollow cold — the deep, considered cold of this place — was not hurting him. It occurred to me that it probably never had.
“You’re not cold,” I said.
“No.”
“Vorath don’t get cold.”
“Not in the way mortals do.”
I took the coat. It was heavy — heavier than it looked, the fabric something I didn’t recognize, dark and dense and clearly old. When I put it on, the warmth was immediate and substantial — the coat itself and not, I told myself firmly, anything to do with residual warmth from the person who’d been wearing it.
It smelled like woodsmoke and something else. Something under the woodsmoke that I had no category for — older, deeper, the smell of a place rather than a person. The smell of the Hollow distilled into something almost bearable by proximity to whatever Aleksei was.
I pulled it closer. For warmth. Obviously.
“The Hall,” he said, which seemed to mean: follow me, or: we’re going now, or simply: here is the next relevant noun in this sequence of events. He was already walking. I followed.
The Hall was larger inside than seemed geometrically possible, which I was going to file under things that the Hollow did that didn’t require explanation so much as acceptance. He led me through corridors of dark stone that held the Hollow’s ambient light in their surfaces — it came from inside the stone, from deep down, slow and patient — and past rooms with closed doors that I catalogued automatically: the shape of the spaces behind them, whether the hinges were on my side, what I could hear or smell from outside. Old habits.
He stopped at a door and opened it. The room inside was — prepared. The fire produced light that was more orange than the Hollow’s ambient blue, and it was warmer, and the combination of the fire and the coat meant I was almost comfortable, which felt like a significant achievement.
Everything in the room was exactly correct. This was the problem with it. The desk had paper and ink already laid out. The fire was at the size that would burn through the night without requiring attention. It was the room of someone who had researched what a person needed and provided it precisely.
Not the room of someone who knew.
The distinction was small and probably didn’t matter and I noticed it anyway because I noticed everything.
“The Hall’s presences will leave you alone tonight,” he said from the doorway. “There’s food in the cabinet by the window. If you need something, you can—” He stopped.
“Can what?” I asked.
He seemed to recalibrate. “You’ll know where I am,” he said. “Through the binding. If something requires my attention, I’ll know.”
Which meant he would feel it if I was in trouble, through the same mechanism that let him feel my general location and wellbeing. I thought about having a Vorath lord constantly aware of my wellbeing, and I thought about all the ways that could be uncomfortable, and I thought about the cold of the Hollow and the fact that I was currently warm in his coat and how none of these things were things I should be thinking about in any connected way.
“Right,” I said. “The binding. Constant.”
“Constant,” he confirmed.
He left. The door closed behind him with the specific quiet of something very heavy moving with more care than strictly necessary.
I stood in the center of the room and looked at the window and the fire and the exactly-right blankets and the view of silver trees going on forever, and I breathed the Hollow air, which tasted like winter and something older, and I was twelve minutes from home by a route that no longer existed, and I was going to be here for a year.
I sat down on the bed. The mattress was, obviously, perfect.
“Sixty silver marks,” I said to the room, to no one, to the fire which crackled with what I chose to interpret as sympathy.
I was still wearing the coat.
I did not take it off.
I was choosing to wear the coat.
The shape of it, now that I had the pieces: I was bound by ancient law to a territory keeper older than anything I had a framework for. The realm he kept was dying. I was carrying something the realm recognized. And the politics of what I carried were going to bring every ancient power in the Hollow to our door.
That was the situation. The other situation — the one I was not examining right now — was that the territory keeper had silver eyes and a silence I wanted to fill and hands that were cold in a way I was starting to find interesting.
Both situations were going to cost me something. I suspected it might be the same thing.
 That was important — not because the coat mattered in itself, but because every choice I made in this place was a choice I was keeping track of. Which ones were mine. Which ones were his. Which ones belonged to the law and which ones I was making freely inside the law’s constraints.
The coat was mine. I was keeping it because I wanted to, not because he had given it to me.
And in the morning I would discover that the Hollow did not observe choices without responding to them — and that its response would rewrite the terms of my stay.
The binding pulsed once — brief, present, the weight of something noting a fact and filing it for later use.
He knew. Wherever he was in this realm, he knew I was still wearing it.
And somewhere in the territory, something else knew too. The Hall’s warmth shifted slightly — not colder, not warmer, simply different, the way a room felt different when someone had just walked in and decided to stay.
I had made a choice about the coat. Every watching thing in this realm had seen me make it.
I did not take it off.


Chapter Four: The First Morning in a Realm That Should Not Exist
Kira
I woke up already cataloguing exits.
This was not unusual. I had woken up cataloguing exits since I was sixteen, the age at which sleeping in places other than where I was supposed to be had become a regular feature of my life. It was the kind of habit that started as a necessity and eventually just became the way your brain arranged itself during the transition from sleep to waking — a fast, involuntary survey of the environment, a check of available options, a brief assessment of whether the options had changed since you closed your eyes.
The options had not changed. The window was still too far down. The door was still the only real exit. I had memorized the number of steps between the bed and the door last night before I slept — twelve — not enough distance to matter against something that moved the way he moved, but information was information and I collected it regardless of immediate utility.
I lay still for a moment, looking at the ceiling.
The ceiling was stone, high and vaulted. The room was large enough to be called a hall itself. The bed was enormous and low-framed. Everything in the room was beautiful in a way I found suspicious — the beauty of things that had been made for someone specific.
That column was very full.
I sat up. I was still wearing the coat.
I looked at the coat. I needed to make a decision about the coat. It was his coat, clearly, despite the fact that he had handed it over with the tone of a person giving away something that no longer required a decision — here, this is yours now, the matter is settled — and it was extremely warm and it smelled like the Hollow and like woodsmoke and like something I was choosing not to examine, and I had slept in it, which I was also choosing not to examine.
I took it off. I folded it over the back of the chair at the desk with what I felt was an appropriate amount of care for something that was not mine and that I was going to return.
Then I stood up and began to properly look at the room.
*   *   *
The first thing I did in a new place was inventory. Not sentimental. Practical.
The room was five paces by eight. The bed was against the wall, the desk near the window, the wardrobe against the far wall. Everything was made from the same blue-grey stone that produced its own ambient light. The room was beautiful and practical and clearly built for someone who was expected to stay.
The window.
I had noticed the window last night, in the way that you noticed something whose full implications you were not ready to process at the moment — you filed it for later, for the part of the evening after the immediate situation was dealt with. Now I went to it.
The silver forest began perhaps thirty feet from the Hall’s outer wall, which meant from the window I was looking directly into it — directly at the silver-barked trees with their interior luminescence and their extraordinary height and the way they moved without wind, a slow, considered motion that was not quite like anything I had a word for. Not swaying. Not reaching. Something between the two. Something purposeful.
The sky above the forest was the same deep, impossible cycling dark I’d seen on arrival, moving through blues and greens and the deep purple that pressed against the eye like held breath. I had no idea what time it was. There was no sun. I was good with time. I needed to stay good with time.
The trees closest to the Hall were bright — the silver bark luminescent, beautiful in the way of things alive in ways I had no framework for. But further out, where the trunks were wider and older, the light was thinner. Not dimmer exactly. Still lit. Still functioning. But something underneath was not being replenished as fast as it was being used.
I did not know enough about the Hollow to know what that meant, which was becoming a theme. I filed it under things to ask about when asking becomes an option.
Below the window: a straight drop of what I calculated to be four floors. Stone wall, no purchase, nothing to break the fall until the ground, which was also stone.
I had known this last night. It was still annoying to confirm.
I turned from the window and looked at the room, at all of it, the whole of it together, and for the first time I let myself notice what I had been skirting around since I woke up.
The room was perfect.
Not perfect the way a very good room was perfect — comfortable, well-appointed, tasteful. Perfect in a different sense, a more unsettling one. Perfect in the way of a room that had been assembled with careful research and considerable precision by someone who had not furnished a room for a mortal in a very long time and had gone about the project the way you went about something you were determined to do correctly.
The blankets were the exact weight I would have chosen, if asked. I had not been asked. Someone had anticipated that I might want to write in quantity. Work through problems on paper. Think by writing.
They were correct. I did.
But no one knew that. No one who had known me for the span of one alley conversation and a tour of a corridor could have known that, because it was not the kind of thing that announced itself, and I was specifically not the kind of person who announced things that could be used to read her.
The room knew anyway. Or whoever had prepared it did.
I went to the cabinet by the window and opened it. Inside: food. Enough for several days.
Someone had sourced mortal food from Zemya. For me. Before I had agreed to stay.
I looked at this for a moment. I ate the bread and the cheese, standing by the window, watching the silver forest.
But further out, in the ring of oldest trees, the light was thinner than yesterday. Not dimmer — thinner, like gas lamps on Saltfish Market running low on fuel. Still lit. Something underneath not being replenished as fast as it was being used.
*   *   *
The door had no lock on the inside.
I found this after breakfast, when I conducted the closer inspection of the room that thoroughness required. No lock, no bolt, no latch on my side — the door opened from outside with a handle I could not reach from within, and from inside with a handle I could reach, meaning I could leave but not lock myself in — either an oversight or a statement, and I didn’t think he was a person who made oversights.
I tested the door. It opened. The corridor beyond was empty, lit by the same slow luminescence in the stone, running in both directions into a distance that should not have been possible given the building’s external dimensions. No guards. No barrier. No visible indication that I was meant to stay in the room rather than leave it.
He had told me last night that I was not a prisoner in the physical sense. I understood that he meant this very literally — there was no mechanism of physical confinement. There was only the binding, the constant awareness that cut both directions, and the knowledge that Nochval’s boundaries were not something I could cross without his knowledge.
The binding was a more elegant constraint than a lock. A lock could be picked. A lock required only skill and time and the right piece of wire. The knowing was quiet, like the awareness you had of your own heartbeat: always present, only noticed when you paid attention to it.
I paid attention to it, briefly. North. Far. Outdoors.
I stepped out of my room and went in the opposite direction.
*   *   *
The Hall was larger than it had looked from outside. Corridors intersected at intervals that felt deliberate, as if the building had opinions about where people should go. And on the second floor, between two corridors that should have been identical, I passed a room with no door that contained something I could not explain: an hourglass of extraordinary size in which nothing was moving.
I looked at the hourglass for a moment. It held the Hollow’s light in its glass in the way everything here held light, pooled at the bottom with the sand and not moving.
I kept going.
There were more corridors. One had a quality of silence behind it that was different from the silence of an empty room. One had a door that was darker than the others, darker than the stone around it, with a heaviness to it that was not entirely physical, and I stood in front of it for a moment feeling the specific quality of that heaviness and then I moved on, because some doors were not mine yet.
Yet. I noticed that word in my own thinking and I set it aside to be annoyed about later.
I found the library by smell — the correct way to find a library. The smell of old paper and binding leather and the specific cool dryness of a room that had been maintained at the right conditions for a very long time — it came from under a door at the end of a corridor that bent slightly, as if the Hall had changed its mind mid-construction, and I followed it without hesitation because if there was a library I was going to know about it.
The library was enormous. I opened the door and stood in the entrance for a moment, adjusting.
Floor-to-ceiling shelves on every available wall, and free-standing shelves in rows down the center of the room, all of them full — not the careful, curated fullness of a collection maintained for display, but the dense, practical fullness of a room that was used. Some shelves were neat. Others had books stacked horizontally on top of vertical ones where the shelf had run out of space, and papers tucked into gaps, and in one place a small object being used as a bookmark that I crossed the room to look at and found was a smooth river stone, gray and unremarkable — the kind of bookmark that happened when you were in the middle of reading something and needed to stop and grabbed the nearest thing.
The nearest thing, in this library, might have been from any century.
There were tables — two of them, long and dark, with chairs that had the worn quality of furniture that had been sat in regularly over a very long time. There was a fireplace here too, smaller than the one in my room, cold at the moment. One of them I stood in front of for a long moment, the script catching at something in the back of my mind the way a word in a half-remembered conversation catches — familiar without being known, the impression of understanding without its substance.
I pulled the book from the shelf.
It was old. The cover was dark and smooth and gave slightly under my fingers in the way of leather that had been conditioned for centuries by dry hands. The script on the cover matched the spine — flowing, angular, with a rhythm to it that suggested it was meant to be read aloud.
I opened it.
The interior pages were the same script, small and precise, in an ink that had not faded. I held it at the angle where the Hollow’s ambient light caught it best, and I looked at the first page, and I did not understand it, and I did.
Both things were true simultaneously — not a state I was accustomed to. I could not read the words. The individual marks meant nothing to me. Almost. Almost. Like hearing your name said just quietly enough that you couldn’t be certain.
I stood in the library of an ancient being in a realm that should not exist, holding a book in a language no one living should have been able to recognize, and I almost recognized it.
I put it back on the shelf.
I put it back where it had been — I had noted the position when I took it, the exact angle it sat at, the way it pressed against the books on either side. I slid it into place until it sat exactly as it had. Then I stepped back and looked at the shelf and breathed slowly and thought about the fact that the binding told me Aleksei was still outdoors and had been for several hours and I had no idea what an ancient Vorath lord did outdoors alone for several hours but I was not going to go and look.
I was going to go back to my room. I had seen enough of the Hall for one morning. I was going to go back to my room and I was going to sit at the desk with the practical paper and I was going to make an organized list of everything I did not understand, because that was how I dealt with things I did not understand, and then I was going to start at the top of the list and work through it systematically until I understood.
This was the plan. It was a good plan. I left the library and retraced my route back through the Hall.
*   *   *
The windowsill in my room had something on it.
I noticed it when I came back through the door — I had left the door slightly ajar when I went out, a habit, and the Hollow’s ambient light from the corridor fell at an angle across the room that it hadn’t had before, catching the windowsill in a way that made the object on it visible from the entrance. Small. Low. Dark.
I had not seen it last night. I had inventoried this room carefully last night, twice — once when I arrived, once before I slept — and this object had not been here.
Which meant either the room had placed it there while I slept — a thing the Hollow was probably capable of, or someone had.
I crossed to the window.
It was small enough to fit in my closed hand. The carving was a fox. Sitting, ears forward, tail curled around its feet with the specific composed alertness of a fox that had decided to be still and was being absolutely, deliberately still. The carving was good — genuinely good, the kind of good that came from long practice and caring about the outcome. The lines were not ornate; they were precise. Every edge that needed to be sharp was sharp. Every surface that needed to be smooth had been worked until it was.
It had been handled. For a very long time, by hands that returned to it regularly: the high points of the carving were worn in the specific way of things touched often, the ears and the bridge of the nose and the raised line of the tail slightly smoother than the recessed surfaces. The wood had the feel of something that had absorbed decades of warmth from human — or not human — contact, and had changed in response to it, and was still changing.
I turned it over. No marks on the base. No inscription. Nothing to explain it.
I turned it back right side up. The fox looked at nothing with its composed, deliberate expression. I looked at the fox.
I thought about putting it in my pocket. I thought about the weight of it, which was heavier than such a small thing had a right to be — the density of the wood, the accumulated weight of however long it had been in existence, the specific kind of heavy that things acquired when they had been important for a long time. I thought about the fact that it had been placed here intentionally, which meant it was here for a reason, and taking it from where it had been placed would change something about that reason in ways I couldn’t fully anticipate.
I put it back.
Exactly where it had been — I had noted the position when I picked it up, the slight angle of it toward the window, the way the tip of the tail nearly touched the window frame. I set it back with the care I’d used with the book in the library, one position restored to its original configuration.
I went to the desk. I sat down. I pulled the top sheet of paper toward me and I wrote at the top:
Things I Do Not Currently Understand (In Order of Urgency).
I wrote for forty minutes. The list was longer than I would have preferred.
*   *   *
He appeared in the doorway sometime around what I was choosing to designate as mid-morning, though the Hollow’s sky gave me nothing to confirm this and I was operating on appetite and the biological sense of time that the body maintained regardless of available light.
He appeared the way he did everything: without announcement, without noise, simply present where he had not been a moment before. I was at the desk with my list. I heard nothing. I looked up because the binding shifted — not dramatically, just the specific recalibration of nearness, the awareness settling from far-north-outdoors to here — and he was standing in the open doorway with the door behind him and his hands at his sides and that particular quality of stillness that I was beginning to understand was simply his default state, the way some people were in constant motion and he was in constant stillness.
He was looking at the windowsill.
Not at me. At the windowsill, at the fox, at the exact position it sat in. The look lasted only a moment — the specific, involuntary quality of a glance that lands before you’ve decided to direct it — and then his eyes moved to me.
I met them. I said nothing.
He said nothing.
Something passed between us in that silence — not words, not information exactly, more like the acknowledgment that something had occurred and had been observed by both parties and did not require discussion. He had looked at the fox. I had seen him look at the fox. He knew I had seen him look. I had put the fox back exactly where it was, which was also a piece of information, and he had received it, and we were both going to proceed as if none of this had happened because that was, apparently, the kind of arrangement we were going to have.
I was unexpectedly fine with this.
“How do you determine time here,” I said. “There’s no sun.”
He came into the room — not all the way, just across the threshold, so he was in it without occupying it, which I was beginning to think was characteristic of how he inhabited spaces. Near walls. Near exits. Never in the center of a room. “The Hollow has a cycle,” he said. “The sky deepens to black at the nadir and lightens toward blue at the peak. The nadir is midnight. The peak is noon.” He paused. “You learn to read it.”
“How long does it take to learn to read it?”
“A few weeks, for most.”
“And for mortals.”
“I don’t have a comparison point,” he said. “You’re the first mortal to stay here in three centuries.”
I absorbed this. Three centuries was a long time by any measure I had available to me. “Why did the last one stay?” I asked.
“They were caught in the Hunt,” he said, which answered nothing and everything simultaneously.
“And after them,” I said. “Three hundred years of Hunts and none of them stayed.”
“None of them stayed.” Something in his delivery — not emphasis, but the absence of it, the flat neutrality that might have been covering something or might have been nothing at all. I had not yet developed the tools to tell the difference.
“But they were all caught,” I said.
“The Hunt catches mortals occasionally,” he said. “Yes.”
“And they all chose the memory option.”
A pause. Shorter than the ones last night, but present. “They were all given the memory option,” he said.
I looked at him. He looked at me.
“You offered me three options,” I said.
“I did.”
“And the last mortal caught — three hundred years ago — they were offered how many.”
He held my gaze without difficulty. The silver eyes were very still, very present, and they gave me nothing. “One,” he said.
I sat with that for a moment. One option. Three hundred years of one option. Last night, without explanation, three. I thought about adding this to my list and decided it was already on my list under a different heading, one I had written in the third line:
Why was I given options.
“Right,” I said. I looked back at my paper. “How do I access the rest of the Hall? Are there rooms that are locked?”
“Most of the Hall is accessible to you,” he said. “There are two rooms that are not. You will know them when you reach them.”
“How will I know?”
“They’ll feel closed,” he said. “The Hall communicates in impressions.”
“The Hall,” I said, “communicates.”
“It is old and it is alive and it has opinions,” he said, in the tone of someone who had been working around this fact for a long time. “You will adjust.”
I thought about the library, about the book I’d almost-read, about the language that had pulled at the back of my mind. I thought about writing this on my list and decided it was already there, fourth line:
The book in the language I should not recognize.
I did not mention the library. Not yet. I was still turning it over, still looking at both sides of it, and I had learned a long time ago not to offer information before you understood what it meant.
“The coat,” I said.
His eyes moved to the chair where I had folded it.
“It’s yours,” he said.
He said it the same way he’d said it last night — flatly, conclusively, the matter settled before I’d finished asking the question. I looked at the coat. I looked at him. He was already looking back at me with the specific patient quality of someone who had made a statement and did not intend to revisit it.
“I was going to return it,” I said.
“It’s yours,” he said again. Not louder. Not warmer. Just: again, in the same tone, with the same finality, like a second reading of a verdict.
I looked at the coat. At the way I had folded it — carefully, the kind of careful you used with something that belonged to someone else. And then at his face, which was showing me nothing except the silver eyes that caught the Hollow’s light and gave it back wrong.
“Thank you,” I said.
Something happened in his expression — small, gone before I finished the word. He turned toward the door.
“There will be food brought to the Hall’s main room in the evenings,” he said, from the doorway. “The Hall itself will show you where, when the time comes. If you require anything specific, you can — the binding will convey it.” He paused, as if reconsidering the sentence. “You can simply need something. I’ll know.”
The intimacy of this — the idea of simply needing something and having him know — sat in the center of my chest with a weight I was going to deal with later.
“And the coat,” I said, because I could not entirely help myself. “You’re certain.”
He was already half-turned to go. He stopped. He did not look back at me when he said it — he said it to the corridor, or to the air, or to some private audience of his own: “Wear it. You’ll be cold otherwise.”
And then he was gone, and the door was open, and I was alone with my list and the fox on the windowsill and the coat on the chair and the whole of the impossible Hollow outside my window, doing its slow, purposeful, watchful almost-swaying.
I picked up the pen.
I added to the list:
The fox. What it is. Why it is here. Why he looked at it first.
And below that, after a moment, in smaller writing, in the half-margins where I kept things I was not sure deserved their own line:
Why it matters that I put it back.
I looked at the fox. The fox looked at the middle distance with its composed, deliberately still expression.
I reached over and picked up the coat. I put it on. For warmth. Obviously.
Outside the window, the silver trees leaned, very slightly, toward the glass.
Not random. Not wind. Not the natural sway of things that grew.
They were looking at me.
The Hollow itself was looking at me. And I did not yet understand why a realm should care about one mortal woman and a coat she had been given, but the trees were leaning and the Hall was warm and somewhere in the territory the binding was steady and present and aware.
I had the gut-level certainty that I had just done something that mattered more than I knew. And something in the Hall’s deep structure — below the presences, below the warmth — had shifted in response. Not toward me. Toward what I was becoming. 


Chapter Five: The Problem with Keeping Things
Aleksei
I had not slept.
This was not unusual. A thousand years of practice had reduced its necessity to something manageable, something I could schedule around the work of keeping two realms in functional proximity. On a normal night after a Hunt I would spend the early hours in the deep territory attending to the membrane’s condition, then sleep briefly, then resume.
Last night was not a normal night after a Hunt.
I had gone to the far edge of Nochval, where the silver trees thinned and the ground sloped down toward the older part of the territory, the part that had been here before I built the Hall, before I had a reason to build anything. Less filtered through my presence. I went there when I needed to think without the Hall’s attention, which cared about things before I was ready.
I stood in the old territory for several hours and thought about the binding.
The binding had activated. In forty-seven Hunts across a thousand years, the binding had never activated. The law had acknowledged each catch in the way it acknowledged all legal events: the notation, the door latched, and nothing else. Nothing that persisted. Nothing that stayed in my Hunt-sense after the fact.
The binding was still in my Hunt-sense.
Not as an intrusion — it was not uncomfortable, which was itself notable. A direction. A distance. The warm, specific, wholly alive signature of a mortal woman who was currently, the binding informed me without being asked, awake and stationary and not in distress. She was somewhere in the Hall. Her state was what I was learning to categorize as her baseline: alert, processing, keeping her own counsel.
She had been awake since before I returned from the old territory. She had been moving through the Hall. I knew this because the Hall knew it and the Hall and I were not separate entities in any meaningful sense — its awareness was an extension of mine. She had found the library.
I had felt her find the library, which is to say I had felt the Hall’s specific quality of interested attention shift when she went through the door, a shift it produced when something it cared about happened. The Hall cared about its library the way it cared about nothing else in the building — it had opinions about the library with a consistency that I had stopped arguing with centuries ago. It had opinions about who could find things in the library, about how books were handled, about the weight of attention brought to the room.
It had, this morning, the feel of something quietly pleased.
I stood in the old territory and I thought about what it meant that my Hall was quietly pleased about a mortal woman in its library, and I thought about the binding, and I thought about the book she had taken from the shelf.
I had felt her take it. She had taken the book in the first language. The oldest book in the library. The one I had not taken down myself in two hundred years because there was nothing in it I did not already know, and the weight of old things being read without understanding was not a weight I went looking for.
She had opened it. She had held it in the Hollow’s light and looked at it.
And she had almost read it.
I stood very still in the old part of Nochval for a long time after that.
*   *   *
Vasilisa arrived at mid-morning.
She did not send word ahead. She never sent word ahead. She appeared at the edge of the Hall’s grounds in the way she appeared everywhere: without transition, fully formed, as if she had simply decided to be there and the world had accommodated the decision.
She was wearing something that had too many layers to count and was the color of the Hollow’s sky at the nadir — not black, too complex to be black. Her hair was loose. She stood at the edge of the grounds with her hands in her layers and looked at the Hall and then at me.
“You look terrible,” she said, with the warmth of someone offering a compliment.
“Vasilisa,” I said.
“Don’t use that tone. I came as soon as I heard.” She was already walking toward the Hall. “How long has she been here? Is she all right? What’s she like? Radovan said she had a knife. I find that very encouraging. Show me.”
“No.”
“Aleksei.”
“She has been here one night,” I said. “She is in the Hall. She is not available for your inspection.”
“I’m not going to inspect her,” Vasilisa said, in the tone of someone who intended exactly to inspect her. “I’m going to introduce myself. Appropriately. As a concerned — what am I? A colleague? A neighbor? An extremely interested observer of the situation?” She considered. “A friend of the household. I’m a friend of the household.”
“You’ve never visited this household in three centuries,” I said.
“I’ve been busy.” She smiled at me with the specific brightness she deployed when she had already decided something and was letting me catch up. “Also there was nothing worth visiting for. There is now. Show me.”
“No,” I said.
She looked at me for a moment with both of her mismatched eyes and the expression of a woman who had known me for so long that she could read the negative spaces in my sentences — the things I was not saying with the same fluency she read what I was. Something shifted in her face. Not the bright social performance she’d been running since she arrived. Something underneath it, quieter and more genuine.
“The binding,” she said. Not a question.
“Yes.”
“How does it feel?”
I looked at the Hall. At the window of the room I’d given her — the third floor, south face, the one I’d had prepared because it had the best view of the forest and the most natural light that the Hollow produced. The window was empty. She was not at it. The binding told me she was at her desk.
“Present,” I said.
Vasilisa was quiet for a moment. This was notable — Vasilisa’s silences were strategic and deliberate and she did not produce them accidentally. This one had the tone of her choosing her next words with unusual care. The next words were going to be words she had already composed and was now deciding whether to deliver.
She delivered them.
“You know,” she said, “in a thousand years, the binding has never activated.” She paused. “Not once. Not for any of the caught.”
“I am aware,” I said.
“Not for any of the mortals you returned. Not for the three you gave options to in the past four hundred years.” Another pause. “Not for the one you kept, three centuries ago.” She was looking at the Hall now, not at me, which gave me the particular freedom of being observed without being watched. “You kept that one because the law specifically required it — a child, if I remember, who had wandered out during the Hunt and had no home to return to. The binding did not activate for them either.”
“I am aware,” I said again.
“So.” She turned back to me. The bright expression was completely gone. What was underneath it was something I had seen very rarely across the centuries — the real Vasilisa, the one who was not performing warmth because she was experiencing it. Careful. Concerned. Something else, buried below those, that looked almost like relief. “The binding activated,” she said. “For a mortal woman with a knife who was outside because she was owed money.”
“Sixty silver marks,” I said.
A pause. Then Vasilisa made a sound that was somewhere between a laugh and something more complicated. She turned back toward the Hall and began walking. She had been the Vorath of Memory for as long as I had been anything, and memory required knowing which things needed to be given time to settle before you looked at them again.
“I’m going to look at the grounds,” she said over her shoulder. “The south side. I haven’t seen the south silver trees since you replanted them four centuries ago.”
“The south side,” I said, “is adjacent to the Hall’s south-face windows.”
