“What a coincidence,” Vasilisa said.
I let her go. There were battles I chose and battles I did not, and this one had been over before it started.
*   *   *
She spent twenty minutes at the south side of the grounds. I knew she was there the way I knew everything that happened within Nochval’s boundary: not by watching, but by the Hall’s ambient awareness, the constant low current of the territory’s attention that ran through me as naturally as my own pulse.
I was inside when the Hall told me that she had found a window to examine.
It was the south-face window. The third floor. The one with the best view.
I was in my study, three floors down, maintaining the membrane at the city’s eastern border. This was real work. The membrane required regular attention; I was correcting this.
The membrane’s eastern section produced a note I had not felt in decades. Not a breach. A listening quality, as if something on the other side had pressed an ear to the wall.
I felt Kira move to her window.
Not through the window. She did not look out. The binding told me she was near it — the directional sense was specific enough to locate her within a room at close range, and she was at the window, not far from the glass, and the Hall told me that Vasilisa was below in the grounds with her face turned up and her mismatched eyes very bright.
I did not go to the south grounds. I returned to the membrane.
Vasilisa came inside twelve minutes later. I heard her in the corridor. She was not bothering to move quietly. She wanted to be heard approaching. A courtesy she occasionally extended when she had something to say that she thought I should be seated for.
She appeared in the doorway of my study. Her expression had the specific quality it had when she was containing something — a category of expression I had learned to identify across centuries because it appeared reliably in the moments before she said something that reorganized my understanding of a situation.
“She’s making a list,” Vasilisa said. “I could see her writing from the grounds. She has a great deal to write down, apparently.” A pause. “She’s also wearing your coat.”
I said nothing.
Vasilisa looked at me with both her mismatched eyes. “You gave her the coat,” she said.
“She was cold.”
“Of course she was.” She came into the study and sat down in the chair across from my desk with the ease of someone who had been sitting in that chair for centuries, which she had. “Tell me about her.”
“I have told you what I know.”
“You’ve told me facts,” Vasilisa said. “Mortal woman, caught in the Hunt, binding activated. That’s the summary. I want the actual account. The details.” Her eyes were attentive in a way that had nothing to do with curiosity and everything to do with a very old intelligence gathering the information it needed to work. “What was she like, in the alley.”
I set down what I was doing. Not because I was done with it — I was not done with it — but because Vasilisa asking for details in that particular tone was something I had learned required full attention, and dividing attention between her and anything else was a strategy that consistently produced worse outcomes than simply giving her what she was asking for.
“She didn’t scream,” I said.
Vasilisa waited.
“She drew a knife and held it. She knew it wasn’t going to be useful. She held it anyway.” I thought about the alley — about the set of her, the specific steadiness of it. “She had already run the options before I finished crossing the space. By the time I reached her she had assessed the exits and found them insufficient and arrived at the conclusion that what happened next was going to happen next, and she was managing that rather than the fear.”
“Managing,” Vasilisa said.
“Deciding what she could control,” I said. “She lowered the knife herself. Deliberately. As a choice rather than a capitulation.” I paused. “She looked at me directly. She held eye contact without difficulty. Most mortals cannot do this with a Vorath at full presence. She did it without apparent effort.”
Vasilisa had a particular silence she used for the moments when she was satisfied with a thing she had already suspected. This was that silence.
“She negotiated,” I said, because Vasilisa was not going to stop looking at me with that expression until I continued. “She found two loopholes in the law within fifteen minutes of hearing it. Both closed. She moved on immediately and started gathering information instead.” A pause. “She asked good questions.”
“What kind of good.”
“The kind that locate the edges of things,” I said. “She was not asking what she could ask. She was finding out where the questions I wouldn’t answer were.”
Vasilisa looked at me for a long moment. Then: “She sounds delightful.”
“She’s a complication,” I said.
“Those aren’t mutually exclusive.” She stood. She was done. She had what she’d come for. I had told her more than I’d intended to — a thing she reliably accomplished when she wanted to and I reliably failed to prevent. She moved toward the door, then stopped at the threshold and said, without turning back: “You checked on her this morning. I felt you go past the room.”
“The binding requires proximity,” I said. “For the first period.”
“Does it.”
The way she said this indicated that she was not unaware that this was not a thing the binding required. I did not respond to it.
“You gave her three options,” Vasilisa said, still in the doorway, still not looking at me. “I’ve been thinking about that since Radovan told me. Three. You haven’t given anyone three options since—” She stopped. A particular kind of stop — not because she didn’t know the end of the sentence, but because she knew it well and was choosing to leave it where it was. “Why three.”
I considered the question. It was not a question I had asked myself yet — I had been occupied with the binding, with the anomaly of it, with the book in the old language and what it might mean. I had not asked myself why I had offered three options when the functional options were one.
“I don’t know,” I said. It was the most honest thing I’d said all morning, and also the most dangerous, which was likely why I said it to Vasilisa rather than to anyone else.
She received it with the quiet of someone handling something fragile. “All right,” she said. Just that. She was nearly out the door.
“Leave her alone today,” I said.
“Of course.” She was gone — I heard her in the corridor, moving toward the south face again. Her footsteps had a particular quality when she was pleased about something, slightly lighter than her baseline. She was pleased about something.
I returned to the membrane.
The binding sat in my awareness with the ease of something that had always been there. She was at her desk. She was still writing. She was a mortal in my Hall for whom I had a legal obligation, and the binding told me she was well, and that was sufficient.
I returned to the membrane.
The membrane did not require this much attention. I was aware of this.
*   *   *
Vasilisa left in the early afternoon.
She had not spoken to Kira directly — I knew this because the Hall’s awareness of Kira had not changed quality in the way it changed when Kira interacted with anyone or anything, the slight heightening that indicated her attention had been engaged. Vasilisa had, however, spent three separate periods at the south grounds looking at the windows with her mismatched eyes and the expression of someone extremely satisfied with what they could see from a careful distance.
She found me in the Hall’s main corridor on her way out.
“I’ll come back,” she said. This was a statement of fact rather than a request. She would come back regardless of what I said about it, and we both knew this, so I said nothing about it.
“Vasilisa,” I said instead.
She looked at me with both mismatched eyes.
“The binding,” I said. “In your memory — in everything you carry. Has it been documented. Any account of it activating for a mortal.”
She held my gaze for a moment. The quality of the look was the one she used when she had an answer and was deciding how much of it to give. “There are accounts,” she said carefully, “of the binding activating in the early period. Before the separation of the realms.” She paused. “Not since.”
“What were the circumstances?”
“Complex,” she said. She was not going to tell me today. She turned toward the Hall’s entrance. “I’ll look into it.” A pause at the threshold. “Aleksei.”
“What.”
“Show her the territory. When she’s ready. The Hollow responds to her.” A beat. “You felt it last night. I know you felt it.”
I had felt it. I had felt it from the old territory while I was trying to think about the binding without the Hall’s attention complicating it — the Hollow’s ambient awareness shifting in a way I had not felt in a very long time. Not threatening. The opposite of threatening. Something the Hollow did when it recognized something it had been waiting for.
I had felt it. I had not known what to do with it. I still did not know what to do with it.
“Go home, Vasilisa,” I said.
She went. At the edge of the grounds, where the silver trees began, she paused and tilted her face toward a particular window. Then she was gone, her Vorath signature withdrawing past Nochval’s boundary like a tide going out.
I stood in the Hall’s entrance for a moment.
The binding told me Kira was still at her desk. Still writing. She had been writing, on and off, for most of the day. The Hall was pleased about this in the quiet way it was pleased about things — a particular quality to its attention, satisfied and warm.
I went back to the north study. I had not accessed the Hall’s visual awareness of the room today. Her room was her room and the Hall’s awareness of what happened inside it was an intimacy that did not belong to me — the binding gave me what the law required, and I did not take from it what the law had not offered.
I had not watched her in the library. I had known she was there. I had felt the shift when she took the old-language book from the shelf and then — and this was the thing I had been arranging my thoughts around since it happened — the particular stillness that had moved through the Hall’s awareness, the sense of something momentarily suspended, when she opened it.
She had almost read it.
I sat down at my desk and looked at the north study’s window, which showed me nothing but the old silver trees at the far edge of the grounds, and I thought about the word almost with more attention than it strictly deserved.
*   *   *
She found the fox.
I had left it in the room because I had not been certain whether to leave it in the room. This was an unusual state of uncertainty for me — I was not, as a general rule, uncertain about the small practical decisions of territory management, which included the furnishing of rooms that had not been used in three centuries. I had furnished the room from the Hall’s stores with the efficiency of someone who had been maintaining a large building for a long time and knew what it contained.
And then I had stood in the doorway of the room with the fox in my hand and I had thought about the windowsill and I had put it there, because the room without it felt like a room with something missing — a thing I had not felt about the room in three centuries and did not examine now.
The Hall told me when she found it. I felt her take it from the sill. I felt the pause — the duration of attention she gave to it — and I felt when she set it down.
She put it back.
I was in the north study when this happened, and I had a piece of work in my hands, and I set the work down and I looked at the wall that pointed south and I thought about the fact that she had put it back.
Mortals took things. Objects in other people’s spaces were objects to be collected or ignored. The ones worth collecting got collected. I had watched this across a thousand years, across any number of mortal visitors to the territories, and it was so reliable as to constitute a law of its own.
She had held the fox long enough to read its history — I knew this because the Hall knew the duration of contact. She had understood it was important. And she had put it back exactly where it had been.
Exactly. The Hall’s awareness of spatial position was precise, and exact was the word — not approximately, not close enough. Exactly. The angle, the position of the tail relative to the window frame, the orientation of the ears. Exactly.
I sat in the north study for a while after that, not working.
Later, when I went to her room to confirm the evening arrangements — food, the Hall’s common room, the practical facts of habitation that I had told her about last night and needed to elaborate on — I came through the door and my eyes went to the windowsill before I directed them anywhere else.
She saw me look. I saw her see me look. We did not speak about it — a choice we made simultaneously without discussing it — the kind of agreement that did not require words and which I had not made with anyone in a very long time.
I told her about the food. About the Hall’s common room. I gave her the coat because she was going to be cold without it and she was going to argue about the coat if I let her and the coat was hers now, which I had decided last night for reasons I was not going to look at directly.
She said: “Thank you.”
There was something in the way she said it — not surprised, not effusive, just honest. The specific honesty of someone who did not say thank you automatically but meant it when they said it. I noted this and noted that I was noting it and left before either observation could develop into anything I was required to address.
The binding sat in my awareness with the ease of something settled.
That evening, after she had eaten and returned to her room and the Hall had gone into its night configuration, I sat in the library for the first time in several weeks. The old-language book was exactly where she had replaced it. I could see, in the slight disorder of the books on either side, that it had been removed and returned — the small disturbance of a careful person who had tried to leave no trace.
She had left a trace. The warm impression of hands on the cover, already fading. The slightly different angle at which the book now sat against its neighbor — close to exact, not perfect, the margin of error that even the most attentive person could not entirely eliminate.
I reached out and touched the cover.
Still warm.
I sat for a while and then I went to find Vasilisa, because I had more questions now than I had started the day with, and Vasilisa was the only being in existence who might have answers. This was, I was beginning to understand, going to be a regular feature of the coming months.
The binding was present in my awareness, steady as a compass north.
The Hall was pleased about everything. I found this enormously unhelpful and entirely accurate.
And then, as I stood to go find Vasilisa, the binding shifted.
Not her location. Not her state. Something else — a third awareness, brief and distant, touching the edge of Nochval’s territory like a finger pressed to glass. Checking. Measuring.
Another Vorath had just noticed something about the binding.
The awareness withdrew before I could identify the source. But the fact of it remained: someone else was paying attention to what should have been a routine Hunt catch and a routine year-long consequence.
The binding was not routine.
*   *   *
The communication from the eastern boundary arrived in the night’s second quarter.
Not Vasilisa. Not Radovan. The membrane carried it in the cold register — the one that belonged to a specific territory and a specific keeper and that I had not felt directed at Nochval in forty years.
Zora.
Not a message. A probe. The membrane equivalent of someone pressing a palm against a wall to feel whether the wall was hollow. She had felt the binding’s activation from her territory. She had felt the Claim. And she was, with the careful patience of someone who had been accumulating information for eight hundred years, beginning to establish what she was dealing with.
I reinforced the eastern membrane without responding. A response would confirm. Silence would merely delay.
But Zora did not need confirmation. Zora needed a crack, and the binding’s activation was the largest crack in Nochval’s boundary in a century.
I would need to tell the others. Not yet — but soon. The window for managing this quietly was narrowing.
And now someone else knew it.


Chapter Six: Rules of the House, Such As They Are
Kira
Three days into my stay in the Hollow, Aleksei summoned me to the Hall’s main room and explained the rules.
I say summoned. The binding, I had established in three days of paying close attention to it, communicated in states rather than signals. He was not calling me. He was simply there, and stationary, and the distinction between that and a summons was technically meaningful and practically negligible.
I came down.
He was standing near the main hall’s center table — not sitting, not leaning, positioned with the deliberate quality of someone who had chosen that specific distance from every wall and every door. The amber of his eyes in the Hall’s luminescence had a quality I could now name: attention. Not surveillance. Care.
I had been thinking about this — about the precise nature of the wrongness he carried, the way it was not threatening exactly but was not safe either, the way you could look at him for a long time and still feel like you hadn’t quite seen him. Three days of cataloguing and I was not significantly closer to a framework that worked.
“Sit,” he said.
“I don’t sit.”
I had not seen him sit in three days, which meant this was likely accurate. “Why not?”
“It doesn’t particularly serve a purpose.”
I thought about this. It was, objectively, a defensible position. “All right,” I said. I sat. He continued not sitting. We were now at different heights. He was looking slightly down at me, which I noted as a dynamic I was going to keep track of. “What are the rules?”
He told me the rules.
*   *   *
They were delivered in the same mode as the law on the first night: efficiently, completely, without editorializing. The formal briefing of a person who had done this before and intended to do it correctly. Some of the rules I had already established for myself through three days of exploration. Others I had not.
The territory of Nochval extended to a boundary that I would feel as a change in the air rather than see as a physical marker. Within this boundary I could go anywhere I chose, at any time, unaccompanied. Beyond it: no. I filed this under things I intended to acquire the ability to do.
The Hall itself: open to me with two exceptions, which I had already identified from the feeling he had described — the closed heaviness behind certain doors. One of them was on the south corridor, second floor. One was on the east face, at the Hall’s far end. I had found both and had not pressed against either. “What’s in them,” I said.
“Things that are not ready to be opened,” he said.
“Ready,” I said. “As in the things inside have opinions about it.”
“As in I do,” he said.
I received this. “Fair,” I said, because it was fair — he was not obligated to open every room in his Hall to a stranger he’d acquired through legal accident three nights ago, and I was not going to press it. I was going to note it, which I did, in the running list I kept in my head alongside the one I kept on paper: two closed rooms, his rules rather than the Hall’s, not yet.
The not yet was mine again, appearing in my thinking without being invited. I was going to stop noticing that and start noting it, which was different.
Communication with Zemya: a mirror in the Hall’s east corridor, second door from the main stair. It worked once in every three days. He had already ensured that Tomasz had been told to look at his bedroom mirror at a specific time. I had three days of messages stored up and was going to deploy them all at once.
“Tomasz,” I said. “My — he’s a fence. He’s known me for six years.” I stopped. “Why did you find him specifically. You didn’t know his name.”
A pause. “The binding,” he said — not an answer. The binding told him my general wellbeing, not my social network. I looked at him. He held my gaze with the flat silver attention he deployed when he had said what he intended to say and was not going to elaborate. I added this to the list.
The other Vorath: I was likely to encounter them. The Hunt had included six others, of whom one — Radovan, the large one who had been waiting at the territory’s edge when we arrived — was likely to visit Nochval. “Is he going to be a problem?” I asked.
“Radovan,” Aleksei said, and something in the delivery of the name had a quality I was beginning to learn, a very slight shift in register that indicated something approaching, but not reaching, warmth. “No. He won’t be a problem.” A pause. “He’ll be irritating in a different way.”
I found this remarkably interesting. “What way?”
“He’ll want to talk to you,” Aleksei said, with the air of someone describing an inconvenience they had resigned themselves to. “He’s curious about most things and has limited patience for the convention of not expressing it.”
“You mean he’s friendly.”
“I mean he has poor impulse control for someone who has been alive nine hundred years,” Aleksei said. “But yes. Broadly.”
I filed Radovan in the potential assets column and moved on.
*   *   *
The rules continued. They communicated in impressions, not words. If something in the Hall felt like it was trying to direct me somewhere, it was probably worth following. He said this with the flat certainty of someone stating a thing that had been reliably true for a very long time.
“How long have the presences been here,” I asked.
“Since the Hall was built.”
“When was the Hall built.”
“Eight hundred years ago,” he said. “Approximately.”
I thought about eight-hundred-year-old presences and decided I was probably fine with them, given that the three-day acquaintance so far had consisted of them occasionally making a door open before I reached it and once, memorably, redirecting a draught away from the candle I was reading by. They had opinions. So did I. We were going to get along.
“The Hollow outside the Hall,” I said. “The training for navigating it — you mentioned that.”
“We’ll begin in a week,” he said. “When you’ve had time to acclimate.”
“I’ve acclimated.”
The look he gave me was brief and entirely without expression and communicated, somehow, that he disagreed. “A week,” he said.
I was going to argue this and then I looked at the silver eyes and the specific quality of the not-expression in his face and I made the calculation that this was a battle I would come back to with better ammunition. “Fine,” I said. “A week. What do I do in the meantime.”
“Whatever you like,” he said, as if this were obvious.
“In a strange building in a realm I don’t understand.”
“You’ve found the library,” he said.
I looked at him. He looked at me. He had not been to the library while I was in it. I had checked, through the binding — when he was in the library, the directional sense would put him there, and he had not been there. He had not seen me there. And yet.
“How do you know that,” I said.
“The Hall,” he said simply.
“The Hall tells you where I’ve been.”
“The Hall has a general awareness of what occurs within it,” he said. “I am not monitoring your movements. The Hall’s awareness is passive — it tells me what it finds significant.”
“And me finding the library,” I said, “was significant.”
Something happened at the corner of his expression. Very small. The adjacency-of-a-smile that I’d clocked twice now, the thing that was not a smile and that occupied the precise space where a smile would go on the face of a person who allowed themselves smiles. “The Hall,” he said carefully, “has opinions about who finds the library and how they do it.”
“And I found it correctly.”
“You found it,” he said, “by smell, which is the correct way to find it.”
I stared at him. “Did you just—” I started.
“The rules,” he said, returning to his previous register with the care of a door closing. “Are substantially covered. Do you have questions.”
I had several questions. I organized them by priority.
The first was about the library. Not because the library was the most important room — because it was the room he had not mentioned.
*   *   *
“The binding awareness,” I said. “The general location and wellbeing. Does it work when you’re asleep.”
“I don’t sleep in the conventional sense,” he said. “But yes, the binding is constant regardless of my state.”
“So you knew I was in the library yesterday.”
“I knew you were in the library.”
“And in the corridor before that.”
“And in the corridor.”
“And this morning, when I was—” I stopped. I had been in the small courtyard off the east wing at dawn, the one I’d found through a door that the Hall had very helpfully left ajar, and I had been doing the exercises I did every morning, the routine I’d built over years of needing to be fast and functional at a moment’s notice, and I had been entirely alone and entirely certain of it and apparently I had been wrong about the certain of it part.
“The binding is constant,” he said, in the tone of someone who had already said this and was not going to apologize for it.
“Right,” I said. “And I have the corresponding awareness of you.”
“The directional sense, yes.”
“Which means,” I said, “that you have also been aware that I have the directional sense, and that I have been tracking your general location through the binding.”
He looked at me steadily. “Yes.”
“And you didn’t mention it.”
“You didn’t ask.”
I looked at him. He looked at me. The exchange had a quality to it that I was beginning to recognize — the specific frequency of two people who understood each other and found the understanding slightly inconvenient. “I’ve been paying attention to where you are,” I said. Not an accusation. A fact.
“I know,” he said. Not an accusation. A fact.
“That’s very mutual of us,” I said.
The corner of his expression did the thing again. Faster this time, and slightly more definite. I was getting better at catching it rather than that he was losing control of it. “It is,” he said, “the nature of the binding.”
“Sure,” I said. “The binding.”
I was aware that I was dangerously close to something I had decided not to examine, and I retreated to safer ground.
*   *   *
“The fox,” I said.
He had been about to continue the rules. He stopped. The quality of his stillness, which I was developing some fluency in, adjusted — not dramatically, nothing he did was dramatic, but the calibration of it changed in the way that a room’s atmosphere changed when the subject shifted to something that required more attention.
“In my room,” I said. “On the windowsill.”
“Yes,” he said.
“It wasn’t there when I arrived. The first night.”
“No.”
“You put it there,” I said. Also not an accusation. I was trying to establish facts.
“Yes.”
“Why.”
He was quiet for a moment. Not the strategic quiet of someone buying time — Aleksei didn’t buy time, he either spoke or he didn’t, and when he didn’t the silence was final and you moved on. This was a different silence. The silence of someone choosing between versions of an answer.
“It belonged to someone I knew,” he said.
Three days in I had already developed the vocabulary to identify this as a partial answer — true, clearly, but occupying only a fraction of the available space. There was a great deal of room around it. I could push. I was good at pushing; I had made a professional practice of locating the place where the pressure would produce results and applying it with precision.
I did not push.
Some information revealed itself when pushed. Other information closed. I was learning, which category he belonged to — a process that was going to take longer than three days and that I was — apparently willing to take.
“It’s well made,” I said instead.
Something in him settled — a small, subtle thing, the particular quality of stillness that was different from his default stillness, less managed and more simply present. “It is,” he said.
“Whoever made it cared about it.”
“Yes.”
I left it there. He left it there. The not-uncomfortable. Three days in and we had a silence about something that was already not-uncomfortable.
I was going to need to think about what that meant.
Later. But later was getting closer. The questions were accumulating faster than the answers, and the gap between the two was starting to feel structural.
*   *   *
The rest of the rules were practical. I asked each question I had organized in priority order and he answered each in kind, and somewhere in the exchange the formal briefing became something else — something closer to an actual conversation, with the specific quality of two people who were both aware that it had shifted and neither of whom was going to acknowledge that it had shifted, because acknowledging it would require deciding what it meant.
He told me about the Hollow’s weather — not weather in the mortal sense — not fronts and pressures and the predictable physics of a world that operated by consistent rules — but moods, the Hollow’s own atmospheric states, which arrived without warning and were readable if you knew what to read. He told me that the Hollow ran cold at depth and warm at the surface and that I would learn to feel the difference. He told me that the creatures of Nochval — which I had not seen yet, which were apparently numerous and largely not dangerous and in some cases genuinely strange — would be interested in me and that I should let them approach on their own terms and not pursue.
“What do they look like,” I said. “The creatures.”
“Varied,” he said. “Some you will recognize from mortal stories — the stories got the shapes approximately correct. Others have no mortal equivalent.”
“What’s the closest mortal equivalent to the thing most likely to approach me first.”
He considered this with genuine thought rather than the look of someone producing a convenient answer. “A fox,” he said. “Made of shadow and cold light. It’s been in Nochval’s territory for several decades. It’s curious about unusual things.”
I held his gaze. He held mine. I thought about the carved fox on my windowsill, and the shadow-creature he had just described, and the word unusual landing with the specific emphasis of something placed rather than incidental. “A fox,” I said.
“Yes.”
“Shadow and cold light.”
“Yes.”
“Curious about unusual things.”
“Generally.”
I looked at him for a moment. There was something in this exchange — in the layering of it, the carved fox and the shadow-fox and the word unusual — that I was not yet equipped to read. I could feel the shape of it without its substance. “Good to know,” I said.
“Yes,” he said, and there was something in that yes, something very nearly not-flat, that I was going to think about later when I had time to think about things properly — a category that was expanding.
*   *   *
We had been in the main hall for somewhere between an hour and two — the Hollow’s sky was giving me a little more to work with now, three days in, enough to approximate — when the rules were, as far as I could tell, substantially covered. He showed no signs of ending the conversation, which I noted, and I showed no signs of ending it either, which I also noted, and for a few minutes we sat — he stood, I sat — in a silence that had completely stopped being the silence of a legal briefing and was now just the silence of two people in the same room who had run out of agenda and had not yet decided what to do about that.
“The coat,” I said. Because I had been trying to find an entry point to this question for three days and had not found a better one.
He looked at the coat. I was wearing it — I had been wearing it since I came down, for warmth, because the main hall was colder than my room and I had made the practical decision to wear it rather than carry it and present it at a significant juncture. This was my justification and I was not examining it.
“Can I keep it,” I said.
“It’s yours,” he said.
“You said that already.”
“I meant it then as well.”
“I mean permanently,” I said. “Not — I’m not asking to borrow it. I’m asking if you actually want it back at some point or whether this is a genuine transfer of ownership, because those are different things and I’d like to be clear on which one is happening.”
He looked at me for a moment with the specific look I was cataloguing as his thinking look — not the flat assessment he deployed for observations, but something with more activity behind it. Then: “It’s yours,” he said. “Permanently. A genuine transfer of ownership, if you want to call it that.”
“Why,” I said.
“You were cold.”
“I was cold once,” I said. “On the night we arrived. I’m not cold every day. The coat is considerably finer than anything I’d wear in Zemya, and it’s yours, and I’d like to understand the logic of giving it away rather than lending it.”
He was quiet for a moment. The Hall’s presences, which I was beginning to develop some awareness of — a slight warmth in the air, a quality of attention — were paying very close attention. I had the distinct impression that I had wandered into something that the Hall found interesting, the same impression I’d had when I asked about the fox, and I stored it in the same place.
“The logic,” he said, “is that I have had the coat for two hundred years and you have had it for three days, and it already suits you better.”
I opened my mouth. I closed it. I looked down at the coat, which was dark and dense and draped across my shoulders in the way of something that had been made for a significantly larger person and had, through some mechanism I was not going to examine, managed to fit anyway. I looked back up at him.
He was already turning away, toward the corridor that led to the north wing, which was where the binding told me he spent most of his time. His shoulders were level. His hands were at his sides. He was, as far as I could tell, entirely composed.
“Aleksei,” I said.
He stopped. He did not turn around.
“Thank you,” I said. “For the coat.”
A pause. I watched his back, the set of it, the specific quality of the stillness he produced when something had landed differently than he had anticipated. It lasted perhaps two seconds.
“You’re welcome,” he said, to the corridor.
And then he was gone, and the main hall was empty, and the Hall’s presences were doing the thing they did when they were satisfied about something, which I had come to recognize as a particular warmth in the air, a quality of the ambient light becoming slightly warmer than its default.
I sat alone in the main hall for a moment.
The coat was mine. He had given it away because it suited me better than it suited him. He had owned it for two hundred years. He had given it away because it suited me better. Two hundred years and three days, and he had given it away because it suited me better.
I pulled it slightly tighter around my shoulders, with both hands, slowly, like a person doing something for a completely practical reason.
“Right,” I said to the empty hall, to the warm air, to the presences that were definitely listening. “Good. Fine. That’s fine.”
The Hall was warm. It stayed warm all the way back to my room, which was longer than the walk should have taken, in the way that distances in the Hall occasionally became negotiable when the Hall had opinions about your pace.
I did not hurry it along.
*   *   *
That night I sat at my desk and added to my list.
Not new entries — I had enough new entries, the list was at risk of becoming more list than page. What I added was to an existing line, the one that read:
The fox. What it is. Why it is here. Why he looked at it first.
Below it, in my normal handwriting rather than the small marginal script I used for things I wasn’t sure deserved a line:
Also: the shadow-fox. Curious about unusual things.
And below that, after a moment in which I held the pen above the paper and thought about whether to write it and wrote it:
He called me unusual.
I looked at this for a while. Then I put the pen down and looked at the fox on the windowsill, which was sitting in its composed and deliberate way with its ears forward and its tail curled, attending to the middle distance.
“Don’t look at me like that,” I said.
The fox, being carved from wood and dead for some years, did not respond.
I went to bed.
I was wearing the coat.
The Hollow’s sky outside the window moved through its deep, cycling colors — the blues and greens and the purple that pressed against the eye like a held note — and at the edge of the silver forest, just at the treeline, something sat in the dark that was not quite shadow and not quite light and watched the Hall with patient, interested eyes.
I did not know this then.
I found out in the morning, when I looked out the window first thing and it was still there, and I pressed my hand to the glass, and the not-quite-shadow creature tilted its head, ears forward, attending.
It looked exactly like the fox on my windowsill.
Of course it did.
And then, as I watched it through the glass, it tilted its head very slightly — not toward me, toward something else. The direction of the north study. The direction where Aleksei was.
Reporting.
The shadow-fox was not just watching me. It was reporting what it watched. Every morning at the window. Every moment at the treeline. Every detail of what I did and how I did it.
I pressed my hand to the glass. The shadow-fox’s ears came forward, attending. Patient. Composed.
I had not been unobserved for a single moment since I arrived. And the fox was not the only thing watching.


Chapter Seven: What I Know About Gods, and How Wrong I Was
Kira
The shadow-fox was still at the treeline when I woke up.
I had established this conclusively at dawn — or what passed for dawn in the Hollow — the sky shifting from its deepest indigo toward something closer to the blue of very old glass, slow and considered, like a color deciding to become another color rather than simply changing. The creature was in the same position it had been in when I’d first seen it the night before: sitting, tail around its feet, ears forward, the composed and patient quality of something that had decided to be somewhere and was going to be there until it had a reason not to be.
I stood at the window for a long time, watching it watch the Hall.
It was not threatening. What it had was interest. The particular quality of something that had found something new and was paying attention to it with the patience that came from having no more pressing business.
The no-more-pressing-business part was doing something to my sense of scale. I was the most interesting thing in this territory. I, Kira Voss, low-city thief and retriever of unreturned fees, was the most noteworthy development in an ancient, living realm in recent memory. This was not a position I had previously occupied, and I was not sure the pay was worth the exposure and I was finding it unexpectedly complicated.
I pressed my palm flat against the glass.
The shadow-fox tilted its head. Both ears swiveled forward, tracking the movement. Then it settled again, and the composed attending resumed, and I stood at the window with my hand on the cold glass until the morning got proper enough that I had things to do.
Aleksei was north and far, per the binding. He had been north and far since before I woke. He was occupying a part of Nochval I had not yet been to. I had not tried to go there yet, because the binding told me direction and distance but not invitation, and I had decided that occupying space he was currently in without being invited to was something I would establish a precedent for carefully and at a later date.
For now: he was elsewhere. The Hall was mine.
I put on the coat — for warmth, and also because I was keeping it, and you kept things by using them, that was the principle — and I went to explore.
*   *   *
I had been through the Hall’s main corridors twice now, enough to have a working map of the central spaces. What I had not done was spend time in any of them properly — I had moved through, catalogued, moved on. You could know the shape of a place without knowing what it was like to be in it, and knowing what it was like to be in a place was, I had found, the more useful information.
So I started over, more slowly.
The main hall first — the great central space with the vaulted ceiling that disappeared into the Hollow’s ambient dark above, the accumulated objects along the walls, the table and chairs that had the worn quality of furniture used over centuries. I sat in one of the chairs. I put my feet on the table, because I could, and looked up at the ceiling, and thought about the eight hundred years of people who had and hadn’t sat in this room.
The presences were there. Three of them, I thought, though counting was approximate. They were in the far corners and along the upper reaches of the walls, and they were paying attention to me sitting at the table with the weight of something that found this mildly remarkable.
“Hello,” I said.
The air did something. A slight warming, a particular quality of attention that sharpened without becoming louder. Not a response in any language I had. But not nothing either.
“I’m going to be here for a year,” I told them. “I thought you should know. In case it changes anything about your plans.”
The warmth in the air became slightly more definite. I had the distinct impression that this was the most interesting thing to happen to this room in some time, and that the presences had opinions about it, and that the opinions were, on balance, favorable.
“Good,” I said. “That’s settled then.”
I took my feet off the table, because I had points to make and manners were a separate issue, and I went to look more carefully at the objects along the walls.
*   *   *
The sword I had noticed before. Up close it was older than I had estimated — the style of it was not from any civilization I could date, the hilt too long and the blade too wide by modern standards, the balance suggesting it had been made for hands or habits that no longer existed. Maintained. Recently. Someone took the sword down and tended to it.
I looked at the sword for a while and thought about Aleksei maintaining a sword that was older than most civilizations, and I held this in the place I was keeping things that complicated my picture of him. The pile there was substantial and growing.
The shelves. I went through them slowly — picking things up, turning them over, putting them back. Most of it was unreadable. Three objects were not objects at all but presences, sleeping in forms that looked like decorative vessels.
I put it back carefully and moved on.
The hourglass with the stopped sand: still stopped. I looked at it from several angles. The sand was at the bottom. The glass had either run out or been stopped partway, and I could not tell which. I turned the glass over in my hands. The sand did not move. I turned it right side up again. Still nothing.
“What stops it?” I asked the room.
The presences gave me the character of warmth that I was learning to read as: we know, and it’s not our information to give. Which was, I was finding, the answer the Hall’s small inhabitants produced for a considerable proportion of my questions. They knew a great deal and they had a refined sense of what belonged to whom.
I put the hourglass back and made a note to ask about it later.
*   *   *
The east corridor had a quality to it I had not paid attention to on previous passes — something about the air there, a particular density that was not unpleasant but was notable in the way of something that had accumulated over a long time. Not the closed heaviness of the two rooms that were not ready. Something different. The air in the east corridor had the feel of a room where a great deal had happened and the walls had been paying attention.
I walked it slowly. The corridor was longer than it looked — the Hall did this, made distances negotiable when it had opinions about pace. Three doorways: one open, one locked, one that opened when I touched the handle.
I stood in the doorway of the window room for a while.
The silver forest from this angle was different — the trees shorter, the spaces between them wider, the luminescence dimmer at ground level. And the oldest trees, the ones at the territory’s core, were the ones where the dimming was worst.
There was a stillness at the ground level that was wrong. The ground beneath the oldest trees was not moving when it should have been. I didn’t have a baseline for what it should have felt like, but my body registered the difference the way it registered the wrong streets in Vreshka — with the weight of something off.
I wanted to go in there.
This was interesting — not the want itself — a predictable response to something beautiful, but the sense of it. It was not curiosity exactly. It was more like recognition. The specific pull toward a place that felt familiar in the way that the old-language book had felt familiar, the sense of something reaching toward the back of my mind where I kept things I didn’t know I knew.
I would ask about the trees later. I walked on.
*   *   *
The Hall’s presences, I discovered, were more mobile than I had understood.
I had assumed they occupied their corners and their upper walls with the specificity of things that had found their places and stayed in them. This was wrong. They moved. It was the kind of movement you detected by noticing that the quality of warmth in the air had shifted, that the sense of occupied attention was now coming from a slightly different part of the space.
They were following me.
Not in the way that things followed you when they intended something — not the heavy, directed attention of a threat or an intention. More the way a cat followed you through a house, maintaining proximity not because it wanted anything specific but because it had decided your movement was the most interesting variable in the current environment and it intended to stay adjacent to that variable.
“You don’t have to keep up,” I told the air in the east corridor. “I’m just looking at things.”
The warmth, which had been tracking at approximately a shoulder’s distance to my left, did something that felt like amusement. As much as warmth could feel like anything.
“That’s fair,” I said. “I’m also just looking at things.”
I had been in Vreshka for twenty-three years. I had talked to shadows in dark alleys, to the absence of light at the edges of rooms, to the Hollow-adjacent wrongness of certain streets in the lower city that everyone knew about and nobody acknowledged. This was not so different. It was more domestic. It was, if anything, warmer.
The presences followed me through the rest of the east corridor, through a short connecting passage that smelled of cold stone and something faintly mineral, and back around toward the main hall. They stayed near the high walls and the accumulated objects. The corridors they moved through. There was a distinction, and it mattered to them, and I had passed through it without noticing and they had accompanied me anyway.
“Thank you for the tour,” I said.
The air in the main hall was warm.
*   *   *
I went to the library deliberately this time.
The first visit had been by smell — the correct way, apparently, though I was not above taking directions when they were accurate. This time I went because I had decided to go, which was different, and I went the fastest route rather than the exploratory one because I knew the route now and the library was where I was spending the day and I was not going to pretend otherwise.
I had brought paper.
I settled at the table on the left — the smaller of the two, the one that had the best light and the closest access to the shelves I most wanted to spend time with — and I arranged myself with the paper and the pen I’d taken from my room and the intention of doing something that was going to take several hours. Then I looked at the shelves.
The Hollow’s sky was in its mid-blue register — midday, the deepest saturation before the shift toward evening. Six weeks and it still surprised me. I should not have been thinking about what that meant. But not right now.
Right now: the shelves.
I started at the far left of the room and I worked my way along the floor-level shelves first — the ones most likely to have been organized by someone using them regularly, the ones most accessible without a step stool or the kind of reach that suggested you were very tall or not quite mortal in the manner of your proportions. The books here were in a mixture of languages: two I read fluently, three I could navigate slowly, two more I had enough of to identify the subject matter if not the specifics, and several that I could not place at all.
I pulled the readable ones and stacked them on the table. History, mostly — the Hollow’s record of itself, written in a language that felt more like music than text. And one book that was not history. One book that the shelf held differently, with the careful grip of a structure holding something significant.
I read for two hours. I took notes. I drank the tea that one of the presences had apparently arranged — when I looked up from the historian’s account, there was a cup on the table that had not been there before, filled with something that tasted like the mortal tea I’d been drinking in Vreshka and that was very definitely still hot — and I thought about what I was learning and what it implied about what I didn’t know yet.
Then I looked at the old-language shelf.
*   *   *
I had not planned to look at the old-language shelf. I had some context now. More than I’d had on the first visit. But not enough.
I looked at the old-language shelf anyway.
The book was where I had put it back. I could see the slight disturbance where I’d replaced it — the books on either side shifted by the margin of error I’d acknowledged to myself afterward, the gap where it sat a fraction narrower than the rest of the row. I had put it back very and it was still not quite exact — a thing I had thought about more than was warranted since.
I crossed to the shelf. I stood in front of it with my hands at my sides. I looked at the book.
The book looked back with the specific quality of old things that had been waiting — not patiently, not impatiently, simply in the way of things that existed outside the categories that made patience and its absence meaningful. It had been here a long time. It would be here longer. Whether I took it from the shelf today or in ten years or never was, to the book, a detail.
I took it from the shelf.
The weight of it — the familiar weight, already, five days after first holding it — settled into my hands with the specificity of something that had been handled before. My hands knew this weight, which was impossible, because I had held it once for a few minutes and set it back. Hands did not remember things from brief encounters. Hands were not that kind of organ.
I carried it to the table and sat down and opened it.
*   *   *
The first page.
I had looked at this page before. The same script, the same flowing angular lines with their internal rhythm, the same ink that had not faded. I had looked at it and had the experience of almost-understanding, the reaching-toward that didn’t quite arrive, and had put the book back.
Today I sat with it.
I sat with it for a long time and I did not try to read it — I had given up on trying to read it before I sat down, because trying to read it was approaching it with the tools I had for known languages, and it was becoming clear to me that the tools I had for known languages were not the tools this language required. The script was not a cipher. It was not a variant of something I knew. It was a different system entirely, built on different assumptions, with a different relationship between the marks on the page and the things they were intended to convey.
And yet.
The page did something — not to my eyes or mind but to something below both, in the sub-linguistic register. The mark was not a word or a symbol in any script I recognized. Something between those categories. And it communicated, the way a melody you had heard once and not retained still lived somewhere in you.
I turned to the second page.
The second page was different from the first in a way I would not have been able to articulate until this moment, and could now articulate only as: the first page was an introduction and the second page was the actual content. The distinction was carried in the weight of the marks, in the rhythm, in that sub-linguistic register that I was apparently reading in a language I had never been taught.
I put my finger on the first mark of the second page.
Something happened.
Not dramatic — nothing in the Hollow was dramatic in the way that things in mortal stories were dramatic, with light and sound and the physical expression of significance. What happened was quieter than that. A concept without language, the way very old things sometimes communicated — not through the specific tools that specific civilizations had developed to convey meaning, but through meaning itself, stripped of its vessel.
The concept was: beginning.
Not the beginning of the book. Not the beginning of a story or an account. Something older. The beginning that came before there were other things for it to be the beginning of.
I lifted my finger from the page.
The impression faded immediately — not slowly, the way sensations faded, but in the way of things that had never quite existed in the first place, leaving only the knowledge that they had been there. I sat very still with my hands in my lap and looked at the page and breathed.
The library was quiet. The presences, which had followed me in and settled near the high shelves in the way they settled near the upper reaches of the main hall, were very quiet in a way that was different from their regular quiet. The regular quiet of the presences was attentive. This was the quiet of something trying very hard not to disturb something delicate.
I looked at the page for a long time without touching it.
Then I turned to the third page.
*   *   *
The third page gave me nothing.
I tried the fourth. Nothing — the marks sat on the page and produced no impression in the sub-linguistic register, no concept without language, no reaching-toward. I tried the fifth. The same. I went back to the second page and put my finger on the second mark and received a faint impression that dissolved before I could hold it. I tried the third mark and got nothing.
Whatever I had accessed was not stable. It was not a system I had cracked open. It was a single instance, a single clear impression, and then the door had closed.
Beginning.
I sat with this word — the closest I could get to what I had received, the word being a thin translation of something that had not been a word — and I thought about what it meant that I had received it. Not what it meant that the book contained it. What it meant that I had received it. That the mark under my finger had communicated to me, directly, in a language I had not learned and could not read, in a register I had not known I possessed until five minutes ago.
I was a low-city thief from Vreshka. I had a passing familiarity with four others from years of professional necessity. I had never had any contact with the Hollow, I had never studied old languages, I had never met a Vorath before six days ago, and I had no framework whatsoever for the experience of receiving a pre-linguistic concept through touch from a text written in a language that, according to everything I understood about history and the world, should not exist anymore.
The Hollow had decided I was manageable. Aleksei had said this.
The book in the first language — the language that predated every civilization I had any knowledge of — had given me a concept through a touch.
The trees bent toward me when I walked in the territory. The shadow-fox had stationed itself at the treeline and was watching the Hall. The binding had activated when it had never activated before.
I looked at the ceiling of the library. At the library’s high, narrow windows and the Hollow’s mid-blue sky beyond them. At the densely packed shelves full of centuries of accumulated knowledge in which I had apparently just become, in some quiet and entirely unexplained way, literate.
I said, to the ceiling, to the presences in the upper reaches who were being so carefully quiet: “I’m going to need to think about this.”
The air warmed, very slightly. The presences knew. They had been knowing, the entire time, with the patience of things that had been waiting for me to know too.
*   *   *
I closed the book.
I held it for a moment — the weight of it, the density, the specific way it sat in my hands. Then I put it back on the shelf in exactly its position, the angle, the gap between its neighbors, as precise as I could manage. I stood back and looked at the shelf.
My hands were steady. I was not panicking. I was doing the thing I always did with information that I did not yet understand: I was filing it, and carefully, in the place it belonged, so that I could find it again when I had the context to use it.
I went back to the table. I sat down. I looked at the notes I had been taking before — the historian, the natural philosophy, the Veil Night accounts — and I picked up the pen.
I wrote, at the top of a fresh page:
What I Know About Gods, and How Wrong I Was.
I underlined it. Then I sat looking at it, because the title was accurate and the content was going to take some working out, and I did not know yet how wrong I was, which was itself the problem.
I started writing.
The presences settled in the upper shelves above me, warm and quiet, and the Hollow’s sky outside the windows cycled slowly toward its afternoon configuration, and the book on the shelf held the slight warmth of my hands the way the stone walls held their light: quietly, for a long time, longer than warmth usually lasted.
I wrote for three hours. I crossed out more than I kept. What I kept was this:
The stories in Zemya about the Vorath were wrong. Not maliciously — just inevitably. The Vorath were older, stranger, and more careful than any description I’d heard. Aleksei was the most careful of all.
But evil was a story you told about something when you did not have a better framework.
The better framework was going to take considerably more time.
I was, apparently, going to have a year.
*   *   *
I was back in my room by the time the Hollow’s sky had reached its nadir-adjacent register — not full black, not the deepest indigo, but the shade that preceded it, which I had learned to read as late evening.
The shadow-fox was at the treeline.
I had looked as soon as I came in, which I was going to stop pretending was a coincidence. It was not a coincidence. I came in and I looked at the treeline, and the shadow-fox was there, and I felt the specific satisfaction of something confirming an expectation, and I was going to sit with what that implied later.
I put my notes on the desk alongside the existing list. The new notes were three pages, cramped and crossed-through in places, with questions in the margins that did not have answers yet. You couldn’t answer them all at once. You answered the one whose answer unlocked the next one.
The first question, the one everything else hinged on, was at the top of the sequence. I had been avoiding writing it because writing it made it more real, and it being more real required me to do something about it, and I was not yet sure what doing something about it looked like.
I wrote it.
Why can I almost read that book.
I looked at it. The fox on the windowsill attended to the middle distance. Outdoors, at the treeline, the shadow-fox attended to the Hall. The binding told me Aleksei was closer than he had been all day — still north, but less far, moving in the direction of the Hall. He was coming back.
I thought about the question at the top of the sequence, and the fact that whatever the answer was, he almost certainly already knew it, and the fact that he had said “have you not” when I told him I had never had contact with the Hollow, which had been his version of telling me that the premise of the statement was worth examining.
He knew. Or he knew enough. He had some version of the answer to the question at the top of my sequence and he was holding it in the careful way he held everything — in reserve, offered when he had the right words, given when the time was something he had decided on rather than something that had been decided for him.
When you’re ready, I had told him, when he’d said he’d tell me about the sealed wing eventually.
He was doing the same thing. Waiting. For the right words, for the right time, for me to be ready. And the thing I was finding, sitting in my room with the fox and the shadow-fox and the question at the top of the sequence and the specific warmth in my hands that had come from touching a mark in a book I should not have been able to read — the thing I was finding was that I was not impatient about it.
I was curious. I was very curious — not the same thing. Curiosity could wait. Curiosity gathered information and built context and bided its time because it knew that the answer, when it came, would be better for the preparation.
Impatience wanted the answer now. Impatience didn’t care what state the answer was in when it arrived.
I was curious.
Aleksei was closer. The binding put him at the edge of the Hall’s grounds now, just inside the silver trees. I did not go to the window to look. I sat at my desk and I wrote a note at the bottom of the page — below the question, below the sequence, in the marginal space I kept for things I wasn’t sure yet deserved a line:
He already knows. He’s waiting for me to be ready to ask.
And below that, after a moment in which I held the pen above the paper and thought about whether to write it and wrote it anyway:
I think I almost am.
I looked at the window. The shadow-fox was at the treeline. The binding told me he had stopped moving. He was at the edge of the grounds. Stationary.
Warm, and alert — like it was listening for what came next.
And somewhere in the silver forest, in the ring of oldest trees where the light was thinner than it should have been, a sound beneath the ground. A low resonance, felt more than heard, like a very old engine running slower than it was designed to run.
The shadow-fox’s ears went flat. First time I’d seen that.
Four seconds. Then it stopped. The fox’s ears came forward. The attending resumed.
I stood at the window for a long time, and I could not decide whether what I had heard was the sound of something starting or the sound of something running out.


Chapter Eight: What the Hollow Is Telling Me
Aleksei
I had been spending too much time at Nochval.
This was a fact I had established by the fifth day and had not done anything about, because doing something about it would have required acknowledging it as a problem, and I had not yet decided whether it was a problem or whether it was simply a change in the distribution of my attention that had not yet been assigned a category.
The territory required management. The membrane at the city’s eastern border had needed attention — I had given it attention.
The membrane’s condition was what it always was: thinner than the start of the season, by a fraction that had been accumulating for a hundred years. I had ceased counting. The answer was always the same. Less than before. Steadily. The kind of loss that was too slow to call a crisis and too consistent to call anything else.
 The Hunt’s administrative residue from the Veil Nights required processing — I had processed it. There were two other areas of the Hollow’s outer boundary that bore monitoring in the weeks following the membrane’s dissolution and reconstitution, and I was monitoring them. All of this was legitimate work that had a legitimate claim on my time, and all of it could have been conducted from anywhere in Nochval.
I was conducting it from the north study.
The north study was adjacent to the corridor that ran past the library. This was a coincidence of architecture rather than intention, and I was not going to examine it further.
The Hall’s awareness was the constant low current it had always been — the ambient knowing of a building that was also, in its particular eight-hundred-year-old way, alive. The Hall knew what moved through it. The Hall knew what was handled, what was opened, what was left where it had been or left slightly not where it had been. The Hall had been the keeper of Nochval’s interior memory since before most mortal civilizations had the concept of memory-keeping, and it performed this function with the thoroughness of something that found it intrinsically worthwhile rather than obligatory.
In the first week of Kira Voss’s residence in the Hall, the Hall had not stopped telling me things.
Not urgently. Not with alarm. The Hall found the mortal woman in the south-facing room significant in a way that it had not found anything significant in a very long time, and it communicated this continuously, and I had been receiving the communication with the flat professional attention I applied to the Hall’s reports and not examining what it meant that I was paying attention to every single one.
What I knew about the first week:
She slept in the coat.
This the Hall told me on the first morning, as information it considered relevant. She had taken it off, folded it over the chair at the desk with a care that the Hall registered as the care used for things belonging to other people, and had then put it back on before sleeping, which the Hall registered with the quality it used for things that were interesting. I received this information and set it aside and did not think about it for the rest of the morning and thought about it at irregular intervals for the rest of the day.
She had found the library on the first day, which I had expected — the library was findable by smell, for anyone paying the right quality of attention to their environment, and she paid that quality of attention to everything. She picked things up and put them back. The Hall noted, with something that translated approximately as approval, that she put things back precisely.
She had talked to the presences.
This was less expected. She had not just detected them. She had addressed them directly, in the conversational tone she used for everything, as if introducing herself to a building’s inhabitants were an ordinary courtesy and not something that had not happened in three centuries. She had told them she was going to be there for a year. She had thanked them for the tour.
The presences had been warm about it for days. They were still warm about it. They had not been warm about anything in three centuries and the warmth was, in the specific quiet way of eight-hundred-year-old accumulated attention, very loud.
I had not been to the library while she was in it. The decision was: her spaces were hers. The binding gave me what the law required. The Hall told me what the Hall told me. I was not going to add my own presence to that accounting.
These were the principles. They were clear. They were also, I was finding, easier to state than to maintain in the face of a Hall that was continuously reporting on what she was doing and a binding that was continuously reporting on how she was, and the gap between the report and the room was becoming, daily, slightly more noticeable.
On the seventh day, the Hall told me she had gone back to the old-language shelf.
*   *   *
I set down what I was working on.
She had found the book on the first day — I had felt her take it, and the Hall had communicated its particular quality of careful attention, and I had felt the shift when she opened it and then the different shift when she put it back exactly where it had been. I had gone to the library after she left and confirmed the exact. I had stood in front of the shelf and not taken the book down and had gone to find Vasilisa because I had questions I did not know how to ask yet.
Vasilisa had given me pieces. The Old Claim, she had said. Beings chosen by the first world to carry its memory forward. The last one died before the separation. The line was thought gone.
I had not told Vasilisa about the book. I had said: the binding activated. I had not said this because I had not yet decided what it meant and I did not discuss things I had not decided the meaning of, as a principle, and also because saying it aloud would have produced a register of reaction from Vasilisa that I was not prepared for in that particular conversation.
The Hall told me she had taken the book from the shelf again.
I stayed in the north study.
The Hall told me she had sat down with it. That she had opened it. That she was looking at the first page.
I stayed in the north study.
The Hall told me she had turned to the second page.
I stayed — with somewhat more effort than I generally applied to staying — in the north study.
And then the Hall told me something it had never told me in eight hundred years of telling me things, in a register I had not felt it use before: not the warmth of approval, not the weight of significance, not the careful quiet of something protecting something delicate. Something older than all of those. Something the Hall used for the first time in my eight centuries of knowing it, because this was the first time it had had occasion to use it.
She had touched a mark on the page.
And the mark had answered.
I went very still.
I had been still before — I was always still, it was the default condition of something that had lived long enough that movement required a reason — but this was a different quality of stillness. The kind that came from the outside in rather than the inside out. The kind that happened when something external produced a state that the body arrived at before the mind had processed the cause.
The mark had answered her.
In eight hundred years of that book sitting on that shelf, in the centuries before the Hall was built when the book had existed in the old territory and the Hollow had known it was there and left it alone with the respect the Hollow afforded to things that were older than it — in all of that time, across everything the book had witnessed and survived and held — the marks in it had never answered anyone. The book was written in the first language. The first language had two speakers remaining in existence. I was one of them.
The mark had answered her.
I looked at the wall that faced south. I looked at it for a considerable amount of time. Then I said, quietly, to the north study and the work I was not doing and the eight hundred years of accumulated walls and stone and careful construction around me: “Not yet.”
Not yet meaning: I am not ready for what this means. Not yet meaning: let me have another day of not knowing before I have to know. Not yet meaning the thing underneath all of it that I had no adequate language for, which was that for the first time since before the sealed wing had a reason to be sealed, something in the world had surprised me badly enough that I needed a moment before I could face it.
Not yet.
The Hall was very quiet. Even the presences had stilled — the ambient warmth of them reduced to something barely perceptible, the specific quiet of things that understood the weight of a moment and were giving it the space it required.
In the library, the binding told me, she had lifted her finger from the page. She was still. She was breathing slowly and deliberately — the pattern she used — I had learned it across seven days of the binding’s constant directional awareness — when she was managing something rather than reacting to it. She was managing.
Of course she was.
*   *   *
I went to Vasilisa in the late afternoon.
Not to the Hall — she was not at the Hall, had not been since her visit on the second day. Vasilisa maintained her own territory in the older part of the Hollow, a region that predated most of the current geography, accessible through the deep membrane-paths that I knew by long practice and that most other beings used maps for. I went through them quickly, with the economy of something that had made this journey more times than it could usefully count, and arrived at the edge of her territory as the Hollow’s sky was cycling toward its mid-afternoon saturation — the deepest blue before the slow descent toward evening.
Vasilisa’s territory did not look like Nochval. It had everything — colors the Hollow’s palette didn’t include, textures from several ecosystems at once. The ground shifted underfoot like a landscape that enjoyed being different. Through the shifts I caught glimpses of stone walls and the dim shape of a hill, all of it transparent, all of it real.
She was in the thin-membrane region when I arrived. She was sitting on a rock that existed in both registers — solid in the Hollow, the barest suggestion of itself in Zemya — with a book open in her lap that she was not reading. Her mismatched eyes were on the ghost-landscape of the mortal world, the one-amber-one-grey tracking something in the middle distance that had nothing to do with what was physically present.
She said, without looking up: “You felt it.”
“Yes,” I said.
“So did I.” She closed the book. Not marking her page — she did not mark pages, she had the Vorath of Memory’s perfect recall of text and had never needed a bookmark in her existence. “Tell me exactly what you felt.”
I told her. The Hall’s report, the mark responding, the quality of the activation — as precise as I could be about something that had no precedent in my experience and therefore no existing vocabulary. I told her what the activation felt like from the Hall’s end: not the warmth of approval, not significance. Older. Something the Hall had been waiting to use.
Vasilisa was quiet for a long time after I finished.
Her quiet was different from mine. I was still because movement required a reason. She went there the way other people went to libraries: purposefully, with a specific question, returning with whatever the archive chose to give her.
When she came back, her expression had the particular quality I had learned to read as: I have found something significant and I am deciding how to give it to you.
“The Old Claim,” she said, “is not passive.” She stood from the rock. “The Claim is the Hollow recognizing something in the carrier. A capacity. A specific resonance with the deep current that goes beyond what the binding measures. The Old Claim is the Hollow saying: this one. Not for us. For itself.”
“The book,” I said.
“The book is one of them,” Vasilisa said. “The marks in it are not writing, Aleksei. They are not a record. They are the first world’s own language — not a language that was developed to describe things, but a language that is things. The marks carry the actual content, not a representation of it.” She stopped walking. She turned to look at me with both mismatched eyes. “The first language can only be read by something carrying the Old Claim. It cannot be learned. It cannot be translated. It responds to the Claim or it does not respond at all.”
The clearing was quiet. The ghost-landscape of Zemya moved through its afternoon in the thin-membrane shimmer — clouds passing over a distant hill, the suggestion of wind in grass that was not quite there.
“She received one mark,” I said.
“One,” Vasilisa agreed. “The first one on the second page.”
“Beginning.”
Vasilisa looked at me steadily. “How do you know what the mark says.”
I said nothing. She asked this one because she wanted me to say it aloud. Because saying things aloud was the mechanism by which you moved them from the private register into the world, and she had decided this particular thing needed to be in the world.
“I wrote part of that book,” I said.
“I know,” she said. Gently, for Vasilisa — not a word she deployed without intention.
“Before the separation,” I said. “Before most of what currently exists had been decided. The second page — the beginning of the actual content — I wrote that page.” I looked at the ghost-landscape. At the hill in Zemya that was barely a shadow of itself here in the thin membrane. “The mark she touched was mine.”
The clearing absorbed this the way the Hollow absorbed most things: quietly, without drama, with the patience of something that had been present for all of it and had opinions about none of it. Vasilisa did not say anything for a moment. When she spoke, her voice had the quality it used when she was being careful on behalf of someone else rather than herself.
“You need to tell her,” she said. “Not all of it — not yet. But something. She is building a sequence of questions and she has the wrong one at the top.” A pause. “She’s going to get to the right one on her own very soon. It would be better if she arrived there with some of the foundation already in place.”
“I know,” I said.
“When.”
I thought about the not-yet I had said to the north study walls three hours ago. About the first-world language that had two speakers remaining. About a mortal woman in a coat that was hers now, sitting in a library building a sequence, already almost at the right question.
“When I have the right words,” I said.
Vasilisa looked at me for a long moment. “You wrote part of that book,” she said. “Before the separation. Before most things. You have had the longest possible time to find words for this.”
“I know,” I said.
“So the question,” she said, with the particular Vasilisa quality of saying the hard thing in the lightest possible tone, “is not whether you have words. It’s whether you intend to use them.”
I looked at the ghost-landscape of Zemya. At the hill. At the distance.
“I’m working on it,” I said.
Vasilisa smiled, the real one, the one she kept behind the bright social performance. “I know,” she said. “I can tell.” She retrieved her book from the rock and turned toward the deeper part of her territory. “Go home, Aleksei. She’s been alone all day.”
“She is not alone,” I said. “She has the presences. She has—”
“She is alone,” Vasilisa said, over her shoulder, already walking. “Go home.”
I went home.
*   *   *
The binding recalibrated as I crossed back into Nochval’s territory — the directional sense settling from the extended range into the closer resolution it maintained within the Hall’s grounds. She was in her room. Stationary. At her desk, from the specificity of the sense, which I had learned to read to that degree of precision over seven days and which I was not going to examine the implications of tonight.
I went through the silver trees slowly.
Slowly because there was no urgency — she was in her room, she was well, the binding reported nothing that required action. Not because I was finding, in the late-Hollow-afternoon light of the oldest trees, that the boundary between working out what to say to her and simply returning to the building she was in had become less clear than it had been a week ago.
The shadow-fox was at the treeline. It watched me pass without moving. Its eyes, which were the cold-light quality of all its kind, tracked me through the trees with the patient following attention it had been directing at the Hall since the second day.
It was not watching the Hall.
It was watching the Hall’s south-facing window, third floor.
I looked at the shadow-fox. It looked at me. We maintained this for a moment — the specific quality of two beings who both understood something about the situation and were not going to discuss it, because the shadow-fox did not speak and I was not going to be the one to articulate it.
I walked on. The shadow-fox returned its attention to the window.
