There was something in its quality of attending that was not territorial and not predatory and that I recognized, with the particular discomfort of recognizing something you have been trying not to name, as the same quality I produced when I stood in the north study adjacent to the library corridor.
The Hollow was paying attention to her. The Hollow’s creatures were paying attention to her. The Hall’s presences were warmer than they had been in three centuries. My binding — the one that had activated when no binding had activated in forty-seven Hunts — sat in my awareness with the ease of something that had always been there.
I had said not yet to the north study walls.
I was going to need better walls.
*   *   *
I was at the Hall’s entrance when I felt the membrane-signature.
Not a threat — nothing in the signature was hostile, nothing was wrong. The double-presence of it. The way it felt in the Hunt-sense like two things occupying the same point, overlapping with the precision of something that had been overlapping for a very long time.
I recognized the signature the way you recognized something you had known for centuries: not because you had to think about it, but because recognition was the immediate and involuntary response to a familiar thing.
I stopped walking.
I looked at the membrane’s surface at the eastern edge of Nochval’s territory, where the thin-place ran along the old boundary I had maintained since before the Hall was built. The signature was there. It had been there, I understood, for some hours — in the way of something that had arrived at the membrane’s surface and had been sitting there, in both registers at once, feeling what was on the other side.
And now it was moving.
Toward the Hall. Specifically, with the directional certainty of something that knew exactly where it was going, toward the Hall’s east face. Which contained the east corridor. Which contained the library.
I stood at the Hall’s entrance and I thought about the mark on the second page of the book in the first language, and the thing that had answered when she touched it, and the membrane-signature that had been sitting at Nochval’s eastern boundary since she had been in the library this morning, and the movement that had started exactly now.
I said nothing for a long moment.
Then, to the evening air, to the silver trees, to the shadow-fox watching its window: “Of course.”
I went inside.
The Hall, which was aware of everything that moved within its boundary, was already warm with the quality it used for things it found significant. It had been waiting for this particular significant thing, I understood, for considerably longer than it had been waiting for Kira Voss, and this was saying something, because the Hall had been waiting for Kira Voss since before she had a name.
I did not go to the east corridor.
Whatever was about to happen in the library was going to happen whether or not I was present for it, and my presence would complicate it in ways that the library and its new inhabitant did not require from me tonight. They were going to have a conversation that had nothing to do with the binding or the law or the position I occupied in this territory, and that was the kind of conversation that was best had without me in the room.
I went to the north study. I sat down. I picked up the work I had left undone all day. The membrane at the eastern border required attention and I was going to attend to it.
The binding told me she was still at her desk. Still stationary. And then — after a pause that the binding communicated as a shift in her awareness, the recalibration she produced when something unexpected had entered her register — no longer stationary.
She was moving toward the library.
I set down the work again. I looked at the north study wall. I thought about the membrane-signature now moving through the Hall’s east corridor, and about a mortal woman with a list of questions and a fox on her windowsill and a second-page mark that had said beginning to her through a touch, walking toward whatever was waiting for her in the library.
And then I thought: not anymore.
The Hall’s warmth deepened, slow and certain, in the way of something that had been patient for a very long time and was now, finally, watching things begin to go the way they were always going to go.
I picked up the work.
I did not put it down again.
This was progress.
The binding pulsed once — not urgent, not alarming, simply present in a way it had not been present before. The quality of something that had been waiting and was now beginning to move.
I did not know what it meant.
But the membrane at the eastern boundary had thinned by a measurable degree since the last time I had checked it. Three days ago.
Three days was not enough for measurable thinning. Not under normal conditions.
The conditions were no longer normal.


Chapter Nine: Someone in My Chair
Kira
Something moved through the Hall.
I was at my desk when it happened — writing the fourth revision of the question at the top of my sequence, trying to find a version that was precise enough to be answerable, when the Hall did a thing it had not done before. It shifted. Not physically — nothing moved, no sound, no change in the ambient light or the temperature of the room. But it was not the presences. It was something else. Something that occupied the same register the presences occupied and was considerably louder about it.
The binding was doing nothing unusual. Aleksei was in the north study — close, stationary, where he had been for the better part of the day. This was not him.
I sat still for a moment and paid attention to the shift, the way I paid attention to things I could not immediately categorize. It was moving. Through the Hall, in the east corridor, toward the library. The particular feel of something that knew where it was going and intended to get there and was going to find what it found when it arrived and have opinions about it.
I put down my pen.
I thought about staying in my room. I thought about the fact that it was evening, that the Hollow’s sky had been cycling toward its deeper register for the past hour, that I had been writing for three hours and my hand was cramped and my tea was cold, and that whatever was in the east corridor was heading for the library — my library, insofar as I had claimed it as mine over seven days of daily occupation.
I was not going to let something have opinions in my library without knowing what it was.
I picked up my pen, looked at it, put it back down, and went to the library.
*   *   *
Someone was sitting in my chair.
Not my chair in any formal sense — I had not been given a chair, specifically, had not been told that any chair was mine, and the library had two perfectly functional tables with perfectly functional chairs attached to both. But I had been sitting in the left-side chair at the left-side table every day for a week, and in the same way that a worn spot on a path became the path, that chair had become my chair through the sheer accumulated weight of seven days of habitual use.
Someone was sitting in it.
She was reading my notes.
Not the old notes. The book was where I had left it, pages still open to the mark that had said beginning. And beside it, something new: a folded page, written in a hand that was not mine, in a script I could not read, placed with the deliberate care of someone who wanted it found.
I stood in the doorway.
She did not look up.
She was — I catalogued, because cataloguing was what I did when I needed a moment — approximately my age in appearance, though something in the tone of her presence suggested that appearance was not the relevant measure. The not-quite-color of the membrane when it dissolved on the Veil Nights. The color the sky went wrong in the alley before everything changed.
She was slightly translucent at the edges.
Not dramatically — not in a way I would have noticed if I hadn’t been paying the particular feel of attention I paid to things that were not quite what they appeared. Like looking at something through very clean glass. Like a thing that was entirely real and also, somehow, in two places at once.
She turned a page of my notes.
“These are mine,” I said.
“I know,” she said. She had a voice that matched the rest of her — not quite fully in the room, the way a voice heard from the next room was not quite fully present but was entirely real. “Your handwriting is very legible. Most people who think fast have terrible handwriting. It’s a structural problem — the hand can’t keep up with the mind, so the mind wins and the letters lose. You’ve solved it by writing smaller.”
I looked at my notes in her hands. My handwriting was, objectively, quite small. “You’re reading them.”
“I am.” She did not stop.
“Without asking.”
“Also correct.” She turned another page. “You’ve organized these by question urgency, which is smart, but the sequence has a flaw. You’ve put the foundational question at the bottom because you think it requires all the others to be answered first.” She looked up properly this time, both membrane-colored eyes on mine, and the directness of it was different from Aleksei’s directness — his was the directness of something contained, carefully managed. Hers was simply the directness of something that had decided eye contact was the most efficient use of the available space. “You have it backwards. The foundational question is the one everything else hangs off. It goes first.”
I crossed to the table. I sat down in the other chair — the right-side one, not my chair, but available — and I looked at her across my own notes. “Who are you,” I said.
She closed the notes. Not quickly — with the deliberate care of someone who understood that other people’s things were other people’s things given that she had been reading them uninvited for however long she’d been here. She set them on the table between us. “Sable,” she said. “I’m a Remnant. Don’t worry, most people haven’t heard of them — it’s not a common category. I’ll explain.”
“Please,” I said.
She explained.
*   *   *
It took, as she had apparently estimated in advance, approximately thirty seconds.
She delivered it directly, without preamble, with the specific economy of someone who valued efficiency because she had spent her life in environments where inefficiency cost you things you could not afford to lose. She was, I was beginning to understand, a person who had been shaped by scarcity into something precise.
“How old are you,” I said, when she finished.
“Older than the Hall,” she said. “Older than Aleksei. Younger than the book on the third shelf from the left, which predates everything currently in existence except the first world itself, and I wouldn’t recommend touching it until you’ve got more of the Claim active.” She said this last part the way you said something that had been established, a known fact being referenced rather than introduced. Then she paused, with the manner of someone registering that she had said something the other person did not have context for. “Ah,” she said. “You don’t know about the Claim yet.”
“I know about the binding,” I said carefully. “And the Old Claim — Aleksei mentioned it. In the context of suggesting there was more to tell.”
“There is considerably more to tell,” Sable confirmed. “He’ll tell you when he has the right words. He’s working on them. He’s been working on them since the second day, which is slow even for him, but the subject matter is genuinely complicated and he is — ” She paused. She seemed to be selecting from a range of options. “He is someone who takes words seriously,” she said — a sentence with a great deal of room in it that I was going to look at from several angles later. “He won’t give you the wrong ones.”
I looked at her across the table. She looked back with those membrane-colored eyes that pulled at my recognition the way the old-language book pulled at it — the reaching-toward, the almost.
“You know him,” I said.
“Eight hundred years,” she said. “Give or take. He built the Hall here because of the membrane-thinning in the east corridor, and the membrane-thinning in the east corridor is where I’m most comfortable, and the intersection of those two facts has made us neighbors since before he finished the east wing.” She glanced at the door, in the direction of the east corridor, with a look that was fond in the specific way of fondness that had been tested regularly and had held. “He tried to make me leave twice. Once in the first century and once about four hundred years ago, when he was going through something. I didn’t leave either time.” A pause. “He hasn’t tried a third time.”
“He just gave up,” I said.
“He recognized that some situations don’t resolve,” she said, which sounded like something she had told him directly at some point and that he had received with the flat acceptance of a person presented with accurate information he did not like. “I’m fairly immovable when I’ve decided to be somewhere. It’s a membrane thing. Once a Remnant is anchored to a space, the space is part of their register, and you’d have to move the space itself to move the Remnant.” She considered. “He has not yet attempted to move the east corridor.”
“I imagine he’s thought about it,” I said.
Something shifted in her expression — not quite a smile but in the same family. “Every decade or so,” she agreed.
*   *   *
“Why are you here tonight,” I said. “Specifically. In the library.”
“Because you touched the mark on the second page of the first-language book this afternoon,” she said, “and the mark activated, and I felt it from the membrane’s surface, and I thought it was worth coming to see.”
“From the eastern membrane. I’m sensitive to first-language activations — old reflex, from before the separation. The first language was the only language then, and Remnants are calibrated for it.” She tilted her head slightly, which sent her hair — the not-black Hollow-deep color of it — shifting at the edges in a way that was almost luminescent. Not quite. “You received beginning,” she said. Not a question.
“Yes,” I said. I had not told anyone this. Aleksei knew through the Hall, and whatever Aleksei knew Vasilisa probably knew, but I had not said it to anyone directly. I was saying it now without deciding to, which was information about what Sable produced in the people around her — some quality that made the usual management of information feel like unnecessary overhead.
“The first mark on the second page,” she said. “Of course it was. The whole first page is an introduction that doesn’t give you anything until you’ve already understood everything, which is typical of the first world’s approach to communication. Very efficient if you already know. Very unhelpful if you don’t.” She looked at the shelf where the book sat, the same assessing look I’d been directing at it for a week. “You put it back exactly.”
“I always put things back exactly,” I said.
“I know.” She looked at me again. “So does the Hall. So does he.”
This landed with the specific weight of something placed carefully. Not thrown, not dropped — set down, with precision, in a position it was going to stay in. I chose not to pick it up yet. “You said I’m asking the wrong question,” I said instead. “The one at the bottom of the sequence.”
“You’ve put it at the bottom because you think answering it requires everything above it as foundation,” she said. “You’re approaching it academically. Establish the smaller knowns, build toward the larger unknown. It’s a sound method for most things.”
“But not for this.”
“Not for this,” she confirmed. “The question at the bottom of your sequence — I saw it, you wrote it after the mark activated, it’s the last thing on the third page — is the question that answers most of the ones above it automatically. You don’t need the foundation first. The question is the foundation.”
I thought about the question. About the three words I had written at the bottom of the third page in my smallest handwriting, in the margin, after sitting very still for a long time.
Why can I almost read that book.
“What’s the right question,” I said.
Sable looked at me for a moment. The membrane-colored eyes were very steady. “We’ll get there,” she said.
“That’s not an answer.”
“It’s a timeline,” she said. “There’s a difference.” She picked up my notes again, which I was deciding to interpret as a positive sign about the direction of things rather than a boundary violation, because she had set them down with care and was picking them up with care and the picking-up-with-care carried the bearing of someone engaging with something they intended to treat properly. “The right question requires some groundwork first. I’ll give you the groundwork over the next few weeks and then you’ll arrive at the right question on your own, which will be better than if I just told you.”
“Better for whom.”
“You,” she said. “Some things have to be found rather than given. This is one of them.” She turned a page. “Have you eaten? You’ve been in here since the afternoon and the Hall said you didn’t come to the main room for food.”
I stared at her. “The Hall told you that.”
“The Hall talks to me,” she said simply. “I’ve been here eight hundred years. We have an arrangement.” She glanced up. “It’s also extremely obvious that you haven’t eaten — you get a particular quality of focused when you’re running low that’s very recognizable once you know to look for it. All mortals do it. You hold still a little more than usual and your blinks get slightly slower.”
I processed this. “You can tell how hungry I am by my blink rate.”
“I’ve been paying attention to mortals for longer than most,” she said, with a serenity I was going to find either deeply comforting or deeply alarming and had not yet decided which. “It’s not surveillance. It’s pattern recognition.”
“There’s a fine line.”
“There is,” she agreed, pleasantly. “Food. Let’s go.”
*   *   *
We ate in the main hall, because the food was there and because the main hall was large enough that two people eating in it did not feel like an imposition on the space’s other purposes. The Hall’s presences were, I noticed immediately, behaving differently in Sable’s presence — not the careful quiet they used around me when I was managing something, not the warm approval they produced when I did something they found correct, but the particular feel of things that had been waiting for a particular person to arrive and were now, with the subdued satisfaction of beings who did not express satisfaction loudly, registering that she had.
“They missed you,” I said.
Sable looked at the upper reaches of the main hall with an expression I was beginning to identify as the one she used for things she had feelings about and was choosing not to produce those feelings in full view. “I was here six months ago,” she said. “They have a long memory.”
“Six months doesn’t seem long,” I said. “Given the timescales everyone here seems to operate on.”
“Six months is very long to something that was alone for three hundred years before that,” she said, in the even tone she used for most things and that I was learning did not indicate the absence of feeling but rather the very precise management of it. The presences warmed. She looked at her food.
I looked at my food as well, because she had not looked away from hers for the specific reason of giving herself somewhere to look that wasn’t at me, and the courtesy of the moment required giving her somewhere to look that wasn’t at me noticing her.
We ate in silence for a few minutes. It was a comfortable silence, which surprised me, because I had met this person approximately forty minutes ago and comfortable silence was the kind of thing I associated with people I had known for years and trusted completely. Sable produced comfortable silence the way Aleksei produced productive silence — not by working at it but by existing in a way that made the space around her feel like it was being used rather than unfilled.
“The coat,” she said.
I looked up.
She was looking at the coat — the coat I was wearing, the coat I was always wearing, the coat that was mine now through what I maintained was a genuine transfer of ownership and not anything else. Her expression was the same neutral-with-something-underneath she had been managing since we sat down.
“He gave it to you on the third day,” she said.
“How do you know that,” I said, in exactly the tone I had used when she knew about the notes, because some situations deserved the same response they had produced before.
“The Hall talks,” she said, the same answer as before with the same easy delivery. “You just have to know how to listen.” She picked up her cup, looked into it. “That coat is two hundred years old. He made it himself — he has a period, around the three-hundred-year mark for most Vorath, where they go through an intensive craft phase. Aleksei’s was textiles. Very specific. He doesn’t do it anymore.”
I looked at the coat. I thought about the fact that he had made it with his own hands in what was apparently a phase of his existence that no longer existed. I thought about him holding it out in the dark at the boundary of the Hall’s grounds with the flat declaration of a person for whom the decision was already made.
“He gave away something he made,” I said.
“He gave away something he made,” she confirmed. “On the third day.” A pause. “Because it suited you better.”
I stared at her. He had said that to me directly, the morning of the rules briefing, and I had been sitting with it ever since and had not told anyone, not Tomasz through the mirror, not the presences, not my notes — I had written around it, written about the coat, written about the transfer of ownership, but I had not written those words. “He told you that,” I said.
“He didn’t have to,” she said. “I told you — the Hall talks. And I know him.” She set down her cup. “He has not given anyone anything he made since before the sealed wing had a reason to be sealed. That’s one hundred and fifty years of not giving anyone anything.” She looked at me steadily. “He gave you the coat on the third day.”
I opened my mouth. I closed it. This was the warmth of things that had been paying attention to the same situation for a week and were relieved that someone was finally saying it.
“I don’t know what you want me to do with that,” I said.
“Nothing yet,” Sable said. “It’s just groundwork.” She picked up her food again with the matter-of-fact quality of someone who had said what they needed to say and was now going to eat dinner and let it settle. “What’s the shadow-fox doing now? It was at the treeline when I came in.”
I was sufficiently grateful for the subject change that I answered immediately. “It was there this morning too. I pressed my hand to the glass and it tilted its head.”
“It tilted its head,” Sable said, with the specific warmth of someone receiving confirmation of something they had suspected. “Has it moved closer since yesterday?”
“A few feet,” I said. “Maybe.” I had not been actively measuring. I had been looking at it each morning without quite deciding to and noting its position without quite deciding to and I was absolutely measuring.
“It’ll be at the grounds’ edge within the week,” she said. “And at the Hall’s entrance within the month. They move slowly when they’re being careful.” She glanced toward the window that faced south — the one I had not approached from this room yet, the one I had been in the habit of not approaching because it was too close to the territory that felt like his rather than mine. “It’s being careful.”
“Why,” I said.
“Because you matter to it,” she said simply. “Things that matter require care.”
I sat with this. The Hall’s presences were warm. The Hollow’s sky outside the south window was cycling toward its deepest color, the purple that pressed, and somewhere to the north — close, stationary, the north study — the binding put Aleksei still at his work.
“Sable,” I said.
“Mm.”
“The groundwork you’re going to give me.” I was choosing my words with the care she had used for hers, because she had earned that care and because I was finding that she was the kind of person who noticed when care had been taken. “The question that goes first instead of last. Is it going to change things.”
She was quiet for a moment. The real kind of quiet — not management, not strategy, the kind that meant she was going to say something she actually meant rather than something she had decided to say. “Yes,” she said.
“In what direction.”
She looked at me across the table in the main hall of an eight-hundred-year-old Hall in a realm that ran by different rules, with her membrane-colored eyes and her almost-translucent edges and the particular feel of someone who had been caught in the moment between two worlds for long enough that they could see both clearly. “The right direction,” she said. “I wouldn’t be here if it weren’t.”
I nodded. I picked up my food.
We ate. And Sable, between courses, mentioned without emphasis that the eastern boundary had been active overnight. Not a breach. Pressure. Someone testing response times.
*   *   *
She stayed until the sky reached its deepest indigo, and then she stood and gathered nothing — she had brought nothing, she had arrived with only herself and apparently needed only herself — and moved toward the door with the particular quality of motion she had, the slightly-not-fully-solid quality that was not graceless but was different from the way solid things moved.
“I’ll come back tomorrow,” she said.
“I didn’t invite you,” I said.
She smiled then — the first full smile, the real one, and it was different from everything I had seen her produce so far in the same way that the real versions of things were always different from the managed ones. It changed her whole face. “No,” she agreed. “You didn’t.”
She left. The main hall settled. The presences, which had been warm and attentive since she arrived, produced a register I had not felt from them before — something like the exhale of a long breath, the particular feel of things that had been waiting and were now, quietly, not waiting anymore.
I sat at the table for a while after she left.
I thought about the coat and the two hundred years and the third day and the sealed wing. I thought about the question at the bottom of my sequence and the right question that went first. I thought about the right direction, and what it meant that someone who had existed since before the world had its current shape thought this situation was worth coming to see.
Beginning of what, I did not know yet.
I was going to find out.
I went to bed with the coat on and the fox watching the middle distance and the shadow-fox watching the window and the binding steady in my awareness — Aleksei in the north study, close, stationary, still working. Still there.
The Hall held its warmth in the way it had been warm since she left: the way of something that had exhaled.
I was, for the first time in seven days, not alone.
I had not known, until the moment I wasn’t, how much I had been.
And now that I knew, I would have to decide what to do with the knowing.
Except that the binding shifted — not her location, not her state. Something finer. A note I had not read before, quiet and particular.
She was dreaming. And the binding was telling me what the dream concerned.
I closed the channel. Immediately. Some information was not mine to have.
But I had already felt what it was, and it was not rest. It was the sub-linguistic register — the same frequency the book used. She was dreaming in first-language.


Chapter Ten: The Weight Shift
Kira
It was Aleksei who suggested the sparring, which I had not expected, and which I spent approximately ten minutes trying to talk myself out of reading into.
Two weeks in, and the days had a shape. Mornings: exercises in the east courtyard. Midmorning: the library, working through the books. Afternoons: the sessions. Evenings: the territory, alone, learning which paths the silver trees preferred and which the shadow-fox used.
The shadow-fox had reached the Hall’s grounds’ edge four days ago. I had said good morning to it. It had tilted both ears forward. This was the closest thing to a conversation we’d had and it was genuinely the highlight of my week, which I was choosing not to examine in terms of what it said about my social situation.
Evenings: the main hall with Sable and whatever food the Hall had decided we were having. Then my room, the desk, the list that was no longer a list of unknowns but a working document of things I was assembling toward the right question, which Sable was delivering groundwork for at the pace of someone who had decided on the pace and was not going to be hurried about it.
Aleksei: present at edges. The north study adjacent to the library. The main hall sometimes in the mornings, briefly, where we had developed the specific wordless efficiency of two people who occupied the same space at the same time regularly enough to have established its choreography without discussing it — he moved left, I moved right, neither of us announced the adjustment.
I was cataloguing all of this. I catalogued it in the document and I catalogued it in the part of my awareness that the binding lived in and I was doing very well at treating both as information rather than anything else.
Then he appeared in the library doorway on a Thursday — I was maintaining a loose approximation of the mortal week on the grounds that structure was useful even when arbitrary — and said: “The training begins today.”
“The territory navigation training,” I said. “You said a week. It has been two weeks.”
“You were acclimating,” he said.
“I told you I’d acclimated on day three.”
“You had not acclimated on day three,” he said, in the tone of someone stating a fact and not a judgment. “You had adjusted to the sensory register. Acclimation includes the body. Your body needed two weeks.”
I thought about this. I thought about the exercises in the east courtyard and the way the Hollow’s air had felt different in my lungs for the first week — not bad, just different, denser somehow, more present, as if the air itself had more content than Zemya’s. I thought about how by the second week it had stopped feeling like anything at all. “Fine,” I said. “Today.”
“Today,” he confirmed. A pause — the specific productive quality of a pause in which he was deciding whether to say something additional. “The territory training will include physical preparation,” he said. “The deep Hollow requires — certain capabilities. I’ll begin training those alongside the navigation.”
I looked at him. He looked back with the silver eyes and the nothing expression that I had, in two weeks, developed a significant vocabulary for and that I was fairly certain I was still only reading correctly about forty percent of the time. “Physical preparation,” I said.
“Sparring,” he said.
Sable, who had been reading in my chair with the apparent lack of interest of someone who was paying very close attention, turned a page.
*   *   *
The courtyard he took me to was not the east courtyard I used for my morning exercises. The walls were high. The sky above was in its mid-morning register, the deepest blue before noon, and the light it produced fell evenly into the courtyard without the shadows that would have complicated the space. It was, I noticed immediately, a very well-designed training space. Everything about it was deliberate.
He had been here before. Many times. The ground knew it.
I left the coat at the courtyard entrance — it was his coat, he had made it, and I was not going to damage it through careless application to a sparring session even if it was mine now through a genuine transfer of ownership — and I came onto the dark earth in my shirtsleeves. The Hollow’s air was cool on my arms. Not cold — the acclimation had adjusted my baseline in ways I was still mapping — but present, noticeable, the particular temperature of a realm that ran several degrees below what I was accustomed to and that I was now accustomed to.
He was already in the center of the courtyard. I had not previously had occasion to notice the feel of how he was built, because the coat and the layers between us had managed the situation. The layers were not currently managing the situation.
I noticed.
I stopped noticing, because noticing was not useful right now and I had a sparring session to approach strategically.
“Tell me what you know,” he said.
“About sparring.”
“About fighting,” he said. “What you have and what you don’t.”
This was the right question to start with and I appreciated that he had asked it rather than assuming. “I’m fast,” I said. “Faster than I look, faster than most people expect. I’m accurate — I don’t waste movement. I’m good with a knife at close range and reasonable at longer distances.” I paused. “I am very good at not being where someone expects me to be.”
He listened to all of this with the attention he gave everything — complete, without interruption, the composure of someone who was actually receiving information rather than waiting for their turn to speak. “You’ve been in real situations,” he said. It was not a question.
“Several,” I said.
“How did they end.”
“I’m here,” I said.
The corner of his expression moved. The almost-not-a-smile, the one I had catalogued eleven times now and that was becoming, daily, slightly less almost. “Good,” he said. “Show me.”
*   *   *
He came at me slowly the first time.
Slowly, I understood immediately, not because he was being careful with me but because he was being educational — each movement deliberate, each piece of his approach visible and readable, a demonstration in the form of an attack. He wanted me to see the components. I saw them. I moved left, used his reach rather than fighting it, put distance between us with the minimum motion required, and ended up where I’d intended to end up, which was behind and to his right where the follow-through of his reach made recovery slower.
He turned. He looked at where I was. Something recalibrated in his expression — the smallest adjustment, the thing I had started to think of as the specific look he produced when something had updated his assessment of a situation.
“Again,” he said.
We went again. Speed was where the gap between big and careful and small and precise closed into something workable. I moved, he moved, I moved again, and we circled for thirty seconds with neither of us making contact, both of us reading the other with the focus of two people who were doing this in earnest.
He was reading me. I had none of that on him. The asymmetry was significant and I was not going to close it today and I was going to do my best to make it irrelevant.
I went for the left side.
He was there before I arrived, which I had expected — it was a test, not an attempt, a question about his reflexes rather than a serious approach. His reflex was approximately what I had estimated: extraordinary, but not instantaneous, with a slight preference for the left that I noted and stored.
“You weren’t actually going for that,” he said.
“No,” I agreed pleasantly. “I was finding out how fast you are on that side.”
Another recalibration. Smaller this time, more internal — the adjustment going somewhere I couldn’t read. “And?” he said.
“Fast,” I said. “But the left is faster. Which means the right is the approach.”
He looked at me for a moment. “Most people don’t tell the person they’re sparring with their strategy,” he said.
“Most people aren’t sparring with someone who can read a binding,” I said. “You already know my state. The least I can do is demonstrate that I’m thinking.”
He was quiet for a beat. Then: “Come from the right,” he said. Not permission — a challenge. Come from the right and see.
I came from the right. My shoulder would carry the bruise for a week — the shape of his grip, pressed into muscle by the precision of a hold that had been exactly enough and not a fraction more.
*   *   *
We worked for an hour.
It became clear very quickly that there were several layers to this session and that they were not all the same layer. Layer two was his assessment — systematic, thorough, the process of establishing what I had and what I didn’t with enough precision to know what to develop. I understood both of these layers and was fine with both of them.
Layer three was mine and I was not going to pretend it wasn’t there.
The courtyard had two exits. The ground was the silver forest’s surface — root and earth and the luminescent moss that grew between the trees. It was the first time I had trained on living ground, and the ground had opinions about my stance.
The deep territory was not somewhere I could survive without his protection, which he had made clear, and which I believed — the Hollow outside Nochval had regions that were genuinely dangerous and I had enough respect for the Hollow by now to take the warning seriously. This was information. I collected information reflexively, about exits and options and the mechanisms of available movement, the same way I breathed — not because I was going to use it immediately, but because information was what kept you alive in the specific category of situations where everything had gone wrong simultaneously and you had about four seconds to do something about it.
He knew I was doing this. I was confident he knew. He said nothing about it, which told me something important about him, which was that he understood the difference between a person who was gathering information as a reflex of survival and a person who was actively planning to use it, and he was not going to treat the first as if it were the second.
I respected this more than I was going to say.
We cycled through positions and approaches for an hour. The corrections were efficient. No wasted instruction, no repetition, everything landing in the exact register it needed to land in to be retained. He had taught before. He was good at it.
He was also, quite clearly, not trying.
Not in the way of someone condescending — he was fully present, fully engaged, his attention on me with the complete quality it had on everything. But he was operating at a fraction of what was available to him, which I had established in the first ten minutes and which was simply the truth of the gap between us. He could end this session at any point, from any position I took, in less time than it would take me to register that he had moved.
I knew this. He knew I knew this. The knowing shared between us without being stated was one of the many things we had apparently decided to share without stating, and I had filed it in the place I kept those.
I was going to get him once. Not through superior skill — I did not have superior skill and I was not going to pretend otherwise. Through information. Through the slight right-side lag I had identified and confirmed across three separate approaches, through the specific shift in his weight that preceded his right-side reach, which I had been reading and reading and reading across an hour of careful attention until I had the shape of it the way I had the shape of Drev’s expression when he was deciding which lie to try next.
He was not Drev. The comparison was absurd. But weight shifts were weight shifts and tells were tells and the part of my brain that read people had been reading him for an hour and it had something.
I waited for the right moment — the moment he shifted his weight to the right in preparation for the reach I’d been provoking him toward for the past five minutes by making his left side look like the better option. The weight shifted. I was already moving when it did — not toward the reach, not the obvious counter, but low and inside, inside his reach before it extended, into the space between his arm and his body that became briefly available in the moment of commitment.
My shoulder connected with his side.
It was not elegant. What it did was make contact, genuine contact, the kind that in a real situation would have followed with something more consequential. I had gotten inside his reach. I had read his tell and used it and I had gotten inside.
He went still.
A full stop — not the practiced stillness of his default state but something sharper, the involuntary kind, the kind that happened when something had occurred that a body needed a moment to register. It lasted perhaps one second.
Then his arm came around.
*   *   *
It happened faster than I could track — not the educational slow of the first approach, not the careful calibrated speed of the past hour, but something closer to what he actually was. One moment I was inside his reach with my shoulder against his side, and the next moment I was not. There was a brief, vertiginous sense of having my momentum redirected — cleanly, with a precision that was almost considerate in its efficiency, nothing rough about it — and then the dark earth of the courtyard was at my back and his forearm was across my collarbone and the rest of the geometry of the situation had resolved in his favor with a completeness that left very little to be unclear about.
I was pinned. Comprehensively. His forearm across my collarbone, his weight distributed with the specific economy of someone who knew exactly how much pressure to apply to be effective without being excessive, and his face was close — the proximity of it something I had not encountered before, this particular close, the silver eyes in the Hollow’s mid-morning light finding mine with the focused attention of his full presence rather than the managed distance he maintained across a room.
He said nothing.
I was not in pain. He had been completely precise about that — not a bruise, not a strain, nothing that would matter afterward except in the most relevant sense, which was that I had lost and he had demonstrated it. But I was very completely not going anywhere; the point, and I was aware of this the way you were aware of a fire in a room: the primary fact in the register, everything else secondary.
“That was good,” he said.
His voice at this proximity was different from across a room. Not louder — never louder, he did not operate in louder, volume was not a variable he seemed to use. Closer. More present in a way that was purely physical, the way all sounds were more present close up, because the distance the sound had to travel had become negligible.
I became aware that I was not breathing with ideal efficiency.
“The tell,” I said. My own voice was even. I was very pleased about this. “The weight shift before the right reach.”
“You read it for forty minutes before you used it,” he said.
“I wanted to be certain.”
“You were certain after twenty,” he said.
I stared at him. He was correct. I had been certain after twenty and I had kept reading it anyway, running the same assessment repeatedly, confirming what I already knew. “How did you know that,” I said.
“You stopped testing the left after twenty minutes,” he said. “You only went back to it to establish the contrast.” A pause, and in the pause, something that I was almost entirely certain was not nothing. “You’re thorough,” he said. Not a criticism. Something in the precise opposite direction, stated with the flatness he applied to all genuine assessments.
The forearm across my collarbone had not moved.
I was aware of the weight of it — not heavy, not uncomfortable, the specific awareness of something in contact with the skin at a location that was not a location I had many things in contact with. His arm was cold. Not the ambient cold of the Hollow, not the cold of stone or air — the cold of him specifically, the temperature that was his own and that I had known abstractly from the theory of what Vorath were and that was now immediately, specifically, undeniably present against my collarbone and the side of my throat.
It was very cold.
I had not expected it to be cold the way it was cold. This was not that. This was its own cold, internal, the cold of something that produced its own temperature the way a fire produced its own heat, and the cold of it was — I was going to find a word for it that was not the word I was currently reaching for, and I was going to find it shortly, and in the meantime I was going to focus very carefully on the conversation.
“The gate,” he said. “North wall. You’ve looked at it four times.”
“Five,” I said. “You missed the second one.”
He looked at me. I looked at him. We were extremely close and we were looking at each other, and his forearm was against my collarbone, and I had just told him I had been counting my exit assessments and corrected his count, and neither of us was going to address any of this directly.
“The bar on the north gate takes four seconds to lift from the inside,” he said. “You’d calculated two.”
“The latch is weighted,” I said. “I didn’t account for the weight.”
“It’s weighted to keep it from rattling in the Hollow’s atmospheric shifts,” he said. “It was not designed as a security feature. If you gripped it here—” He described a position with specificity that indicated he had thought about this. He had thought about this. He had watched me look at the north gate and worked out my calculation and identified its flaw and was now correcting it — not a thing I had expected and that I had no adequate response to. “—you could do it in two seconds.”
I looked at him for a long moment. He looked back.
“You’re teaching me how to escape faster,” I said.
“I’m teaching you accurate information about your environment,” he said. “What you do with it is yours.”
The cold of his forearm was against my skin and his face was close and he was teaching me how to lift the latch on the gate more efficiently and I was making a decision about what this all meant that I was going to need considerably more time to make.
“I think we’re done for today,” I said.
He stepped back. He extended a hand. I looked at it. I took it, and he pulled me upright with exactly the force required to do that and no additional force whatsoever, which was another thing I was filing.
“Tomorrow,” he said. “Same time.”
“Tomorrow,” I agreed.
He went to retrieve his outer layers from the wall where he’d left them. The coat smelled like the Hollow and woodsmoke and something underneath that I had still not categorized to my satisfaction. I pulled it close.
He passed me on the way out of the courtyard. He did not look at me. He looked at the north gate, briefly, and then at me, with the specific look of someone confirming that information had been received. I held his gaze.
“The tell’s gone now,” I said. “You’ll have corrected for it.”
“Yes,” he said.
“So I’ll have to find a different one.”
The corner of his expression moved. Smaller than usual — almost nothing. But the almost-nothing was becoming, I had established over two weeks of observation, one of the most reliable indicators of what was happening behind the controlled surface. “Yes,” he said. “You will.”
He left the courtyard. I stayed for a moment longer, looking at the north gate, at the latch, at the position he had described for lifting it most efficiently. Then I looked at my hand — the one he had pulled upright, the one that had briefly been in his.
Cold. Still. My hand was still cold, faintly, the ghost of it — not cold the way the air was cold but cold with the register of his cold, the internal cold, the temperature that was his own. I had held his hand for approximately two seconds and the cold of it was still in my palm and the side of my fingers.
I closed my hand.
I stood in the north courtyard with my hand closed and the coat around me and the Hollow’s mid-morning sky above, and I looked at the north gate, and I thought about accurate information, and I thought about a person who taught you how to escape more efficiently and called it what you do with it is yours, and I thought about the cold of a forearm against my collarbone that I was fairly sure I was going to be thinking about for the rest of the day.
I went inside.
I went to the library, where Sable was in my chair.
She looked up when I came in. She looked at me. She looked at my hand, which I was still holding slightly closed. Her membrane-colored eyes did a small, complete circuit of available information and arrived at a conclusion with the speed of something that had been paying attention to context for longer than most things had existed.
She said nothing.
She turned back to her book.
This was, I was beginning to understand, one of the most valuable things about Sable: she knew the exact moments that required silence the way she knew the exact moments that required the placed observation. She had calibrated this over more centuries than I had been alive, and the calibration was, in its way, a form of care.
I sat in the right-side chair.
I picked up my notes.
I read the same paragraph four times without retaining it.
The cold lasted in my collarbone and the side of my hand for an hour. I tracked it precisely, with the attention I applied to information I intended to use, and when it faded I noted the fading with the same precision, and I sat with the fact that I had tracked it and noted it and that tracking and noting was the thing I did with information I intended to use, and I thought about what it meant that I was treating the ghost of his cold on my skin as information I intended to use.
I turned to a fresh page in my notes.
At the top, because I was not going to lie to myself in my own notes, I wrote:
He taught me how to escape faster.
And below it, after a moment, in the small marginal handwriting:
I did not think about escaping for the entire last half of the session.
I looked at this for a while. Sable turned a page. The shadow-fox, which had taken up its position on the east courtyard wall again at some point, was visible through the library’s narrow windows — the cold-light quality of it patient and attending in the way it always attended.
Tomorrow, he had said. Same time.
I wrote, below the two lines, so small it was barely legible:
Good.
Outside the narrow windows, the silver trees leaned again, as if the realm itself approved.
And in the courtyard, the shadow-fox made its quiet vibration — the first time it had done that inside the Hall’s boundaries. The creature’s range had contracted in recent months — it used to go to the territory’s eastern edge. Now it stayed within the inner ring, the brighter trees. It had moved inward the way a person moved away from a drafty window.
The first time. Not the last.


Chapter Eleven: Context, Foundation Pieces, the Structural Ones
Kira
I knew someone was coming before she arrived, because the Hall told me.
Not in words — the Hall communicated in the impressions it had always used, the warmth and weight and quality of attention that I had been reading for three weeks now and was getting better at. What the Hall produced this particular morning was anticipation. The particular feel of a building that knew a thing was about to happen and had strong feelings about it. It was the warmest I had felt the Hall since Sable’s first visit, which was saying something, and it was additionally mixed with a quality I had not felt before — something almost anxious, if buildings could be anxious, the quality of a host that wanted things to go well.
I mentioned this to Sable over breakfast.
Sable looked up from her food with the expression she used when she had information relevant to the current conversation and was deciding how much of it to provide. She had many expressions in this family — they were among the most common ones she produced — and I had developed enough fluency to identify which end of the information-provision spectrum each one sat at. This one was: I have quite a lot of information and I’m going to give you the minimum useful amount.
“Vasilisa is coming today,” she said.
I set down my cup. “She’s been to the grounds before. The Hall wasn’t like this.”
“The Hall knew you were in the library then and was weighing its priorities,” Sable said, in the serene tone she used when she was being entirely accurate about something slightly alarming. “Now you’re at breakfast. She’ll be at the entrance within the hour.”
“Should I be prepared for something,” I said.
Sable considered this with genuine thought. “Vasilisa is warm,” she said. “Genuinely so — she’s not performing it.” She picked up her cup. “She’ll like you. She already likes you. The question is whether you’ll notice that she’s also gathering information the entire time.”
“Can I do both,” I said. “Let her gather information and gather some of my own.”
Sable looked at me over her cup with both membrane-colored eyes. “Yes,” she said. “That’s the right instinct. Just — be aware that she’s very good at it. She’s the Vorath of Memory. She has been paying attention to things for longer than most things have been worth paying attention to.”
“So have you,” I said.
“I’m not Memory,” she said. “I’m just old.” She set down her cup and stood, gathering nothing as she always gathered nothing, existing as a thing that required only itself. “I’ll leave you to it. This particular conversation will go better without me in it.”
“You’re abandoning me to an ancient information-gathering Vorath,” I said.
“I’m giving you an unmediated introduction,” she said, “to the person who knows him best.” She was already moving toward the door. “You’ll be fine. You are also, for what it’s worth, very good at it.”
She left. The main hall settled. I finished my breakfast, straightened the table with the efficiency of someone who had grown up in small rooms where disorder was a liability, and went to wait.
The Hall’s anticipation did not settle.
I found this, if anything, more endearing than alarming. An eight-hundred-year-old building was nervous about a visit going well. I understood the feeling.
I made a decision: I would tell her tonight. Not everything — not yet. But enough to change the terms between us. The cost of delay had exceeded the cost of disclosure.
*   *   *
Vasilisa arrived exactly when Sable had predicted, which I was beginning to understand was simply the relationship between Sable and time: she stated things about it with the accuracy of someone who experienced it differently from anyone else.
I was in the main hall when the binding shifted — not Aleksei’s signature, which I would have recognized immediately, but something adjacent to it, the particular quality of Vorath presence that I was learning to identify in the way you learned to identify individual instruments in an orchestra. Same family, different instrument. This one had a warmth that his did not, a brightness in the register, something that arrived before she was physically in the room the way very vivid personalities arrived before the person themselves did.
Then she was in the doorway, and I understood immediately why the Hall had been anxious, because Vasilisa was the kind of person who made spaces feel smaller and more alive simultaneously just by entering them.
She was wearing approximately seventeen layers of something in the color of deep water — a blue that cycled into green at the edges, iridescent in the Hall’s ambient light. Her hair was loose and very long and the silver-white of a Hollow sky at its deepest point. Her eyes were different colors: one pale grey and one the warm amber of old resin, and both of them found me before she had finished crossing the threshold, with the feel of a very precise instrument taking a measurement.
She covered the space between the door and where I was standing in a way that was not quite walking — too fluid, too easy, the motion of something that had been moving through spaces for so long that it had arrived at pure efficiency. She stopped in front of me. She was, up close, slightly taller than she appeared at a distance — a quality she shared with Aleksei — the way of beings whose physical presence had a weight to it that made the distance between you feel different from the reality.
She looked at me for a moment. I looked back.
Then she smiled, and the measurement quality vanished, replaced by something so genuinely warm that the transition was almost startling. Not performed — the smile arrived the way real smiles arrived, in the face rather than on it, changing the whole configuration. “You looked right back,” she said. “Good.”
“Was I supposed to look away,” I said.
“Most people do,” she said. “With Vorath at close range. The presence is uncomfortable for mortals — the age of us presses, and the instinct is to look at something else.” She was still smiling, the warmth of it genuine and assessing and completely simultaneous. “You held it. You did in the alley too. He mentioned it.”
I filed this in several places at once. “He mentioned it,” I said, keeping my voice at its usual register.
“He mentions things about you,” she said, with the serenity of someone dropping a stone into still water and watching the rings. “I’m Vasilisa. I suspect you know that.”
“I do,” I said. “I’m Kira.”
“I know,” she said. “Shall we sit? I’ve been traveling and I prefer chairs to the alternative.”
We sat. The Hall’s presences, which had been at their most attentive since I’d come downstairs, produced a warmth that was the feel of something that had been anxious and was now, with cautious relief, beginning to feel that things were going well.
*   *   *
Vasilisa was warm. Genuinely warm — she asked questions and waited for answers with the patience of someone who cared about the responses. She was the first Vorath I had met who produced warmth as a natural feature rather than a controlled display.
“Sixty marks,” she said. “He has been telling that story to anyone who will listen with an expression on his face that he probably doesn’t know is on his face.”
I looked at her. “What expression,” I said, very carefully.
She looked back with both mismatched eyes and the specific warmth of someone who had decided, in this moment, to be generous. “—worthwhile,” she said. “He doesn’t surprise easily. A thousand years is a long time to run out of surprises. You’ve produced several in three weeks, which is a rate I don’t think I can find a comparison for.”
I held her gaze. She held mine. The information was sitting on the table between us like the fox on the windowsill — placed, intentional, positioned where I would see it. I was not going to pick it up immediately. “You’ve known him a long time,” I said.
“Since before the Hall was built,” she said. “Since before this part of the Hollow had a name.” The warmth in her expression adjusted — not dimming, becoming more layered. “He is — it takes time to understand him. The surface is very consistent, and it covers a great deal, and most people read the surface and form a complete picture from it and are wrong about almost everything.” She looked at the fire, which the Hall had lit with the specific courtesy of an eight-hundred-year-old building that wanted a visit to go well. “He has been at Nochval every day since the Hunt,” she said.
I kept my expression even. “Is that unusual,” I said.
“He visits Nochval twice a month,” she said. “Normally. The Hall is his primary residence but the territory manages itself well enough that he can — he has other things. Other places. Responsibilities that take him elsewhere.” She looked back at me from the fire. “In three weeks he has not left Nochval’s boundary once.”
I said nothing. The information was in a very specific part of my awareness now, the part I reserved for things that were going to require significant attention later and that I could not give significant attention to in the current moment.
“He doesn’t leave when something requires his presence,” she said, gently, in the tone of someone clarifying something they thought might be misread. “He’s not — it’s not obligation. He would tell you, if you asked him, that there are things here requiring attention. He would be telling the truth. There are always things requiring attention.” A pause. “He would not tell you that the things requiring attention have been requiring approximately the same level of attention they require at all times and that he has simply decided his attention belongs here.” She smiled, and the smile was very kind. “He might not know that himself yet.”
I looked at the table. At the grain of it, the eight-hundred-year-old dark wood that had been sat at by anyone who had ever been in this Hall, pressed smooth by use. “You’re telling me things,” I said.
“I am,” she agreed.
“Deliberately.”
“Very.”
“Why,” I said.
She considered the question with the weight it deserved. “Because he won’t,” she said. “But there are things that don’t require the right words. Things that are just — facts. Context. The kind of information that makes other information make sense.” She looked at me steadily. “You are someone who works from information. I could see it in the first two minutes. You build from what you know outward to what you don’t, and you’re patient about the building, but you need the foundation pieces in place first or the structure doesn’t hold.”
I looked at her. She was accurate. She was very specifically accurate, in the way of someone who had made a rapid and thorough assessment and was choosing to show you the results rather than conceal them — a choice not everyone made and that I respected when I encountered it. “All right,” I said. “What else.”
Something in Vasilisa’s expression became pleased — not brightly, the way she had been pleased when she laughed about the sixty marks, but quietly, in the way of someone who had correctly estimated a person and was satisfied to be correct.
*   *   *
She told me, in pieces, over the next hour.
The binding had never activated before. Every time. Without exception. For her, it had not settled. She was watching him — she said this without apology, as a simple fact about what she did — and the not-settling was visible in the way of things that did not behave as expected, and she had been watching it not-settle for three weeks with the particular attention of someone who was seeing something she had not seen before and intended to understand it.
“What does it look like from the outside,” I said. “The binding. When it doesn’t settle.”
She thought about this. “It looks like someone learning the specific weight of a new constant,” she said. “He’s in the natural phase now. He knows where you are the way he knows where the Hall is.” A pause. “He has always known where the Hall is.”
The mortal who had last stayed at Nochval — three hundred years ago, the one Aleksei had mentioned on the first night — had been a child. The Hall had accommodated the child the way Halls accommodated things they were asked to accommodate. The binding had not activated. The child had gone home. The Hall had not been warm in that specific way again for three centuries.
“The Hall was different before she came,” Vasilisa said — she meant me, I understood, the she was me — “in a way I would not have predicted and that I’m not sure he predicted either. Buildings accumulate the quality of what happens in them. Three hundred years of — not emptiness. He was here. But three hundred years of one person is its own kind of quiet.” She looked at the upper reaches of the main hall, where the presences were doing their warmest and most attentive thing. “They were quiet for a very long time,” she said. “They are not quiet now.”
I thought about the morning I had introduced myself to the presences. About saying I was going to be here for a year and meaning it as practical information and hearing the warmth of them shift in response. “They were waiting,” I said. It was not quite a question.
“I think so,” Vasilisa said. “For something, anyway. Things that are old enough become patient. It’s not a choice — it’s what time does to things that survive it.” She looked at me. “You’ll understand eventually. Or something like understanding.”
She told me about the Hunt — not the law, which I knew, but what the Hunt was for him. The forty-seven times. The maintenance work, the membrane management, the administrative reality of it that the stories never mentioned. “He does it because it requires doing and it is his to do and he is — he does not leave things that are his to do undone. But he has never loved the Hunt the way some of the others do. He would be happier,” she considered, “sitting in the library.”
I thought about the north study adjacent to the library corridor. I did not say this. “He doesn’t sit,” I said instead.
“He doesn’t sit with other people present,” she said. “He sits alone. In the library, late, when the Hall is in its night configuration and there’s no one to maintain a particular quality of presence for.” She looked at me. “Or he did. I imagine the habit has changed somewhat.”
She said this last part with a very deliberate evenness and immediately moved on to something else, and I filed it in the same place I was filing everything else and thought about my list and what reorganizing it would look like and whether I needed a second document at this point.
I probably needed a second document.
*   *   *
We had been talking for an hour when Aleksei appeared.
He did not appear in the way of a person who had been approaching and arrived — he appeared in the way of a person who had been somewhere specific and had decided to stop being there and be in the main hall instead. One moment the binding told me north study, and the next moment the main hall doorway had him in it — a distance of three corridors and two flights of stairs and I had not heard a single footstep.
He looked at Vasilisa. He looked at me. He looked at the table between us, which had the feel of a table at which two people had been having a conversation for a significant amount of time, and something moved through his expression that was fast and controlled and that I had enough vocabulary by now to identify as a very specific kind of wariness.
“Aleksei,” Vasilisa said, in the bright tone of someone extremely satisfied with events.
“Vasilisa,” he said. In the tone of someone who had been in the north study adjacent to the library corridor and had felt his guest’s presence in the main hall for an hour and had been deciding whether to come down and had decided to come down and was now mildly regretting the decision while also not regretting it.
“We’ve been talking,” she said. “Kira is excellent company.”
“I know,” he said. He said this the way he said things that were simply facts — without inflection, without apparent awareness of how they landed. Then he looked at me and the wariness was still there underneath the flat surface but it had acquired a different quality, a question underneath the wariness. I held his gaze.
“She’s been telling me things,” I said.
“Has she,” he said. He looked at Vasilisa.
“Context,” Vasilisa said pleasantly. “Foundation pieces. The structural ones.” She stood, layers settling around her with the ease of someone accustomed to a great deal of fabric and at peace with it. “I should go. I have things.” She said this in the way of someone for whom things was a category covering a significant portion of the known world. She turned to me, and the warmth of her was direct and genuine and I felt it the way I felt the Hall’s warmth: as something real. “It was very good to meet you properly,” she said. “I’ll come back.”
“I didn’t invite you,” I said.
She looked at me. Then she looked at Aleksei. Something passed between them — a communication without words, the specific register of two people who had known each other for a thousand years and had developed a vocabulary for it that did not require language. Then she looked back at me and the real smile arrived, the one that changed her whole face. “No,” she said. “You didn’t.”
She left. The Hall warmed, and then settled into the particular quality it had after a significant visit — not the anxious anticipation of before, not the careful attention of during, but the specific warmth of something that had gone well.
Aleksei was still in the doorway.
“Sit down,” I said. “If you’re going to stand in the doorway looking like that, at least come in and do it at the table.”
He came in. He did not sit — he never sat, I had catalogued this, the standing was not going to change — but he came to the table and stood adjacent to the chair across from me, which was close enough. “What did she tell you,” he said.
“Things,” I said. “Context. Foundation pieces.”
He looked at me steadily. “Specifically.”
“That the binding has never activated before,” I said. “That you’ve been at Nochval every day since the Hunt.” I watched his face. He was very controlled and the control was absolute and it told me nothing directly. “That you normally visit twice a month.”
He said nothing.
“She didn’t tell me anything I wasn’t going to figure out eventually,” I said. “I want you to know that. She gave me the timeline, not the conclusion. The conclusion I’m drawing myself.”
A pause. The particular feel of one. “And what conclusion are you drawing,” he said.
I looked at him — at the silver eyes in the main hall’s fire-warm light, at the controlled surface covering the great deal that it covered, at the person who had made a coat with his own hands and given it away on the third day and taught me how to escape faster and stood outside the library for two weeks without coming in and had been here every day since a Hunt that happened once a decade and that he found tedious.
“I’m still drawing it,” I said. “I’ll let you know when I’m done.”
He looked at me for a long moment. Something in the controlled surface shifted — very slightly, in the specific way of things that were very controlled and had encountered something they had not fully prepared for. “All right,” he said.
“Vasilisa likes you,” I said. “In case you wanted to know what my impression was.”
“I’m aware she likes me,” he said, in the tone of someone for whom this was an old and established fact. “She is also frequently inconvenient.”
“She said the same thing about you,” I said.
He went very still. “She did not say that.”
“She implied it very thoroughly,” I said.
The corner of his expression moved. The almost-not-a-smile, clearer than it had been yesterday, clearer every day, the thing that kept deciding to be more definite by increments I was tracking. “She always does,” he said. A pause. “Are you all right.”
The question landed with a gentleness that was not his usual register and that I was not expecting. Not the binding-check quality of how are you as functional assessment. Something more direct. Something that was asking whether the hour of information had been too much rather than too little.
“Yes,” I said. And then, because he had asked: “It was a lot. It’s useful and it was a lot.”
He nodded. Not a dismissal — receipt. He had heard this and registered it. “The sparring is in an hour,” he said. “If you’d rather—”
“I’ll be there,” I said.
A small pause. “Good,” he said. The word had the feel of a thing meant simply, without layering. He moved toward the door. At the threshold he stopped, the way he stopped sometimes, as if a door’s frame was a place where additional things could be said that were harder to say in the middle of a room.
“Vasilisa,” he said, “tends to present information in the arrangement she finds most effective rather than the arrangement that is most complete.” He said this to the doorframe. “If something she told you doesn’t fit with something you know directly — ask me.”
“I will,” I said.
He left.
I sat at the table for a while in the Hall that was warm and quiet with its particular post-visit quality, and I thought about everything Vasilisa had placed and everything Aleksei had just offered — the specific gift of ask me — the most open he had been since the night of the fox question, and which landed in the same careful place I kept the right direction and the translated book.
Then I went upstairs to get ready for sparring, and on the way I stopped in my room and stood at the windowsill and looked at the fox, and I thought about what Vasilisa had not said — had specifically not said, which she was as deliberate about as what she did say — and I looked at the room around me with the fresh eyes of new information.
The blankets, the exactly-right weight of them. The practical paper, the quantity of it, the specific grade. The fire that first night, built to exactly the right size. The food in the cabinet, sourced from Zemya with the specific knowledge of what I would want, before I had agreed to stay.
She had told me the binding gave him her general wellbeing. General was the word. General was not enough to produce, from scratch, the room I had walked into on the first night and recognized — in the quiet way that you recognized something not seen before — as almost exactly the room I would have chosen.
Almost was the word. Almost exactly. Not quite perfect. Research rather than knowing.
He had researched me. With whatever resources an ancient being in a living realm had available to him, in the window between catching me in an alley and walking me to a room, he had researched what I would need and built the closest approximation he could manage.
I sat on the edge of my bed and looked at the room and the fox and the window with the silver forest beyond it and the shadow-fox at the treeline where it was always at the treeline, and I thought about that window of time — the alley, the corridor, the room — and what it meant to fill that window with research rather than assumption.
Most people assumed. You put a person in a room and you gave them what you thought a person needed and you did not particularly verify whether the person was the kind of person who thought by writing or who slept best in heavy blankets or who would want a window that faced the silver forest rather than away from it.
He had verified. In the space of a few hours, in the immediate aftermath of the Hunt, with the binding fresh and the law acknowledged and nothing settled — he had verified.
I looked at the fox. The fox attended to its middle distance with its composed, deliberate expression.
“Yes,” I said to the fox. “I know. I’m thinking about it.”
I was going to be thinking about it for a very long time.
I picked up the coat from the chair and put it on and went to the north courtyard to be pinned in the dark earth and to find a new tell, because that was what was happening in an hour and I was going to be there on time and I was going to find the tell and I was going to hold the fact of the researched room in the specific place I held things I was not yet ready to bring into full view.
Sable found me as I was leaving the east courtyard. She did not speak immediately — she walked beside me for several steps. She was deciding how to say whatever she was going to say.
“Word has reached the other territories,” she said finally. “About the binding’s behavior. About you.”
I kept walking. “From Zora.”
“From Zora. From Radovan. From anyone who has reason to pay attention to what happens in Nochval.” She paused. “They are asking questions. About what you are. About why the Hollow responds to you. About what restoration means for the territorial balance.”
“And what are they concluding,” I said.
“They are not concluding yet,” Sable said. “They are watching. But watching has a way of turning into opinions, and opinions have a way of turning into actions.” She looked at me with the steady mismatched eyes. “You should know that not all of the watching is friendly.”
I stopped at the Hall’s entrance. “How unfriendly.”
“The kind that sends representatives to convenings,” Sable said. “Eventually.”
She went inside. I stood at the threshold for a moment, looking at the silver forest and the Hollow’s cycling sky and the shadow-fox at the treeline, and I thought about the fact that every mark I received, every moment I spent in training, every interaction I had with the Hall was being reported somewhere.
Watching had a way of turning into opinions.
And I was being watched by beings who had been alive for centuries and who did not, apparently, welcome change.
The shadow-fox watched me cross the grounds from the treeline, ears forward, with the patient attending quality of something that had seen this coming and was very satisfied to be seeing it.
I did not blame it. I was starting to see it coming too.
*   *   *
The east corridor door was cold.
Not Hollow-cold. Intent-cold — the cold of something with purpose behind it. The handle burned my palm with frost when I touched it, and the skin went white in the shape of my grip.
Behind me, the corridor had gone dark. Not sky-dark. Off-dark. The luminescence in the stone walls — that ancient trapped light — was simply gone. And at the far end, where the window room looked onto the oldest trees, something was standing that had not been standing there before.
Tall. Narrow. Still in the way of things that did not need to breathe.
The Claim recognized it. And the Claim was afraid.
I did not run. I moved backward, one step, two, keeping my eyes on the shape. It did not advance.
Then the Hall’s presences surged — a wall of heat, fierce and sudden, slamming into the corridor. The dark broke. The luminescence returned. The shape was gone.
Aleksei arrived ninety seconds later. He looked at the corridor, then at me, then at my hand.
“Something was in the corridor,” I said. “Tall. Still. The dark came with it. The Hall drove it out.”
“The east corridor has not been breached in eight hundred years.”
“It was breached tonight.”
He took my hand — slowly, carefully. His cold on my cold. For a moment the two colds met and I couldn’t tell which was his and which was the corridor’s. Then the corridor’s cold broke and his remained, and the difference was the difference between a thing that wanted to harm you and a thing that did not.
“A sending,” he said quietly. “A constructed awareness, shaped to intimidate. It could not have harmed you. But it was not meant to harm you. It was meant to show you that you are reachable.”
They were testing our defenses. And the test had worked.
And that, I realized as I reached the Hall’s door, was the problem.
The foundation concepts were nearly complete. Four marks received, each one building on the last, each one preparing me for something. The fifth mark was coming soon — Aleksei had said as much. And after the fifth, the sixth, and seventh, until whatever I was being prepared for arrived.
Seeing it coming meant understanding that the preparation had a purpose. That the purpose was not gentle. That the marks were foundation because what came after would require a foundation to survive.
I had been focused on learning. I should have been focused on what the learning was doing to me. Because reaching for the Hall’s door, I noticed that my hand — the one that had received four marks — was colder than the other.
Not his cold. Not the Hollow’s.
Mine. Something that was now mine. Four marks in, and something was already different about what I was.


Chapter Twelve: The Cold Lives in the Skin
Kira
I had been wrong about sparring.
When he said training, I had understood sparring — the kind of physical engagement I was already familiar with, two people in a space working through an understood vocabulary of movement and counter-movement, which I had been doing since I was sixteen and had opinions about. What he had said was training, and what training actually meant, I was discovering three weeks into it, was considerably more than what I had understood.
Sparring was a component. But the training for the deep Hollow was also an excuse — proximity with a functional justification, contact with a clear purpose. Both things were true. The training was real. The other thing was also real.
All of this involved him.
