Present, close, teaching with the same completeness and efficiency he brought to everything, and the training had become — over three weeks of daily sessions in the north courtyard that had sometimes extended into the lower territory — the part of the day I was structuring everything else around.
I was not going to examine this directly. I was going to note it and move on.
What I was also noting, and also moving on from with varying degrees of success, was the physical contact.
He had not touched me outside of sparring before Chapter Ten — the pin on the dark earth, and the hand pulled upright after, and the cold of both that had lasted an hour. Those had been incidental. Combat-adjacent. The kind of contact that had a clear and functional explanation that did not require any other examination.
The training contact was different. Not in kind — it was entirely functional, entirely purposeful, always brief — but in frequency and in specificity. He corrected. When my stance was wrong he corrected it. When my grip on a position was wrong he corrected it. When my weight distribution for the deep Hollow’s ground was wrong he corrected it, and the corrections were always exactly as brief as they needed to be and not a moment longer — a precision I had catalogued across three weeks and that was doing something to my ability to treat it as ordinary.
The cold lived in the skin.
That was the thing. Not for two seconds, not for a minute. It lived there, in the skin, in the specific locations of contact, for the full duration of each training session and for a significant portion of the time after.
I had been tracking it. Left wrist, inner surface: approximately forty-five minutes. The back of my right hand when he repositioned my grip: an hour, sometimes longer. The point between my shoulder blades where he had adjusted my posture for the Hollow-walking position twice and where I had subsequently maintained perfect posture without being asked and without admitting why: still present, faintly, when I went to sleep.
I was not going to write this in my notes.
I was absolutely going to write this in my notes.
*   *   *
The session that particular morning began the way they had all begun since the first week: him in the courtyard when I arrived, already there, which I had never been able to catch him arriving at and had stopped trying to, in the center of the dark earth with his hands at his sides and the particular quality of his stillness that I had now spent four weeks watching and that I was, at this point, almost able to read as well as his almost-not-a-smile.
Almost. He was still significantly ahead of me in terms of illegibility and I was not going to pretend otherwise.
“Today is the ground work,” he said.
“I know,” I said. He had told me yesterday. The deep Hollow’s ground work — not combat, not navigation, but the specific physical relationship between a body and ground that did not behave like ground. He had been building toward this for three weeks, in the specific way of someone who taught by constructing foundations before introducing the thing that required them, and I had understood this was coming and had been paying attention to the foundations accordingly.
“You’ll have questions,” he said.
“I always have questions,” I said.
“These will be different questions.” He looked at me with the silver eyes that caught the Hollow’s mid-morning light and held it. “The deep Hollow’s ground is alive in the way that the Hollow itself is alive — not passive terrain. It responds to what moves over it. Your body has to learn to move in a way that the ground reads as belonging rather than intruding.”
I thought about the book in the first language and the mark that had said beginning and the Hollow’s general tendency to treat me as something it recognized. “And if it already reads me as belonging?”
Something happened in his expression — the specific recalibration he produced when something had updated his assessment, faster and smaller than it used to be because he was getting better at containing it or because I was getting better at reading the contained version. “Then it will be easier,” he said. “But we train for the version where it isn’t.”
“Because I should be able to do it regardless of whether the Hollow cooperates,” I said.
“Yes.”
“That is a very sensible approach to training,” I said.
“I’ve had some time to refine it,” he said, in the dry way that I was increasingly certain was his specific register for things that were technically jokes, and that I had decided to receive as jokes regardless of whether he intended them as such, because the alternative was treating an ancient being’s dry observations about a thousand years of experience as straight statements and that seemed like a waste.
I did not laugh. I produced a sound that was adjacent to a laugh and then looked at the opposite wall and said: “Show me.”
The corner of his expression moved.
*   *   *
The ground work required a specific stance.
He demonstrated it first — that was always how he taught, demonstration before instruction, showing the full thing before breaking it into components — and watching him in the stance was its own category of experience, the way watching something very old doing something it had done many times was its own category. The stance was low and centered, weight distributed differently from combat stance, the relationship between the feet and the ground deliberate in a way that was not bracing but settling. Like the ground was a conversation partner and you were indicating you intended to participate.
I tried it.
“Left foot,” he said.
I adjusted.
“Further,” he said. “The weight needs to—” He stopped. He crossed to where I was standing and crouched beside me, which brought him to approximately the level of my knee, and he placed both hands around my left ankle and moved my foot to the exact position it needed to be in.
His hands were cold.
I knew they were cold. I had known for four weeks that they were cold, this was established, documented, the data was complete. The cold of his hands around my ankle, brief and purposeful, repositioning my foot with an efficiency that required approximately three seconds of contact before he stood and stepped back.
Three seconds.
The cold lived in my ankle.
“Better,” he said, from behind me now, assessing the stance from the angle that showed the weight distribution. “Your right shoulder is compensating. Drop it.”
I dropped it.
“You’re anticipating the correction,” he said. “You’ve been doing that since the second week. When you hear the shoulder is wrong you correct before I finish the sentence.”
“Is that a problem,” I said.
“No,” he said. “It means you’re learning to feel the wrong before it’s named. That’s what I’m training toward.” A pause. He moved around me, the slow circuit he made when he was looking at all angles of a position, and I tracked him through the binding — the directional sense that had become as natural as any other sense, no longer effortful, just present. He stopped at my right side. “Here,” he said, and his hand came to my right arm, just above the elbow, repositioning the angle.
Three seconds. Exactly. The cold came in and settled and he moved away and I was in the correct position and the cold remained, specific and precise, in the exact location of contact.
“Hold it,” he said.
We worked through the stance for twenty minutes before he was satisfied, which was approximately the time it took for me to stop thinking about the ankle and the arm and simply inhabit the position correctly. The mind understood things immediately and stored them abstractly. The body needed repetition and sensation and the feel of being shown rather than told.
He was teaching my body. This was a thing I was going to hold at arm’s length. My legs were shaking from the sustained precision the stance demanded. intellectually and simply execute practically, because the alternative was not going to help anyone.
*   *   *
The second part of the session was movement across variable ground, which the north courtyard simulated imperfectly but adequately. He had brought in material from the lower territory — dark, dense patches of deep-Hollow ground that sat in the courtyard’s corners like displaced landscape, which was exactly what they were — and the training involved moving across them in the stance while he made corrections.
The corrections were, again, physical.
I was aware of this in advance. I had been aware of it in advance for the full twenty minutes of the stance work, in the specific anticipatory way of knowing something is coming and having complicated feelings about the knowing. The functional layer was learning to move across ground that was alive and that I needed to be able to navigate.
He was precise about the corrections. He was completely aware of every correction. He knew exactly where his hands were and exactly how long they needed to be there and he did not extend a single contact beyond what was necessary. Brief and purposeful, always exactly that, never more.
The never more was doing something.
Not the presence of contact — the boundary of it. The contact was always exactly enough and never more, and that precision was not restraint. It was calibration.
His hands were always where they were for a reason.
This was — I needed a word that was not the word I kept finding. It was significant. It was information. It was going in the notes.
I was crossing the third patch of deep-Hollow ground in the correct stance, with the weight settled the way he had shown me — the conversation-partner quality, the settling rather than the bracing — when he said “stop” and I stopped. He came around to my right side. “The stance is holding but your centre is drifting right on this ground. It’s the density variation — this patch has a heavier concentration here.” “There.”
There.
The cold came in through my ribs. Not through the fabric — through it, in the way his cold moved through things, the internal temperature of him that did not require direct skin contact to register. I had discovered this in the second week and had subsequently been very calm about it and was being very calm about it now, because calmness was available to me as a choice and I was choosing it.
“Feel the difference,” he said.
I felt the difference. Between the drift and the centre, between the wrong position and the right one, between the stance that was technically correct and the stance that was actually settled. He was right — the density variation in the ground was pulling my centre without my registering it, and his hand had showed me where my centre actually was, and the stance from the correct centre felt different from the outside and different from the inside and the difference was significant.
“Yes,” I said.
He moved his hand. He stepped back. He said: “Again from the beginning of the patch.”
I went again from the beginning of the patch.
The cold lived in my ribs. I crossed the patch twice more in the correct stance and did not drift and registered the density variation consciously this time, feeling where the ground was pulling and choosing the correction rather than having it made for me, and when I reached the end of the third crossing he said “better” in the tone he used when something was better and I registered the feel of that better in the place I registered things I was not going to write in my notes and then absolutely was going to write in my notes.
*   *   *
We worked for two hours. By the end of the two hours I had cold in my left wrist, my right arm above the elbow, the side of my ribcage, the back of my stance-hand twice, and a brief contact at my left shoulder when he had caught my weight-shift before it became a fall on the variable ground, which had been fast and certain and had lasted perhaps one second and that I was filing under things I was going to think about later with enormous thoroughness.
He stopped the session the way he always stopped it — not a declared ending, just the shift in his quality of presence that indicated the functional portion was complete and the remainder was optional. I had learned to read this shift the way I learned to read everything about him: by paying attention until the pattern was clear, and then trusting the pattern.
“The stance held,” he said. “By the third crossing you were feeling the density variation before it pulled rather than correcting after.”
“I was trying to anticipate it,” I said. “Get ahead of the drift.”
“That’s the correct approach,” he said. “The deep Hollow rewards anticipation over correction. It moves faster than you can correct in real time.” He looked at me with the silver eyes — assessment, the kind he made at the end of sessions, measuring what had changed. “You retained everything from the first week. All of it.”
“I retain most things,” I said.
“Most people retain sixty percent after three weeks,” he said. “The rest drifts. The body forgets when the repetition stops.”
I thought about the point between my shoulder blades where the posture correction had been made two weeks ago and that I had maintained perfectly every session since without it being mentioned again. “Maybe I have an unusual relationship with forgetting,” I said.
He looked at me for a moment. “Maybe,” he said, and the word had something in it I did not have a complete vocabulary for yet — a quality I had been encountering more frequently in the past two weeks, something that was not quite the almost-not-a-smile but was in the same family, something that lived in the spaces between what he said and what he meant. I was working on it.
I retrieved the coat from the courtyard wall. He stayed while I put it on — not demonstrably, not in the way of waiting, just present in the way he was present when the session had ended and neither of us had left yet, which had become, I realized, a regular feature. The sessions ended and there was a period of some minutes that was neither session nor departure and that we occupied in the same space without apparent difficulty.
I did not know when this had started. I knew it was regular.
“The weather’s shifting,” I said, because the sky above the courtyard was doing the thing I had learned to read as an atmospheric change — not the deepening of the regular cycle but a different quality, a lateral change, the Hollow’s mood rather than its time. “Tomorrow’s session will be different.”
He looked up at the sky. “Yes,” he said. “The lower territory has a pressure change coming. Two days, maybe three.” He looked back at me. “We’ll use it. The deep Hollow’s atmospheric shifts are part of the training.”
“You were going to teach me to read the weather anyway,” I said.
“I was,” he confirmed.
“And the timing is convenient.”
“The Hollow’s timing is very rarely coincidental,” he said, and I filed this in the place I was keeping things Sable had said were groundwork — the Hollow responding, the recognition, the beginning — and thought about the atmospheric shift arriving exactly when the training required it, and thought about the territory’s behavior in general toward me, and thought about what Vasilisa had said about the Hall being patient for something.
I was going to have to ask him about the Hollow soon. Properly ask, not the careful edge-finding of the first week, but directly, with the specific question that Sable kept saying was the right one and that I was building toward with the accumulated groundwork of five weeks.
Not today. Today I had enough.
“Same time tomorrow,” I said.
“Same time,” he said.
He went toward the Hall’s north entrance. I went toward the east, because the shadow-fox was at the east courtyard wall and I had taken to passing it on the way back — and the shadow-fox had taken to dropping from the wall when I passed and walking approximately six feet behind me to the Hall’s east door before melting back into the territory.
It did this today. I heard it land behind me — not a sound exactly, the cold-light creatures did not fully commit to physical sound, more the suggestion of a landing — and walked to the east door with my coat pulled close and the cold living in six specific places on my body and the Hollow’s atmospheric shift moving in the sky above.
I said good morning to the shadow-fox at the door. It sat and attended, ears forward.
I went inside. The cold in my marked hand had not faded. If anything it was deeper — settling into the bone like something that intended to stay.
*   *   *
Sable was in the library. She was in my chair, because she was always in my chair, and she was reading something from the left-side shelves that was not one of the books I had been working through. She had her own relationship with the library’s contents that predated and ran parallel to mine and that I was increasingly curious about.
She looked up when I came in. She looked at me in the complete way she looked at things — taking in whatever the complete way involved, which I had given up trying to enumerate and had simply accepted as: Sable sees everything — and then she looked back at her book.
She said nothing.
This was, again, the Sable calibration — the knowledge of which moments needed silence. I sat in the right chair and pulled my notes toward me and looked at the page I had been working on yesterday — a revised sequence of the questions with the right one working its way up from the bottom, and I tried to read it.
I read the same three lines four times.
The cold in my ribs was specific and present and entirely too distracting for a person who considered herself in good control of her attention.
I turned to a fresh page.
“The stance work for the deep Hollow requires physical corrections,” I said, to my notes rather than to Sable, because I was not having this conversation with Sable, I was simply saying something to the air that Sable happened to be in.
“I imagine it does,” Sable said, to her book.
“His hands are very cold.”
“They would be,” she said. “He runs cold in the literal sense. It’s a Vorath characteristic — the oldest of them run the coldest. He’s been the coldest in the territory for a thousand years.” A pause. “The cold tends to linger on contact.”
“For about an hour,” I said. “On average. Depending on location.”
Sable turned a page of her book. She said nothing.
The nothing was extremely loud.
“I’m simply observing it,” I said. “As a physiological characteristic of the situation.”
“Of course,” Sable said.
“It’s relevant data.”
“Entirely,” she agreed.
I looked at my fresh page. I looked at the pen in my hand. I looked at the cold in my ribs, which was currently my most present sensory experience and had been for forty minutes and was going to be for another twenty at minimum.
“Sable,” I said.
“Mm.”
“If you have something to say, you can say it.”
She looked up from her book. She looked at me with both membrane-colored eyes in the library’s quiet Hollow-light, and she had the expression that meant: I have something to say and I am deciding whether now is the moment. Then she decided.
“I have been alive since before the separation of the realms,” she said. “I have watched a very large number of people navigate a very large number of situations. I have watched, specifically, Aleksei across eight hundred years of this Hall and everything it has contained.” She paused. “I have not, in all of that time, seen him make a correction twice.” She looked back at her book. “The stance correction at the shoulder blades was made in the second week and has not been mentioned since. You’ve held the position without being asked.”
I was very still.
“He notices,” she said, in the tone of someone delivering a fact rather than an opinion. “He noticed immediately. He hasn’t said anything about it.” She turned a page. “He also noticed, in the fourth session, that you had stopped using his right-side lag as an approach. You’d closed it — absorbed it, made the knowledge part of your training rather than a tactical tool. He adapted his right side without telling you he had.” A pause. “He could have left it. It was, by that point, not a real vulnerability. He closed it anyway.”
I thought about this. About the symmetry of it — her absorbing information about him and incorporating it rather than deploying it, and him closing the gap anyway, and neither of them acknowledging it, and both of them knowing. “Why are you telling me this,” I said.
“Groundwork,” she said. She closed her book — fully, the way she closed things when she was done with them rather than pausing — and stood. “The right question is getting closer. I want the foundation to be solid when you get there.”
She left. I sat in the right chair with the cold living in six places on my body and the fresh page in front of me and the quality of the library around me — the old attention of it, the patient books on their shelves, the presences in the upper reaches that were warm and quiet and knowing.
I wrote, at the top of the fresh page:
What I know about the training, recorded accurately.
Below that, in my regular handwriting, not the small marginal kind:
He corrects once. I hold the position. He has noticed that I hold it. He closed a vulnerability I stopped exploiting without being asked. Neither of us has said anything.
I looked at this. Then I wrote below it, in the medium handwriting, the one between full and marginal that I was apparently developing as a third category for things that were too significant for the margin and too unresolved for full view:
We are both doing the same thing. Operating on information received and held rather than stated. Choosing not to name it. Something about the not-naming feels —
I stopped. I held the pen above the page for a moment.
Then I wrote the end of the sentence.
— correct. Like a thing that will mean more when it is finally named because of how long it went unnamed.
I looked at what I had written.
I looked at the cold in my ribs, which was still there, still present, still the most immediate sensory fact in my current experience.
The cold lived in the skin. I had been documenting this for four weeks as data. I was going to keep documenting it as data, and alongside the documentation I was going to let the other layer exist — the layer that was not data, the layer that was the simple and inconvenient truth that it was his cold and that I knew exactly whose it was every time it arrived, and that I had not, once, in four weeks of daily sessions and daily cold, wanted it to stop.
I was going to keep documentation and the other layer and I was going to call both things what they were, at least in my own notes, at least on this page, in the medium handwriting that was neither marginal nor full view.
I turned the page. I went back to the question sequence. I worked for two hours with the particular focused attention of someone who has said a true thing and is now able to think about other things without the unsaid true thing sitting in the way of the thinking.
The cold lasted the full hour.
*   *   *
I went to the eastern boundary alone.
Not spontaneously — I had been thinking about it for three days, since the session where Aleksei had walked me through membrane-reading theory but refused to let me attempt it practically. Too soon, he’d said. Foundation first. Understanding before application.
I understood foundation. I understood theory. What I did not understand was why I should wait for permission to attempt something I was already capable of doing.
The eastern boundary was a twenty-minute walk from the Hall, through the silver forest where the trees ran their steady light and the shadow-fox followed at its devoted distance. I did not tell Aleksei I was going. The binding would tell him my general location and wellbeing — it always did. But general was not specific, and if I was going to attempt this, I wanted to attempt it without the pressure of him watching.
The membrane at the boundary was not visible in the ordinary sense. It was felt — a change in the quality of the air, a pressure that suggested threshold, the specific feeling of standing at the edge of something that had rules about crossing.
I stood three feet back from where the pressure intensified. I breathed. I did what Aleksei had taught me: found the deep Hollow stance, located the membrane’s frequency, extended my awareness toward it the way you extended your hand toward warmth.
The membrane responded.
Not gently. Not the way it had responded to Aleksei when he demonstrated. It surged toward my awareness like something that had been waiting, and the contact was immediate and overwhelming and too much.
I stumbled backward. The membrane followed — not physically, but in my awareness, pressing against the edges of my perception like water pressing against a door that was not meant to hold water. I tried to pull back. The membrane held.
The binding flared.
Not gently. Aleksei’s state shifted from steady to sharp alarm in the space of a single heartbeat, and I felt him moving through the territory, fast, closing the distance between the Hall and the eastern boundary.
The membrane was still holding. I could not pull my awareness back. I tried the deep Hollow stance again — tried to find the frequency, tried to match it rather than resist it. The membrane shifted slightly. Not releasing. Adjusting.
And then Aleksei was there, physically present at the boundary, and his hand was on my shoulder and his voice was saying something in the first language — not a command, not a mark, something else. The membrane released. My awareness snapped back into my body with the feel of something that had been stretched too far and was now returning too fast.
I sat down. Not by choice. My legs simply stopped holding me.
Aleksei knelt beside me. Not speaking. His hand was still on my shoulder — the cold present, steady, grounding.
“I thought I could do it,” I said. After a long moment. When I could speak without my voice shaking.
“You could,” he said. The even voice doing considerable work. “That was the problem.”
I looked at him. “What.”
“The membrane responded to you because you have the Claim,” he said. “It recognized what you are. It responded at full strength rather than the measured introduction I would have given you.” He paused. “If I had not been here to release you, you would have been locked in contact until the membrane decided to release you on its own. Which might have been minutes. Or hours.”
“Oh,” I said.
“Yes,” he said.
We sat at the eastern boundary for a while. The shadow-fox arrived and settled into its attending position. The silver forest ran its light. Aleksei’s hand stayed on my shoulder, the cold constant and present.
“I should have asked,” I said.
“Yes,” he said.
“I wanted to prove I could do it.”
“I know,” he said. “The binding told me. Your state shifted three days ago — from accepting the training pace to resenting it.” He looked at the membrane, at the place where the air changed quality. “I should have addressed it then. I was giving you time to decide whether to ask or attempt on your own.”
“And I attempted on my own,” I said.
“Yes,” he said. “Which tells me something useful about how you solve problems.” Not disapproving. Simply noting. “And it tells me I need to adjust the training pace. If you are ready to push boundaries, then the boundaries need to be somewhere you can push them safely.”
He helped me stand. We walked back to the Hall together. The binding settled back to its normal register — his state steady again, mine recovering but not yet steady.
“Tomorrow we attempt membrane-reading,” he said at the Hall’s threshold. “Properly. With the measured introduction. With me present to release you if the contact overwhelms.”
“All right,” I said.
“And if you decide to test boundaries again,” he said, “tell me first. The Hollow will not always release you simply because you ask. Some things require preparation. Some things require having someone present who knows how to undo what you’ve done.”
I looked at him. “Are you angry.”
“No,” he said. The even voice. The truth. “I am recalibrating. You are faster than I planned for. I need to plan differently.”
He went inside. I stood at the threshold for a moment, thinking about the membrane and the contact and the way it had held me, and thinking about the fact that being fast was not the same as being ready and that I had just learned the difference at the eastern boundary.
The shadow-fox made its quiet vibration from the courtyard wall.
I went inside.
I tracked it, as I always tracked it.
But the fox’s vibration had not been the attending sound. It had been the other one — the lower register, the one Aleksei had not yet told me about.
In the morning I would learn what it meant. I would wish I had asked sooner.


Chapter Thirteen: She Arrives at Things Faster Than I Plan For
Aleksei
I had been teaching for a long time.
Not in any formal capacity — I had no students, had never designated myself a teacher, had not in a thousand years found a situation that required the specific and sustained effort of transmitting knowledge to another person across daily sessions. I had answered questions. I had given information. This was different.
What made it different was a question I had not examined until the third week of training, when I arrived in the north courtyard before she did — as I always arrived before her, because arriving after would have required her to wait for me and I did not make her wait for things — and stood in the dark earth and considered what it was I was actually doing here.
I was teaching. I had been doing this for three weeks and had not noticed I was doing it until I caught myself, two nights ago in the north study, working through the atmospheric shift that was coming and planning the specific sequence in which I would introduce its components and thinking: she will ask about the deep current first. She always starts with the mechanism before the application.
She always started with the mechanism before the application.
I had a student who had a consistent and identifiable learning pattern, which I had mapped to enough precision to anticipate her questions, and I was planning my sessions around that anticipation. This had happened without a decision. This was simply what the sessions had become over three weeks of daily contact, and I was examining it now, in the courtyard before she arrived, because I had learned that the things which happened without decisions were the things most worth examining.
The binding told me she had left her room. Moving toward the east courtyard first — she always checked on the shadow-fox in the morning before sessions, which she did not announce and which I was not going to mention knowing. I had approximately three minutes.
I used them to arrive at no useful conclusions and stood in the center of the dark earth and waited.
*   *   *
She came through the courtyard door in the coat.
She was always in the coat. The coat on her was the coat in its correct configuration. I had thought this on the third morning when she came to breakfast in it and had filed the thought in the place I filed things that did not require immediate attention and then kept finding in my way regardless.
She took it off at the courtyard entrance and hung it on the wall and came onto the dark earth in her shirtsleeves with the specific efficiency of someone transitioning between states — the at-rest state and the working state — that had a clean boundary and no ambiguity between them. She had always been like this. She was present before the session started. She had already been working through whatever the session would cover before she arrived. She came in having thought.
This was not a common quality. I had been doing this long enough to know it was not common.
“The atmospheric shift,” she said, by way of greeting, settling into the starting stance with the ease of someone who had made it habitual. “It started last night. The sky had a different quality around the third hour.”
“Yes,” I said.
“I tried to read what direction it was coming from. I couldn’t.” She looked at the sky above the courtyard, which was in its morning register, the deep blue before the shift to noon. “I can read the standard cycle now. The lateral changes are harder.”
“The lateral changes are harder because they don’t follow the cycle’s logic,” I said. “They follow the Hollow’s emotional logic, which is a different system.”
She looked at me. “The Hollow has emotional logic.”
“The Hollow has moods,” I said. “The cycle is the body. The lateral shifts are the moods. They’re related but not identical, and you can’t read the moods through the cycle’s grammar.”
She was quiet for a moment. Not the quiet of someone processing an expected answer but the quiet of someone whose sequence of questions had just reorganized. I knew this quality of her silence by now — the specific shift in her attention that happened when new information had structural implications rather than additive ones. The question order was changing.
“So I need two separate grammars,” she said.
“You need to understand that they’re separate,” I said. “The grammar develops from that understanding.”
She nodded, once, in the way she nodded when something had settled into place. I had learned to read her nods as precisely as she had learned to read my almost-not-a-smile — or perhaps more precisely, since she appeared to have a finer vocabulary for what my expression did than I had ever developed for my own face. “All right,” she said. “Show me the mechanism.”
I showed her the mechanism.
*   *   *
She asked seven questions in the first hour.
Not seven questions in the way of someone generating questions — that category of person asked questions for the asking, for the performance of engagement, and the questions arrived in clusters with no necessary relationship to each other. She asked seven questions that were a sequence. Each one built from the previous answer. Each one was the question that the previous answer had made possible, the exact next question in a line of reasoning that she was building in real time, out loud, using my answers as the material.
I had taught many things across many years. I had transmitted information in various directions. I had answered questions that ranged from precise and answerable to poorly formed and requiring significant reformulation before they could be addressed. In a thousand years of answering questions, I had never been asked a sequence of seven questions that required me to think alongside the questioning rather than simply ahead of it.
She arrived, on the seventh question, at a conclusion I had not expected her to reach this session. I had planned to introduce it in the third week of atmospheric training. She arrived at it, from her own sequence of reasoning, in the first hour of the first day.
“So the deep Hollow’s pressure system isn’t a closed system,” she said. It was not quite a question — it had the sense of something being stated for confirmation rather than asked for information. “It bleeds into the upper territory through the membrane surface, and the bleed is how the upper territory reads what’s happening below, and that’s why the shadow-fox knew the shift was coming before the sky changed. It was reading the membrane.”
I looked at her.
“That’s the mechanism,” she said. “The grammar is learning to read the membrane’s surface the way the shadow-fox reads it.”
I had been going to teach her this in the third week.
“Yes,” I said.
She held my gaze for a moment with the brown eyes that I had been looking at for five weeks and that I was no longer looking at with the removed attention of assessment. That had changed somewhere — I was not certain when — and I was not going to locate the change right now in the middle of a training session, but I was aware of the change and aware that it was relevant information that I was not examining at the moment and intended to examine later.
“The shadow-fox has been teaching me too,” she said. “I didn’t know that’s what was happening.”
“It often works that way,” I said. “The territory’s creatures teach by proximity before they teach by anything more direct.”
“You knew it was doing this,” she said.
“I suspected,” I said.
“And you didn’t tell me because—”
“Because being told you’re being taught is different from learning,” I said. “And what it was teaching you required not knowing.”
She looked at the sky. She was thinking — the tone of her thinking, which I had also mapped, which involved a stillness that was different from her default stillness, more interior, the stillness of someone whose attention had fully moved inward. “The not-knowing was the lesson,” she said.
“Part of it,” I said.
She looked back at me. It lasted perhaps two seconds before the management reinstated. She looked back at the sky.
I looked at the space where the expression had been.
*   *   *
The stance corrections were the part of the training I had not planned adequately.
That was the honest accounting of it. I had known the ground work required physical corrections, had known this from the beginning, and had approached it as a practical component of the training with the same removed efficiency I applied to practical components generally. The first session had been exactly that — practical, efficient, calibrated, producing exactly the results intended.
The second session had been the same.
By the third session I had understood that I had not planned adequately.
The problem was not the corrections themselves. The corrections were exactly what they were: purposeful, brief, informational, a physical means of transmitting knowledge that the body retained better than the mind. I had made corrections like this before, in the very occasional situations across a thousand years where physical training had been a component of something I was responsible for. There was nothing unusual about the corrections.
The problem was warmth.
Vorath ran cold. This was the baseline of what I was — not the cold of the air, not the cold of the Hollow’s lower registers, but the cold of something very old that had never run at mortal temperature and had no particular relationship with the concept. The cold was simply the condition of existence, the way gravity was the condition of existence: not experienced as a thing, simply the state in which everything else occurred.
I had not thought about warmth in a very long time.
Mortal warmth: I knew it abstractly, knew it the way I knew the properties of things I had not encountered recently. Mortals ran warm. This was not new information.
What was new was the tone of her warmth.
It arrived the way sunlight arrived: not a thing you reached for, not a thing that required your cooperation or your attention, simply present and then registered as present in the involuntary way of things that the body processed before the mind had weighed in. I had not stood in full sunlight in a very long time. The Hollow did not produce sunlight in the mortal sense. I had not had occasion to remember what sunlight felt like.
Her warmth reminded me.
This was the fact I had been managing since the third training session and that I was managing with the professional efficiency I applied to all things that required managing, which was: acknowledge, file, do not let it alter the functional layer. The training was the functional layer. The corrections were components of the training. Her warmth was a characteristic of the corrections. File, manage, proceed.
The management was becoming slightly more effortful daily.
I had not told anyone this, because there was no one I would tell, except Vasilisa, who would receive the information with the specific expression that had prompted me, on two previous occasions across centuries, to deeply regret having given her any information at all.
*   *   *
Today’s corrections were at the ankle, the arm above the elbow, and the side.
I noted this afterward, in the private accounting that I maintained in the place that was mine alone, with the same precision she used for her notes. Ankle, arm, side. Each brief, each purposeful, each exactly as long as necessary.
Her warmth at the ankle: three seconds, and then the absence of it, and then the absence noted.
Her warmth at the arm: slightly longer, because the arm correction required the stance to be rebuilt from that point upward and confirming the rebuild took more time than the initial correction. Five seconds. The absence more noticeable after five than after three.
Her warmth at the side: the heel of my hand against her ribs, through fabric — not direct contact and which I had not anticipated would register the way it registered. It registered. It moved through things. It moved through the fabric of the coat she had taken off at the wall, and it moved through the working shirt she was wearing, and it moved into the heel of my hand with the feel of a thing that intended to be felt.
I had said there.
I had meant the position of her centre. I had been, in that moment, entirely correct and fully technical, and I had meant it technically and produced it technically and every element of the correction had been exactly what it was supposed to be.
The warmth in my hand afterward was not technical. The warmth was simply a characteristic of warm things touched briefly. It faded in the time that things faded. It was not significant.
I noted that it faded. I noted when it faded. That we were both tracking something. That neither of us had named what we were tracking. And that the not-naming had a quality to it that was not avoidance but something more considered, more deliberate, something that both of us had apparently decided on independently and simultaneously, which was that this particular thing was worth keeping unnamed until it had fully become what it was going to be.
This was a thought I had not meant to have.
I returned to the session.
*   *   *
After the session, in the period that was neither session nor departure — which had also become habitual, which I had also not decided to make habitual — she asked about the atmospheric shift’s second component.
“The deep current,” she said. “It moves in the opposite direction from the surface shift. I noticed that when the sky changed last night the shadow-fox went still rather than moving. If it were tracking the surface shift it would have moved. But it went still, which means it was tracking something coming from below.”
I looked at her.
“The deep current moves up when the atmospheric shift moves across,” she said. “It’s the Hollow’s version of pressure equalization. The shift creates a gradient and the gradient pulls from the deep.”
I had been going to teach her this tomorrow.
“You’ve been paying very close attention to the shadow-fox,” I said.
“I’ve been paying close attention to everything,” she said. “But the shadow-fox is the best available model for Hollow-reading that I have direct access to. It’s faster than me and it’s been doing this longer and it knows I’m watching, which means if it’s going to teach me—” She stopped. Something in her expression shifted — the quick reorganization of someone who had just arrived at the same conclusion twice from different directions. “It knows I’m watching,” she said again, slowly. “It let me watch.”
“Yes,” I said.
She looked at the courtyard wall where the shadow-fox was not currently present, having moved to the east grounds after our session began in the way it moved when the session occupied the north courtyard. “It’s been teaching me from the beginning,” she said. “Since the second day. The treeline position — it wasn’t watching the Hall. It was giving me something to watch.”
“The creatures of the territory,” I said, “make their own assessments. I don’t direct them. The shadow-fox decided on the second day what it was going to do and has done it consistently.”
She turned to look at me. The open expression was back — not the two-second version, a longer one, the composure of someone who had stopped managing something for long enough to simply be looking. “You’re not surprised by any of this,” she said. “The shadow-fox. The book. The atmospheric shift. None of it surprises you.”
“No,” I said.
“You expected it,” she said. Not an accusation, a statement — she was cataloguing, as she catalogued everything, organizing new information into existing structures. “Not all of it. But enough of it.” Her eyes were steady on mine. “You knew the territory would respond to me.”
I said nothing.
“That’s on my list,” she said. “On the sequence. Not yet at the top. But close.”
“I know,” I said.
She held my gaze for a moment. This silence had a question in it that was not quite asked. She was deciding whether to ask it.
She decided not to.
“Same time tomorrow,” she said.
“Same time,” I said.
She retrieved the coat. She put it on with the ease of someone whose coat this was and had always been, and she went to the east door, and I watched her go with the weight of attention that I was filing under the things I was going to examine later.
The door closed. I stood in the empty courtyard.
I did the accounting.
Five weeks. Forty-seven sessions if you included the daily navigation work alongside the sparring, which I was now including because separating them required a clarity about what constituted a session that I was no longer certain I had. Forty-seven sessions, and in forty-seven sessions she had arrived ready every time, had asked sequences of questions rather than questions, had reached conclusions I had planned to introduce later every single time I had introduced a new foundation, had maintained every correction after it was made without requiring it twice, had made me think — genuinely, alongside her, in the way of problems that required a different intelligence than you arrived with — in every session that involved the atmospheric systems, the membrane-surface, the deep Hollow’s living properties.
She had made me think.
This was a thing I was noting with the care it deserved. I was not a person who was made to think by conversations. I did not generally require the other person to think alongside me, because the other person generally did not have the foundation to think alongside me, and the gap between foundations was too large to bridge in real time, and the most useful thing was usually transmission of information and then the other person’s thinking happened independently later.
She had no formal foundation for the Hollow. She had five weeks of active construction. The construction had happened fast enough and in the right order that she could now think alongside me in real time on things that were at the edge of what the foundation supported — not because she knew more than she knew, but because the way she built her foundation created the kind of structure that allowed for reasoning at the edges.
She had arrived at the deep current from the shadow-fox and the shadow-fox’s stillness and a logic I had spent three days constructing the session to build toward.
She had arrived at it in the post-session period while putting on her coat.
I stood in the empty courtyard and I made the accounting with the same precision I applied to all accounting, and I arrived at the thing I had been arriving at for five weeks across forty-seven sessions, and I said it to myself, plainly, in the specific interior register I reserved for things that were true regardless of what I did about them:
She was the most interesting thing to happen to this realm in a hundred years.
Not the Old Claim. Not the binding. Not the Hollow’s waking or the shadow-fox’s attention or the book that had answered her touch. Those were significant, and they had implications I was still mapping, and they would require conversation when I had the right words.
Her. Specifically. The person who arrived ready and asked sequences and maintained corrections and made him think and retrieved her coat with the ease of someone whose coat this was and had always been.
Her.
I stood in the courtyard for a while after that.
The Hollow’s atmospheric shift moved through the territory above the high walls, the lateral change in the sky that was the Hollow’s mood rather than its body. The shadow-fox was somewhere in the east grounds, reading the membrane surface, teaching and being watched teaching and knowing it was watched and having decided this was acceptable. The Hall was warm in the way it had been warm for five weeks, steady and specific and pleased about things in the quiet way of things that had been patient for a very long time and were now watching their patience produce results.
The binding told me she was in the library.
I went to the north study. I sat down. I thought about warmth, and the sense of it, and what it meant that I was aware of sunlight for the first time in longer than I could precisely calculate, and I thought about the word she, standing alone at the end of a thought the way a door stood at the end of a corridor, marking the entrance to something I had not yet walked into.
The north study was adjacent to the library corridor.
I sat in it for a long time, with the warmth that had faded from my hand an hour ago and that I was somehow still aware of, and I thought about the question she was building toward and whether I was going to have the right words when she arrived at it, and I thought that I had been working on the words for five weeks and was going to need to work harder, because she was arriving faster than I had planned for.
She arrived at things faster than I planned for.
I found I did not mind.
What I minded was that arriving fast meant reaching the difficult things sooner. And the most difficult thing — the architecture of the telling I had been building every night — was the thing that could make her leave.
The Claim was not the binding. It was not law. It could be carried out of Nochval and I could not follow.
Thirteen days before the sequence required me to tell her everything.


Chapter Fourteen: Something Worth Staying Close To
Kira
Six weeks into my stay at Nochval and the north courtyard had begun to feel like a room I knew too well.
Not boring — nothing about the training was boring, the training was the most functionally interesting thing I had done in years, and I was aware that boring was not the word. Contained was the word. The courtyard was not giving me new things to learn from anymore.
I knew he knew this. The contained feeling had been in my state for three days. He had said nothing.
So when he appeared in the library doorway on a Tuesday morning — I had maintained the mortal week on principle, and Tuesday was the day after Monday which was the day I had written in my notes: if he does not suggest the territory this week I will suggest it myself — and said: “We’re going into the forest today,” I received this with the equanimity of someone who had been prepared for exactly this for several days and was choosing not to indicate that.
“Good,” I said.
He looked at me briefly with the look that had acquired, over six weeks of looking, a quality when he suspected I had been ahead of him. I had seen this look four times. It was one of my favorite looks. I did not tell him this. “The atmospheric shift has stabilized,” he said. “The conditions are good for the first navigation session.”
“When do we leave,” I said.
He looked at Sable in my chair, because Sable was always in my chair, and Sable looked back at him from her book with the particular expression she reserved for him: fond, slightly exasperated, entirely pleased with events. “An hour,” he said to me, not to her.
“I’ll be ready in twenty minutes,” I said.
“I know,” he said. And left. Sable turned a page.
I sat looking at the empty doorway for a moment.
“He said I know,” I said.
“He did,” Sable agreed, to her book.
“He knows my pace. He knows how long I take to prepare for things.”
“Six weeks of a binding that communicates state as well as location,” she said. “And the Hall. And the fact that you have been ready for this for three days and he could feel it.” She turned a page with the serenity of someone who had said enough and was going back to her book. “He gave you an hour because he thought you might want it. He knows you’ll use twenty minutes.”
I looked at the doorway for another moment. Then I put down my pen, closed my notes, and went to get ready. I used nineteen minutes. I was not going to examine the missing minute.
*   *   *
The silver forest was different from inside it.
I had been to its edge dozens of times — in the mornings, in the walks I took along the territory’s perimeter, in the daily ritual of the shadow-fox at the treeline that had become as habitual as breakfast. I had pressed my hand to the glass of my window and watched the forest from above and I had stood at its border where the Hall’s grounds became the territory proper and felt the shift in the air. I had understood, intellectually, that being inside it would be different from being at its edge.
Understanding it intellectually was not the same as being inside it.
Inside the forest, the beauty changed category. It arrived before you decided to notice it, moved through the body before the mind formed an opinion. The trees were not decorative. They were structural — the Hollow’s architecture expressed as a forest.
I stopped walking four steps in.
He stopped beside me. He did not say anything. He had the quality he sometimes produced — rarer than the stillness, rarer than the controlled surface — of someone who was letting something happen rather than managing it. He was letting me have the four-step stop. He was standing in the forest with his hands at his sides and he was giving me whatever I needed to give the moment.
I breathed in. The forest had a smell that was also not categorizable in the existing vocabulary: cold and deep and mineral and the quality of things that were alive in a way that was older than the ways I knew things were alive. I breathed it in, and I stood in the layered light, and I felt the ground under my feet shift in the way it shifted when I practiced the deep-Hollow stance in the courtyard except more — more present, more specific, more like a genuine communication than a property of terrain.
“It knows you’re here,” he said quietly. Not a surprise in his voice. A confirmation.
“It always knows I’m here,” I said. “From the window.”
“From the window you’re in the Hall,” he said. “The Hall mediates. Here there’s no mediation.” A pause. “It’s different for you than for most people in this territory.”
“Most people,” I said carefully, “would be Hollow-sick by now.”
“Yes,” he said.
“And I’m not.”
“No,” he said.
We stood in the silver forest with the layered light and the ground that was communicating and the six weeks of everything between us, and I felt the question that was rising toward the top of my sequence pushing at the sentence I was about to say, and I made a decision about it. Not yet. Today was for the forest. “Show me how to navigate,” I said.
He looked at me. I looked at him. Something passed in the silver light between us that was not a conversation but was adjacent to one. He nodded, once. “This way,” he said.
We went into the forest.
*   *   *
Navigation in the silver forest was not what I had expected — a compass-bearing-and-landmarks approach, the kind of navigation I had been doing in Vreshka’s streets since I was twelve. The forest moved. Not dramatically, not in the way of a thing that intended to confuse — more the way of a thing that was alive and therefore not static, that adjusted its arrangement in response to the state of itself and everything in it adjusted constantly without the adjustment requiring a decision.
“The landmarks change,” he said. “Don’t use them as fixed points. Use them as current information only.”
“Then what’s fixed,” I said.
“The membrane’s surface direction,” he said. “Always west of wherever you are. The deepest Hollow pulls north, always. The light from the oldest trees is different in quality from the newer growth — learn to read it. And here—” He stopped. He turned to face me in a small clearing where three trees met at their canopy, their light intersecting in the particular layered way that the intersections produced. “Feel the ground.”
I settled into the deep-Hollow stance. The ground was telling me things. Not in language, in the sub-linguistic register where the book’s mark had communicated beginning, the same register, the same quality of something older than language.
“North,” I said, without deciding to say it. The ground had given me north the way a compass gave north — not as a choice, as a simple fact about the world.
“Yes,” he said.
“And west—” I turned slightly. The membrane’s pull was faint at this distance from the territory’s edge but present, a directional awareness that was distinct from the binding’s directional sense, a different quality altogether. “That way.”
“Yes.” There was something in his voice I had not heard before — something careful and warm simultaneously, the combination of two qualities he did not usually produce at the same time. I looked at him. He was watching me with the silver eyes in the silver light, and the expression was not the controlled nothing and it was not the almost-not-a-smile. It was something I had not catalogued yet because he had not produced it before. I held it, turning it, trying to place it, and could not.
“What,” I said.
“Nothing,” he said. And turned back to the path. “The deep pull gets stronger as you go north. Don’t follow it past the second grove — you’ll feel the shift in the ground when you reach the grove. Stop there.”
I filed the expression in the place I filed things I had not yet named and could not yet name and followed him deeper into the forest.
*   *   *
We walked for two hours.
Not continuously — the navigation training was not a walk, it was a work session that happened to occur in a forest, with stops and assessments and demonstrations and the kind of focused teaching he brought to every session. He tested my membrane-reading three times, asking me to locate the west direction from different positions inside the forest where the trees’ arrangement changed and the light quality shifted. I was right twice and wrong once, and the wrong time was because I had been reading the light rather than the membrane and the light here was not a reliable compass the way the membrane was — the lesson.
“You’re compensating with visual information,” he said. “The membrane is felt. You can’t see it.”
“I know that intellectually,” I said. “In practice I keep reaching for the light.”
“Because you’ve been navigating with visual information your whole life,” he said. “The membrane-reading is a new sense. New senses take time to trust.”
I thought about this. “Is it a new sense,” I said. “Or is it something I already had that I’m learning to use.”
He went quiet in the way he went quiet when a question had landed somewhere real. “It may be the latter,” he said carefully. “The deep-Hollow stance came to you faster than it comes to most people with training. The membrane-reading is similarly — available to you in a way it isn’t usually available.”
“Because of the Hollow recognizing me,” I said. It was not quite a question.
“Yes,” he said.
“Which you’re going to tell me about when you have the right words.”
He looked at me sidelong — the look he used when I had said something accurate that he was not going to acknowledge as accurate and was also not going to deny. I had catalogued this look five times. “Yes,” he said.
“The words are taking a while,” I said. Not a criticism. He knew it wasn’t a criticism — the binding would tell him that much.
“Some things are more difficult to word than others,” he said — the most he had said about the process directly, and which I received in the specific place I received information from him that arrived voluntarily and without prompting. It went in carefully. It stayed.
We walked on. The shadow-fox appeared midway through the second hour, moving through the trees to our left at a distance of approximately thirty feet, its cold-light quality distinct against the silver forest’s bioluminescence. It was not following us — it was moving in parallel, the feel of something that has its own route through a space and is simply occupying the same space at the same time with a decided awareness of the other occupants.
“It comes into the forest,” I said, watching it move between the trunks.
“It’s lived here for decades,” he said. “The Hall’s grounds are relatively new territory for it. The forest is home.”
“And the Hall’s grounds are — what? Curiosity? Something worth watching?”
He was quiet for a moment. “Something worth staying close to,” he said, and did not elaborate.
I watched the shadow-fox move between the silver trunks and thought about things worth staying close to, and the thought had an application Not now in the middle of a navigation session in the silver forest. I filed it in the medium-handwriting place and walked on.
*   *   *
The atmospheric shift arrived in the second hour without warning — which was the point, which was why he had timed the session to coincide with it.
I felt it before I saw it. The membrane gave me a lateral shift from the east, moving fast. Vorath. The Hunt-sense oriented, gave me position, distance, trajectory. They were approaching from the oldest part of the forest, moving through the atmospheric shift with the ease of someone for whom the shift held no difficulty.
The bioluminescence in the trees responded. Each tree brightened, slightly — the way pupils dilate in changing light, an involuntary physiological response to the shift in the environment. The forest became, in the space of sixty seconds, several degrees colder and noticeably more lit and somehow more present, as if the atmospheric shift had the weight of attention coming to focus.
“Read the membrane,” he said.
I read the membrane. The west direction was still west, unchanged. But the deep pull from the north had intensified — the ground under my feet was communicating something I did not yet have the vocabulary for, the same sub-linguistic register but louder, more urgent, a change from the background communication of the normal state. “The deep current is moving faster,” I said.
“The atmospheric shift drives it,” he said. “What else.”
I felt for the other things. The light had brightened but the light was not directional information. “The Hollow is paying attention to something,” I said. “The shift is — it’s not weather. It’s more like alertness. Something has its attention.”
The silence that followed was one of the specific ones — not the productive thinking kind, not the door-closing kind. The kind that contained something he was deciding whether to give to the conversation.
“What,” I said.
“You’re reading correctly,” he said. “Something does have its attention.” He looked east, through the trees, toward the direction the shift had come from. His expression was doing the thing I had no name for — not the controlled nothing, not any of the catalogued looks, something new and something old simultaneously. Something careful in a way I had not seen him be careful. “The shift is from the eastern boundary,” he said. “There are other territories beyond Nochval’s eastern edge.”
“Other Vorath,” I said.
“Yes.”
“One of them is at the boundary,” I said. Reading his face, reading the membrane, reading the way the forest had brightened and the deep current had accelerated and the Hollow’s mood had the weight of attention coming to focus. “And the Hollow is — it’s not pleased about it.”
He looked at me. Then he looked east again. Whatever was at the boundary, I noted, he was choosing to stand between it and me — not dramatically, not visibly, but the geometry of where he was standing relative to where I was standing had adjusted, and I had been paying attention to geometry for six weeks and I noticed.
“We should go back,” he said.
“Who is it,” I said.
“Someone who will be introduced properly,” he said, “at a time I choose rather than a time they’ve decided on.” His voice had the particular feel it used for things that were settled rather than open to discussion, the flat certainty of ancient authority, and I had learned by now which things were genuinely closed and which were closed for the moment and would open later. This felt like both, and I made the decision that later was the right time and now was the time to walk back through the silver forest with the atmospheric shift brightening the trees around us and the Hollow’s alertness in the ground under my feet.
“All right,” I said.
He moved. I fell into step beside him — and this was new, I noted it immediately, the beside rather than the behind or the directed-path arrangement of the training sessions. He had adjusted from instructional proximity to walking proximity — a different geometry, side by side, our pace matching without either of us announcing an intention to match, the specific ease of people whose rhythms had acquired enough familiarity to synchronize without effort.
I noticed this. I did not say anything about it.
He did not say anything about it either.
We walked.
*   *   *
The walk back through the atmospheric shift was beautiful in a way that felt deliberate — the Hollow showing off, or maybe just being what it was without the filter of someone watching it nervously. The trees were brightest during the shift.
I wanted to stop and look. I was aware this was not the appropriate response to a situation that had just included an unidentified Vorath at the eastern boundary and an express suggestion to return to the Hall, and I did not stop and look.
But I looked while I walked, because looking while walking was available to me and I was not going to miss this.
“You’re reading the trees,” he said.
“They’re doing something new,” I said. “I want to understand what.”
“The bioluminescence responds to the Hollow’s atmospheric state,” he said, and I could hear that he was doing what he sometimes did — adjusting into the teaching register because information was available and the asking was implicit. “Different trees at different ages produce different responses. The oldest produce the most light during lateral shifts — they’ve been present for enough shifts that the response has deepened. It’s accumulation.”
“The same way the Hollow accumulates memory,” I said.
“In a similar way,” he said. “Everything in the Hollow accumulates. It’s the property of the realm.”
“Including you,” I said.
He was quiet for a step, two steps, three. I did not rush it. “Including me,” he said.
I looked at the oldest trees — the ones producing the near-white intensity, the ones whose light was bright enough to make real shadows on the silver ground — and I thought about accumulation. About a thousand years of lateral shifts and each one deepening the response. About a person who accumulated the way the Hollow accumulated, steadily and without announcement, carrying everything forward with the patience of something old enough to know that nothing was wasted.
We came out of the forest at the Hall’s east entrance. It watched us come in with the composed attending quality and then, with the specific deliberateness of a creature that made choices, it dropped from the wall and walked with us to the door. Not following — walking. The three of us to the door.
At the door it stopped.
I looked down at it. It looked up at me. Then, for the first time in six weeks of increasingly close proximity, it did something it had not done before: it pressed the top of its head briefly, lightly, against my hand.
Cold. Not his cold — different, the cold of something made of shadow and light, the cold of the membrane itself. Present for two seconds, then gone.
I stood at the door of the Hall with the ghost of that cold in my hand, looking at the shadow-fox, which had resumed its composure and its attending with the air of something that had done what it intended to do and was satisfied.
I became aware that Aleksei had gone still beside me.
Very still. The specific stillness I had first noted in the alley when the binding activated — not his managed stillness, the stillness from the outside in. I looked at him. He was looking at the shadow-fox with an expression I had not seen before and could not fully read: something that was old and was affected and was working hard not to show either.
“Aleksei,” I said.
He took a breath. It was such a deliberate breath — the kind that people took when they were assembling something. “It hasn’t done that,” he said, “to anyone. In the decades it’s been in this territory.”
“Is that significant,” I said.
He looked at me. The expression was still there, the old-and-affected, and underneath it something I had the closest thing yet to a vocabulary for — the same quality as the warmth Vasilisa had described, the careful-and-present, the thing that did not look at you the way things looked at you when they were noting your presence and looked at you the way things looked at you when your presence mattered to them.
“Yes,” he said. Quietly. “It is.”
We went inside.
*   *   *
I went straight to my room because I needed ten minutes of not being in anyone’s presence, including Sable’s, and the binding told me Aleksei had gone to the north wing rather than the north study, which was different and which I was going to think about later.
I sat on the edge of my bed. I held my hand in my lap — the one the shadow-fox had pressed its head to — and I looked at it with the same attention I had given his cold — the attention of someone cataloguing something they intended to return to.
The cold had been different. Two different colds in six weeks: his cold, the internal temperature, the one that lived in the skin for an hour. And the shadow-fox’s cold, the membrane-cold, the sense of something that existed in both registers at once. Both cold. Categorically different.
I looked at my hand for a while.
Then I looked at the fox on the windowsill. The carved wooden one, worn smooth by centuries of handling, sitting in its composed way with its ears forward and its tail curled and its expression that had no expression, which was its expression.
“There’s someone at the eastern boundary,” I told it. “He called it another territory. He stood between me and the direction they were in without mentioning he was doing it.” I paused. “He had an expression I have not catalogued before.”
The fox attended to the middle distance.
“I have one hundred and forty-seven catalogued observations,” I said. “That is not a small number. The fact that there is a new one after six weeks is — it’s notable. Is what it is.”
The fox remained composed.
“Right,” I said. “That’s what I thought too.”
I got up and went to my desk. I opened my notes to a fresh page. I wrote, at the top, the new observation, in medium handwriting — not marginal, not full view, medium, the category I now had for things that were true and unresolved:
New expression. When he looked at the shadow-fox touching my hand. Old and affected and working hard not to show either. I do not have a word for it yet.
Below it, after a moment:
There is someone at the eastern boundary. The Hollow went on alert. He walked beside me without deciding to.
Below that, small but not marginal:
I did not feel afraid of the thing at the boundary. I felt — close. To him. The proximity was different in the forest than in the courtyard. Side by side through the brightened trees, our pace matching, the shadow-fox ahead of us, the Hollow alert around us.
I stopped writing.
I sat with what I had written for a long time, in the room that the binding told me was his building, in the hall he had been coming to every day for six weeks, with the coat on the chair and the fox on the windowsill and the question at the top of my sequence that was, I was becoming genuinely sure, going to ask itself within the week whether I was ready or not.
Outside my window, the silver forest was still running bright from the atmospheric shift. Every tree producing its most light, the accumulation of all their previous shifts in the quality of this one, the oldest ones burning near-white against the Hollow’s deep sky.
Somewhere to the east, past the territory’s boundary, something was still there.
And Aleksei was in the north wing rather than the north study, which he had gone to every evening for six weeks. A different room tonight. I did not know what was in the north wing. I knew the binding was telling me he was stationary and close and that the register of his state — the general wellbeing, the blunt instrument that was finer-grained than it had any right to be — had the composure of someone who was thinking through something difficult.
Old and affected and working hard not to show either.
Something the shadow-fox had done that it had not done in decades had produced that expression, and the shadow-fox had pressed its head to my hand, and the connection between those two facts was available to me if I wanted to draw it.
I drew it.
The Hall was warm. The trees were bright. And somewhere in the north wing, the person who had been here every day for six weeks and had walked beside me without deciding to was thinking through something difficult in a room he didn’t usually use, and the binding made that fact as immediate and present to me as if I were in the room with him, and I found — sitting in my room with my hand in my lap and the cold of the shadow-fox still faintly present in the skin — that I minded less about the thing at the eastern boundary than I probably should have.
Because he had stood between me and the east.
Without mentioning it.
I added one more line to the page, in handwriting that was not quite any of the three categories because it required its own:
I am not afraid of what’s coming.
I looked at this. Then, below it, smaller:
I don’t know if that’s because I should be braver or because something has changed about what I consider close enough to be safe.
The forest burned silver-white outside my window.
I did not close the curtain.
The curtain stayed open — and somewhere in the silver forest, the shadow-fox made its quiet vibration, as if answering my decision.
And somewhere beyond Nochval’s eastern boundary, in the territory where winter lived year-round and the cold had opinions, Zora was reporting what she had seen to whoever asked.
She had said I was not afraid. She had said it was interesting.
I had the street-level certainty that being interesting to a winter Vorath was not, actually, the safe position I had thought it was when I said it.


Chapter Fifteen: How Interesting
Kira
I knew something was wrong before I was fully awake.
Not wrong in the way of danger presenting itself — wrong in the way of an environment that had changed register overnight, the register of a space that had been one thing when I went to sleep and was another thing when I woke up. I had developed this sense in Vreshka over years of sleeping in places that were not always safe, where the difference between a room at rest and a room with a problem in it was sometimes the only information you had before you had to be already moving.
I lay still for a moment and gathered the available data.
The Hall was cold. Not the ambient cold of the Hollow that I had acclimated to over six weeks and that registered as normal now — colder than that. Specifically colder, in the way of a temperature that was not produced by the environment but imposed on it, pushed into the air from somewhere external and sitting there in the Hall’s rooms and corridors like a presence rather than a climate.
The presences were quiet.
This was the thing that moved me from lying still to sitting up. The presences were quiet in a way I had not felt them be quiet before. Not the exhaled quiet of after Sable’s first visit, satisfied and settled. This was smaller. Pulled in. The particular feel of things that had made themselves less visible, less present, less loud, because something was in the Hall that they did not want to attract the attention of.
The presences were afraid.
I was out of bed before I finished forming the thought.
The binding: Aleksei was at the Hall’s main entrance. Not the north study, not the territory — the entrance, stationary, with the feel of state the binding communicated as alert in the specific way I had come to read as: he is positioned, he has made a decision about positioning, he is between something and something else.
I put on the coat. I went downstairs.
*   *   *
She was already inside.
I heard her before I saw her — or rather, I felt her, different from the feeling of Aleksei’s presence or Vasilisa’s or Sable’s or Radovan’s brief visits, all of which had a quality I could identify and place. It was the quality of winter. Not winter as a season, not the cold of low temperatures and bare trees — winter as a force. The specific property of a thing that isolated other things from each other, that turned the air between people into a barrier rather than a medium.
Then I came around the corner of the main hall corridor and saw her, and I understood that I had not prepared adequately for what she would look like.
She was standing near the main hall’s center table — the Vorath never sat. She was tall the way Aleksei was tall, more present than the space usually accommodated. Her eyes, when they found me — and they found me the instant I came into the room, before I had fully turned the corner, before I had made any sound — were a pale grey so close to colorless that in the Hall’s ambient light they registered as silver and in another light would have been almost transparent.
She was beautiful in the way that very dangerous things were sometimes beautiful: completely, without apology, as a property of what they were rather than an effort they made.
Aleksei was between her and the room’s interior. Not dramatically — the geometry of it was not a blockade, not a statement. But he was positioned between the door and where I now stood, with the specific arrangement of a person who had placed himself there before I arrived and had done it quietly enough that only someone paying the right kind of attention to geometry would notice.
I noticed.
He looked at me when I came in. Something in his expression was controlled more completely than I had seen it controlled in six weeks — not the ordinary controlled nothing, but a different layer of it, a managed quality that had more effort in it than usual, which told me everything I needed to know about what the usual cost of that control was and what it was costing him now.
“Kira,” he said. His voice was even. Completely. “This is Zora.”
Zora looked at me with the near-transparent eyes. She smiled.
The smile did not reach her eyes, not because she was concealing something, but because her eyes were a different system from the smile entirely — they were assessing, precise, the weight of something that looked at things to know them rather than to communicate about the looking. The smile was courteous. The eyes were working.
“Kira,” she said. Her voice was the voice of the Hall’s cold this morning — the same quality, the same register, something that moved through the air in a way that left the air a little less warm after it had passed. “I’ve heard about you.”
“I’ve heard about you,” I said.
It was not, technically, true. I had inferred her at the eastern boundary from the Hollow’s alertness and Aleksei’s geometry and the presences’ behavior now. I had not been told her name until thirty seconds ago.
But she did not need to know the specifics.
Something moved in her expression — not warmth, something sharper than warmth, the quality of interest arriving in a face that had not been expecting to find something interesting. Her eyes completed their assessment and settled. “Did you,” she said.
“You were at the eastern boundary yesterday,” I said. “The Hollow went on alert.”
The room was very quiet.
