The presences, which had been making themselves small since before I came downstairs, were doing their absolute minimum — barely a warmth, barely a presence, the posture of things that had pressed themselves flat against the walls and were hoping not to be noticed. I was aware of Aleksei’s stillness to my left, the particular quality of it, the stillness that was working.
“It did,” Zora said. She tilted her head slightly — a small movement, the specific gesture of something recalibrating an estimate. “You read the Hollow’s atmospheric state.”
“I’m learning to,” I said.
“In six weeks,” she said. Not a question. A measurement.
“I have a good teacher,” I said.
*   *   *
The conversation that followed was the most precisely managed conversation I had ever been in, including negotiations in Vreshka’s lower city where the wrong word cost money and the word after that sometimes cost something more permanent.
Zora was not hostile. That was the first and most important thing to establish, and I established it in the first five minutes with the attention I applied to reading rooms: she was not hostile to me. She was something that required more precision than hostile — she was interested, in the way of something that had encountered an unexpected variable and was assessing its properties and implications with the thoroughness of someone for whom variables were either assets or problems and the determination of which category applied was a matter of gathering sufficient information.
She asked about Zemya. Zora’s questions about Zemya were specific: which city, which district, what work, how long had I been doing it, had I been in the lower city all my life. Not interest in Zemya. Interest in me, specifically, in the contours of the life that had produced me and whether those contours told her anything useful.
I answered accurately and without elaboration — the strategy for conversations where the information you gave was going to be used in ways you hadn’t agreed to. Accurate, because she was the kind of person who would identify a lie with the efficiency of someone who had been listening to people for centuries. Without elaboration, because elaboration was what gave you away — not the facts, the shape you put around the facts.
She asked about the coat.
She did it so smoothly that it was nearly not a question — a passing observation, a small comment about what I was wearing worked into a longer sentence about the Hall’s temperature. “The cold has been unusual this week,” she said. “Though I see you’ve come prepared.” “Old,” she said. “Aleksei’s, unless I’m mistaken.”
“Mine,” I said.
Her eyes moved to him. His face gave her nothing — the full controlled nothing, the absolute surface. She looked back at me. “Of course,” she said pleasantly. “My mistake.”
It was not her mistake. She had not made a mistake. She had taken a measurement and received an answer she had been expecting and had filed both the question and the answer in whatever accounting she kept, and the pleasantly was the polished surface over a quality that was not pleasant and was not interested in being pleasant, it was interested in information and it had gotten some.
I held her gaze. She held mine. We maintained this with the understanding of two people who knew exactly what had just happened and were not going to discuss it directly.
“You’re not afraid,” she said.
The observation had the feel of something being said aloud that had been noted internally some time ago. “Should I be,” I said.
“Most mortals are,” she said. Not unkindly — factually. “At this proximity. The Vorath presence is uncomfortable for mortal sensory systems. You’ve been here long enough to acclimate to Aleksei and to some degree Nochval’s territory generally. I’m a new presence. The discomfort should be significant.”
“It’s significant,” I said honestly. “You’re very cold. The Hall has gotten cold since you arrived and I can feel that it’s you producing it rather than the environment. It’s not comfortable.” I paused. “That’s not the same as afraid.”
She looked at me for a long moment with the near-transparent eyes. Then something shifted in her expression — not warmth, not softening, but the quality of genuine attention arriving on a face that had previously been deploying polished performance. She was actually looking at me now. Not measuring, not assessing for the accounting. Looking.
“No,” she said. “It isn’t.” A pause, exactly calibrated. “How interesting.”
These were the two most alarming words anyone had said to me in six weeks, and I had been caught by an ancient god-king in an alley during the Hunt.
*   *   *
She stayed for forty minutes.
She and Aleksei spoke for a portion of that time in the specific register of two beings who had a great deal of history and were not going to perform warmth about it — not cold exactly, not hostile, simply the feel of two people who knew each other too well for courtesy to be necessary and too cautiously for ease to be comfortable. I sat at the table and I listened and I gathered the things available in the listening.
Zora’s territory bordered Nochval’s to the east. This I had established from the boundary visit. What I established now was the specific nature of that border — it was old, and had been renegotiated twice in the last century, and the renegotiation had not gone smoothly, and the way Aleksei referenced the second renegotiation, very briefly, with a quality in his voice I had not heard him use for anything else, told me the second renegotiation had cost something beyond territory.
She had known about my presence since the night of the Hunt. This was not explicitly stated — it was present in the way she talked about the Veil Nights and the Hunt’s outcome and the specific question she asked about whether all thirteen Vorath had been present for the Veil Nights, which she knew the answer to, which told me the question was not about information but about what the answer would tell her about what he would say. He said yes, all thirteen, and gave nothing further, and she received this with the pleasantly that was not pleasant and moved on.
She had not come for forty minutes of courtesy. She had come to see me. I understood this by the twenty-minute mark and confirmed it at thirty-five when she turned back to me in the middle of a sentence directed at him and asked, with the specific precision of something that had been working toward a question the entire time: “How are you finding the Hollow’s library?”
I went still.
Not visibly — I had been keeping my visible stillness consistent throughout and I was not going to change it now. But internally, in the place where the question landed: still. She knew about the library. She knew about the books. She knew about the old-language shelf, or she was feeling for whether she should know.
“Extensive,” I said. “I’ve been working through the historical sections. I don’t have enough of some of the languages yet for the older materials.”
“The older materials require particular preparation,” she said, with the same pleasant surface over the working machinery of her attention. “Some of them are not safe for mortals without the right foundation.”
“I’ve been careful,” I said.
“I’m sure you have,” she said, and looked at Aleksei, and the look between them was a conversation I was not part of and that lasted perhaps two seconds and that communicated more in those two seconds than most conversations communicated in an hour.
He said nothing. His face gave her nothing. But the nothing had a quality — resolute, the feel of a door that was not going to open regardless of what was applied to it — and she read it and looked back at her hands and said, “Well,” in the tone of someone setting a conversation down temporarily.
She left five minutes later. The Hall’s warmth did not fully return for eleven minutes. I counted. Zora’s passage had cost us something, and I did not yet know what.
*   *   *
The Hall took ten minutes to warm up after she left.
Not gradually — in increments, each one perceptible, the specific process of a space recovering from a cold that had been imposed rather than natural. Then the ambient temperature of the rooms, rising degree by degree back toward the normal that I had spent six weeks establishing as my baseline. Then the presences fully, with the specific warmth I had come to know as the Hall at ease — present, attending, warm with the sense of something that found its current state satisfactory.
I sat at the table and watched the Hall warm up and catalogued the process with the same attention I gave everything, because the process was information and the information was that even the Hall, which was eight hundred years old and had been built by a being older than that and was not a thing that alarmed easily, had been suppressed by Zora’s presence for the full forty minutes.
Aleksei came back into the main hall after seeing her out. He had the expression that was not any of the catalogued ones — not the controlled nothing, not the almost-not-a-smile, not the new one from the forest. This was another new one, and it had the manner of someone who had just managed something difficult and was now assessing how the management had gone.
“She was not hostile,” I said.
“No,” he said.
“That’s worse.”
He looked at me with the silver eyes in the warming room and something shifted in the expression — not becoming warm exactly, but less managed. More present. “Yes,” he said.
“She came to see what I was,” I said. “She already knew I was here. She came because she needed to determine for herself what the information meant.”
He was quiet.
“She asked about the library,” I said.
“I know,” he said. And then: “I heard the question.”
“She knows about the old-language section.” I was not asking. I was building the sequence out loud, because building it out loud was faster than building it silently when the person across from you had more information than you did and you needed them to confirm the architecture so you could work with it. “She doesn’t know what I’ve done in it or she was testing whether you would tell her. You didn’t tell her anything.”
“No,” he said.
“You’re not going to tell her,” I said.
“No,” he said, with the flat certainty that was the door that would not open. Not protective in the performed sense. Decided. The way of someone for whom the decision had been made before the situation presented itself and was not going to be reconsidered by the situation.
I looked at him across the table in the warming hall. The presences were at full warmth now, recovered, producing the quality of the Hall at ease. The coat was on my shoulders. The question that had been working toward the top of my sequence for two weeks pressed against the back of my throat with the specific urgency of something whose time was nearly here.
Not yet. Not this moment. This moment was for the Zora accounting, for the building of the sequence that now included her.
“She’s going to come back,” I said.
“Yes,” he said.
“And she’ll be watching now. For whatever she came to find.” I thought about the near-transparent eyes and the measurement quality of them and the specific sharpening of her attention when I had said it’s not the same as afraid. “I think she found it interesting that I wasn’t afraid.”
“So did I,” he said.
I looked at him. This one was simpler than all of those. Simpler and, in its simplicity, more legible. As if the management had gotten tired for exactly one moment and stepped back from the door.
I held it for as long as it lasted, which was perhaps three seconds, and then he moved — back to the door, toward the north wing, with the economy of motion that always indicated the conversation had reached its end and he had somewhere to be. “I’ll tell you about her,” he said, at the threshold. “When I have—”
“The right words,” I said.
“Yes,” he said.
“How long,” I said. Not impatiently — genuinely. I wanted to know the timeline.
He stopped at the threshold. He did not turn around, but the set of his shoulders changed — a small shift, something processing. “Soon,” he said. “Before she comes back.”
Then he was gone. I sat in the warming hall with the presences and the coat and the fox on the windowsill upstairs and the question that was now pressing against the top of its sequence like something that had run out of patience with being second.
*   *   *
Sable arrived forty minutes later.
She had not been in the Hall during Zora’s visit — I had noticed her absence and had not had time to think about it — and she came in through the east corridor door with the quality she sometimes had of arriving from the membrane’s surface rather than through the Hall’s normal entrances, a slight density to the air around her that resolved into her solidity over the first few seconds of her presence in a room.
She looked at me. She looked at the hall around us. She assessed the residual cold with the specific attention of someone taking a temperature reading. “How long was she here,” she said.
“Forty minutes,” I said.
Sable sat across from me at the table. Her hair was in its deepest register today, nearly the not-black of the Hollow’s nadir sky, which I had learned to read as: the membrane had been active recently, the Hollow’s atmosphere had been doing something that required Sable’s particular attention. She looked at the table for a moment and then looked at me. “Are you all right,” she said.
“Yes,” I said. “She wasn’t hostile. She was—”
“Interested,” Sable said. “I know.”
“You’ve met her,” I said.
“Many times,” she said, in the tone she used for things that had a history she was not going to provide all of right now. “The membrane runs along Nochval’s eastern boundary because Zora’s territory is cold enough to keep it thin. We have overlapping interests in the thin-membrane regions and therefore overlapping presence in the same spaces.” A pause. “It is not an easy relationship.”
I thought about the Hall going cold. About the presences pressing flat against the walls. About Sable, who had been in this Hall for eight hundred years and who the presences adored, choosing not to be in the Hall while Zora was in it. “You stayed outside,” I said.
“Her presence and mine in the same space produces complications,” she said carefully. “Winter and the membrane do not run together easily. I thought it was more useful for you to meet her without that complication in the room.”
I filed this in the place I was keeping information about Zora and what she represented. “She asked about the library,” I said. “The old-language section.”
Sable went still in the specific way — not her usual not-quite-solid quality, but a sharpening. “What did you say.”
“That I didn’t have enough of some of the older languages yet,” I said. “I didn’t mention the book. Or what happened with the mark.”
Sable held my gaze for a moment. Then something in her expression settled — not relief exactly, the Remnant version of it, which was more like: the thing that was a risk is still contained. “Good,” she said quietly. “That was exactly right.”
“Why does it matter what Zora knows,” I said. “About the book. About the Claim.”
Sable was quiet for a moment. This was not the calibrated quiet she used for deciding how much information to give — this was the real kind, the kind where she was working something out in the private interior of her own understanding before she brought it out. When she spoke, it was in the voice she used for things she was saying because they needed to be said rather than because she had decided the moment was right.
“The Old Claim makes you significant,” she said. “Not to me, not to the Hall, not to Aleksei — those are personal responses. The Claim makes you significant to the Hollow itself. You are the first carrier with a full Claim resonance in three hundred years. Do you understand what that means for the politics?”
“And when she does,” I said.
“When she does,” Sable said, “things become considerably more complicated. For you. And for him.”
The hall was fully warm now. The presences were present, attending, producing their ordinary warmth. The coat was on my shoulders, mine now in the way things were mine that had been given with care, and the shadow-fox was somewhere in the territory, and the binding put Aleksei in the north wing still, close and stationary, thinking something through in the room he had gone to when the forest had brought the atmospheric shift and the shadow-fox had done the unprecedented thing.
For you, Sable had said. And for him.
The and for him was the part I was going to think about.
“Sable,” I said.
“Mm.”
“The right question,” I said. “The one that goes first. Is it about the Claim.”
She looked at me with the membrane-colored eyes that always pulled at my recognition and said nothing for a moment. Then: “It is adjacent to the Claim,” she said. “The Claim is the answer. The question is about you.”
“Soon,” she said. “I think you’re ready.”
I had been almost ready for three weeks. I was not sure ready was the right word for what I currently was. But I nodded, and Sable went to make tea, and the Hall was warm around us with the specific warmth of something that had been suppressed and had recovered its full nature, and somewhere upstairs the fox sat on the windowsill attending to the middle distance with its composed ears forward expression, and I sat at the table and thought about the question that was about me.
About what I was.
About why an ancient being with near-transparent eyes had looked at me, for two seconds, with genuine attention rather than assessment and said: how interesting.
I added a line to my mental notes, in the medium handwriting, below the entry about Zora’s visit:
She is not the danger. The danger is what she represents — the world beyond this territory learning what the Hollow already knows about me, before I know it myself.
And below that, very small, in handwriting that had no category because I only wrote it to have written it:
He said soon. Before she comes back.
I thought: then let her come back quickly.
I was ready.
Sable appeared in the main hall doorway an hour after Zora left. She did not come all the way into the room — she stood at the threshold with the manner of someone delivering information that was not going to be pleasant.
“She will tell them,” Sable said. “About you. About the binding’s behavior. About the library.”
“I know,” I said.
“And they will have opinions,” Sable said. “About what you are. About what you mean for the territorial balance. About whether restoration is actually what Nochval needs or whether it’s what Aleksei wants.” She paused. “Some of those opinions will not be friendly.”
*   *   *
The book was wrong.
I went to the library the morning after Zora’s departure and the reaching-toward quality — the mutual pull between carrier and text that had been constant for weeks — was gone. The book was paper. Ink. Binding. Nothing else.
Aleksei was in the courtyard. The binding had already told him something was wrong. I could see it in his posture — the shift from territory-attention to her-attention.
“The book,” I said.
He was already moving.
He held it for a long time, the silver eyes listening rather than reading. “The suppression came from outside the territory,” he said. His voice was even but the binding was telling me something I hadn’t felt from him before. Cold anger, running deep. “Through the membrane. At the eastern boundary, where Zora’s passage weakened the barrier.”
“She did this?”
“Not Zora. The political cost of direct sabotage to a Claim artifact is absolute. But her passage created a window. Someone used it.”
Maren. Neither of us said the name.
“How long until it comes back?” I said.
“I need to close the breach at the boundary. Four hours. While I am there, I am not here.”
“Go,” I said. “The Hall won’t let anything else through.”
He looked at me with something that cracked the controlled surface — the weight of a person about to leave the thing they were protecting.
“I’ll be here,” I said. “I am not the kind of person who needs to be told twice to stay inside.”
“You went out on the Veil Nights for sixty silver marks.”
“That was my money. This is my book.”
He left at Vorath speed. The Hall went quiet around me. The presences pulled tight.
I sat in the library with my hand on a dead book and waited.
The book came back in the fourth hour — gradually, like warmth returning to a room after a fire is relit. But different. The reaching-toward was urgent now, less patient, as if the suppression had taught it something about the cost of interruption.
When Aleksei returned: “The breach is closed. I found evidence of deliberate manipulation. Not proof enough for a formal accusation. But enough to know.”
“Enough to know it’s not theoretical anymore,” I said.
“Yes. The politics have become practical.”
I lost two days. Two marks I could not get back. And the timeline to the convening had just gotten shorter by exactly what we could least afford.
I looked at her. “How unfriendly.”
“The kind that sends representatives to convenings,” Sable said. “The kind that votes.”
I thought: then let her come back quickly. I was ready.
I was ready, but I suspected I was ready. But the book on the shelf had gone quiet again — not the reaching-toward silence of something waiting, but the held-breath silence of something that had been told to stop. Someone had reached into our library a second time. And this time they had not taken anything. They had left a message.


Chapter Sixteen: The Words I Found in the Night
Aleksei
Zora left Nochval through the eastern membrane at the boundary, and I felt her signature withdraw the way you felt a sustained pressure withdraw: not gradually, completely, the specific relief of a thing that had been maintained ceasing all at once.
The Hall took ten minutes to recover. I counted them. Not because I was uncertain when the recovery would be complete — I could feel it, the presences returning to their full warmth, the ambient temperature of the rooms climbing back toward normal — but because counting was what I did when I was managing something significant and needed the precision of a number to anchor the managing to.
Ten minutes. The Hall was not easily suppressed. I had built it to resist exactly that kind of pressure, the cold authority of a winter Vorath who could impose her temperature on spaces the way she imposed it on everything — completely, without asking permission, because the asking had never been part of her method and she had been too powerful too long to start now.
The Hall had held. The presences had made themselves small and quiet; they did this when they could not fight something and had the sense to know it, but they had not been extinguished. They were back now, warm and present and doing the specific thing they did when they were unsettled and were working through it — a slightly restless quality to the warmth, attending in several directions at once rather than the singular attending of their settled state.
I understood the feeling.
*   *   *
I went to the north wing after I saw Zora out.
Not the north study. I had kept one of them functional. A reading chair, a fire grate, a window that faced north toward the oldest part of the territory where the trees were tallest and their light was the deep silver of great age.
I sat in the reading chair. This was notable. I did not sit. I had told her this on the second day — it doesn’t particularly serve a purpose — and I had meant it, and I still meant it, and sitting now was the specific decision of someone who had a great deal to think through and intended to do it with the full commitment of someone who was not going anywhere until it was done.
I sat and I thought about Zora.
We had known each other for the better part of eight hundred years. Not always in the same territory, not always in contact — there were centuries where the boundaries between our territories had been clear and the contact had been minimal and the minimal contact had been, on balance, the more stable arrangement. The last century had not been the more stable arrangement.
The second boundary renegotiation had been eighty years ago. I had not thought about it in a careful way in some time — there was a category of thing that was easier not to think about carefully, and the second renegotiation lived in that category, and I had maintained it there with the specific discipline of someone who knew that some processes were better handled by being set aside than by being examined in full. I did not have that option anymore.
Zora had come to see Kira.
She had come because she had felt the Hollow’s response to the new presence in Nochval, and because she had felt the binding’s behavior, and because in eight hundred years of knowing me she had never felt either of those things from my territory and she had needed to come and see them with her own eyes rather than trust the information from other sources.
She had come, and she had seen, and she had asked about the library in a tone that communicated she already knew the answer to the question and was watching what I would not say about it.
And Kira had said: I don’t have enough of some of the older languages yet. Accurate, without elaboration, giving Zora the minimum she could give while producing an answer that sounded complete.
I had been watching Kira manage Zora for forty minutes and I had felt, in the binding, the composure of someone who was alert and engaged and holding herself with the precise economy of a person who was in a situation they had assessed correctly and were navigating on the assessment. She had not been rattled. She had not been afraid, which she had said directly to Zora’s face and which had produced the most honest expression I had seen on Zora in forty years — genuine interest, unperformed, genuine interest arriving in a face that had stopped expecting it.
She had told Zora she had a good teacher.
The north wing’s window showed me the oldest trees at the territory’s edge, their light deep silver, slow and steady in the Hollow’s night register. The trees were unchanged. There was a comfort in that and also a weight in it that I was not going to examine further tonight.
Tonight I needed to think about the words.
*   *   *
The words.
I had been working on them for seven weeks. But I had been working on them. The approach, the sequence, the specific order in which information would build toward understanding rather than arriving as a fact without a foundation.
The problem with words, for things of this kind, was not the finding of them. Words existed. The problem was the architecture of them — the order, the weight, the specific arrangement that would allow the information to land without distortion.
Because the information, distorted, would be wrong in a way I could not correct after the fact.
What I needed to tell her was this:
The Hollow was dying. Had been dying for a century — not collapse but a slowing, a dimming. The connection between the Hollow and Zemya thinning year by year. And I had known about the problem for longer than she had been alive, and I had not, for a century, had a solution.
The Old Claim was a living covenant from the first world. The first world, before separation, had chosen certain beings to carry its memory forward across the split. Not passive memory — active. The capacity to understand, to communicate with, to potentially restore what the separation had damaged. A hundred years of the Hollow dying was the consequence of a hundred years without the Claim’s presence.
She carried it. The Hollow had known it since the first night. The binding had known it since the first moment of contact. I had known it since the morning after the Hunt when I stood in the old territory with my hand examined and felt the shape of what had locked into place.
The Claim did not compel her. The law had bound her to Nochval for a year but the Claim was not law — it was nature, older than law, older than the separation, older than any of the structures that had been built on top of it since. The Claim was simply what she was. What she had always been. What the first world had decided when it chose, with the patience of something that operated across spans of time that made a thousand years look like a breath, who would carry the capacity for continuity forward into whatever came next.
And she had not been told.
She had been living in this Hall for seven weeks, building her sequence, asking her questions, learning the Hollow’s grammar, touching the book’s mark and receiving the sub-linguistic concept with the steady composure of someone who managed extraordinary things as a professional practice — and she had not been told what she was. What she carried. Why the Hollow knew her.
I had been working on the words for seven weeks and the reason the words had taken this long was not that I couldn’t find them. It was that I had been afraid of them. Specifically afraid, in the way that someone who did not fear much was afraid of the things that remained: afraid of telling her something this significant in terms that were inadequate to it, afraid of the shape of her face when she understood, afraid of whatever she would decide after the understanding arrived.
She could leave.
Not because of the Claim — the Claim was not the binding, the Claim was not law. But the year had a mid-point and after the mid-point the option to take the memory and go had technically always been on the table and I had technically always known it was on the table and I had not thought about it with any precision until Zora’s visit had made the precision unavoidable.
Zora knew something significant was happening in Nochval. She did not know the specifics but she would find them. When she found them, when she understood what Kira was, there would be pressure from outside the territory that had nothing to do with what I wanted and nothing to do with what Kira would have chosen.
She needed to know before Zora found it. She needed to know from me, with the right words, in the order that would build understanding rather than alarm.
She needed to know so that whatever she decided, she decided with the complete information. Not the partial version, not the pieces Sable had given her in groundwork increments, not the thing inferred from the Hollow’s behavior and the book and the shadow-fox and seven weeks of careful observation.
The whole thing.
I sat in the reading chair with the window and the oldest trees and the Hollow’s night sky cycling through its deepest registers, and I worked through the architecture of it, the sequence that would carry her from where she was to where she needed to be, and I found that the words, when I stopped being afraid of them, were not as hard to find as I had believed.
Some things were more difficult to word than others. I had said this to her three days ago, and it had been true. It was still true. But difficult was not impossible. Difficult was a thing that required more time and more care than easy things, and I had given this seven weeks of time and I was going to give it the remainder of what care it required now, tonight, in this room, until the architecture was complete.
The fire in the grate burned low. I fed it without getting up — the Hollow accommodated small requests of that kind, the fire responding to attention the way most things in Nochval responded to attention. I watched the flame and I worked through the sequence until the sequence was right.
It took most of the night.
*   *   *
The binding told me she was asleep by the time I had finished.
This was expected — it was the Hollow’s deep night, the sky at its nadir, the territory in its quietest register. She slept in the pattern I had learned through seven weeks of the binding’s continuous presence: soundly, in the quality of wellbeing that was genuine rest rather than managed rest, the deep quality of someone whose body knew how to recover. She had always slept well in Nochval, which had told me something from the first night about what the Hollow thought of her.
I stayed in the reading chair until the sky had begun its slow recovery toward morning. I did not sleep. I thought about the sequence, testing it against everything I knew about how she received information, the ways her mind worked through new architecture, the specific things that would build correctly and the specific things that would require more care in the delivery.
The shadow-fox moved past the north wing window at some point in the deep night. It did this sometimes — a slow circuit of the Hall’s exterior, the specific patrol of something that had decided the Hall was worth watching over. I watched it pass and thought about the moment yesterday when it had pressed its head to her hand, brief and certain, with the feel of something that had decided the time for the gesture was now.
She had looked at her hand afterward the way she looked at things she intended to return to.
I thought about the coat on the chair and the fox on the windowsill and the notes on the desk and the question at the top of her sequence that she had told me was close, that I knew from the binding and the Hall and six weeks of daily proximity was very close, that was going to ask itself with or without the architecture I had just built.
I had said soon. I had said before she comes back.
Zora would come back.
Tomorrow was soon enough. Except that tomorrow was one day closer to the convening, and every day of silence was a day the opposition used to prepare.
*   *   *
She was in the east courtyard when the Hollow’s sky reached its morning register.
I knew this before I went to look — the binding put her there, the tone of her in motion, the pattern of the exercises she did every morning that I had mapped through the binding across seven weeks and that I was not going to pretend were not mapped. I had the map. The morning exercises were forty minutes and she had been at them for perhaps twenty. I had twenty minutes.
I used fifteen of them to confirm that the architecture of the sequence was still right in the morning that it had been right in the night. It was. The architecture of the sequence did not change. It was right at night and it was right in the morning and the rightness of it had a quality I recognized as the feel of a thing that was true: it held under examination.
The remaining five minutes I used to go and find her.
She was at the east courtyard wall when I came around the corner of the Hall’s east face, going through the last portion of her exercises with the economy of someone who had done this sequence long enough that the body carried it without the mind’s full involvement, the mind free to be elsewhere. The shadow-fox was on the wall above her, which it was more often than not in the mornings now, and it looked at me when I came around the corner with the specific awareness it had given me since the forest — not the wary-of-Vorath calibration, something else, the awareness of something that understood the situation and had been watching it develop and had opinions about the rate of development.
I understood the feeling.
She finished the sequence before she looked up. Not rushing, not extending — finishing at the natural end — the bearing of someone who respected their own process and did not abbreviate it for an audience. When she looked up and saw me the expression that arrived was the one she produced when she had been expecting something and it had arrived on schedule: something between readiness and relief, the bearing of a person who had been prepared and was glad the preparation was being used.
She knew.
Not what I was going to say — she could not know that, the architecture was mine and I had built it carefully and it was not visible from the outside. The time had arrived. She could see it in the same way she read geometry — not from a declaration, from the arrangement of things.
“Morning,” she said.
“Morning,” I said.
She pulled the coat from the wall where she had left it during her exercises and put it on. She looked at me with the brown eyes in the Hollow’s morning light, direct and steady at me in the alley when she drew the knife — not threatened, simply present, fully present, the composure of someone who was giving the current moment the full weight of their attention.
“Walk with me,” I said.
She fell into step beside me without hesitation. The three of us moved through the Hall’s east grounds toward the silver trees, and the Hollow’s morning sky was in its deep-blue register above us, and the bioluminescence in the forest ahead was the steady silver of the normal state, not the brightened atmospheric-shift intensity of two days ago. Ordinary. A morning like the mornings before it.
Except it was not.
We walked in silence for a minute — not an uncomfortable silence — our silences had never been uncomfortable, and this one had the feel of a silence that was holding space for something rather than filling it. She did not prompt. She did not ask. She walked beside me with the coat around her shoulders and her hands at her sides and she gave the silence the room it needed — the thing she always did with things that required room.
I had found the words.
I was going to use them.
“The Hollow is dying,” I said. “It has been dying for a century. I want to start there because everything else is shaped by it and the shape won’t make sense without that foundation.”
She did not stop walking. She absorbed this the way she absorbed everything difficult — not flinching, not reacting immediately, giving it the quality of attention that meant: I am receiving this, I am not dismissing it, I will not respond before I have understood it. Then, after three steps: “How long has it been dying.”
“A century,” I said. “Slowly. Not visibly — you would not see it in a year or ten years. Across the longer account it is clear.”
“You’ve been watching it for a hundred years,” she said.
“Yes.”
“Alone,” she said.
I did not answer immediately. It was accurate. I had watched it alone. I had not told the other Vorath, had not brought the problem to the larger accounting, because the problem without a solution was a different kind of problem and I had not had a solution. “Yes,” I said.
She walked. I walked. The silver trees were ahead of us, their morning light steady and silver and beautiful, and I was aware of them with the particular clarity of something I had been looking at for centuries and that I might, if the dying continued and no solution was found, not be looking at in a century hence.
“Tell me the rest,” she said.
I told her the rest.
*   *   *
I told her about the Claim as truth — the first world’s recognition that certain mortals had a capacity for connection with the deep Hollow beyond binding or covenant. Something older and fundamental, the way a hand remembered the correct position of a book on a shelf after putting it back in the dark.
She made a sound that was not a word.
I continued.
The Claim was alive. The last carrier before her had died before the separation was complete. The line had gone cold. A hundred years of the Hollow dying was the consequence of a hundred years without the Claim’s active presence — the Hollow recognizing the absence of the one thing it needed for continuity and having no way to call it back.
Then she had been in an alley in Vreshka on the night of the Hunt, and the Hollow had known her before she crossed the boundary, and the binding had locked into place with the ease of something finding what it had been looking for.
She had stopped walking.
I stopped beside her. She was very still. Not the managed stillness — the absolute kind, the kind from outside in, the body arriving at a state before the mind had finished processing the cause.
I gave her the silence. I was good at silence. I had been practicing it for a thousand years and the practice had produced, if nothing else, the knowledge of when to use it.
“The book,” she said, after a long moment. “The mark on the second page.”
“Beginning,” I said. “The first mark of the actual content. I wrote that page.”
She turned to look at me. It was open in the way she had been open in the clearing when the Hollow gave her north through the ground, the two-second version, except this was not closing. This was staying open. Something working through it that she was not managing and had decided not to manage. “You wrote it,” she said.
“Before the separation,” I said. “Before most of what currently exists. The book records the first world’s account of the Claim — what it is, how it works, what it is for. I wrote the second page because the second page is the beginning of the actual content and the Hunt is mine and what is begun is mine to begin.” I paused. “The mark that answered you was mine. The beginning was — specific.”
She was looking at me. The Hollow’s morning light was on her face and she was looking at me and I was aware of being looked at by someone who was fully present and fully open and had just been given the most significant information of their life and was not flinching, was simply looking — directly, completely, giving the thing she was looking at her full weight.
“The Claim doesn’t compel me,” she said. Not a question. Testing the architecture — using it to see if it held.
“No,” I said. “It is what you are. It is not what you must do.”
“It doesn’t fix the binding,” she said.
“No,” I said. “The binding is law. The Claim is older than law.” I looked at her. “They are not the same thing.”
She received this. Something that was — I searched for the word the way I searched for all words, through the whole available range, and found it: grief. Small, specific, the grief of someone who had just been told that the central mystery of their life had been a mystery to everyone who had watched it except the one person who had not said so.
“You knew,” she said. “From the first night.”
“I suspected,” I said carefully. “I confirmed it the next morning. I have known with certainty for seven weeks.”
The silence that followed was the longest silence we had had in seven weeks of silences. She turned back to the silver trees. The shadow-fox had stopped at her left, sitting in the way it sat when it intended to stay, ears forward, the composed attending quality of something that had been waiting for this morning for some time and was now simply present in it.
“Why seven weeks,” she said, quietly.
This was the question I had known was coming and had known the most about and had the most specific and honest answer for. “Because the right words matter,” I said. “I did not want to give you the wrong version of it.” A pause. “And because I was afraid.”
She turned to look at me. The open expression was still there. Something in it had shifted — the grief moving, making room, something else arriving alongside it. “Afraid of what,” she said.
I considered the full honesty of the answer. The full honesty was available to me — I had been working toward it all night, and the architecture of the sequence had always had this at the end of it, the specific truth that was underneath the practical reason and the care over the words. “Afraid of what you would decide,” I said. “After you knew.”
The Hollow’s morning light was steady around us, silver and deep and without urgency, the light of a realm that had been patient for a century and could be patient a little longer. She was looking at me with the brown eyes and the open face and seven weeks of everything between us, and I was looking at her, and the silence had a quality I had not felt a silence have in a very long time — the weight of something on the edge of becoming something else, the held breath before the word, the moment in which every possible next thing existed simultaneously.
“I haven’t decided yet,” she said. “I want you to know that I’m not — I need time with this. To build the sequence. To understand what I’m deciding.”
“I know,” I said. “Take the time.”
“But,” she said, and stopped. She looked at the shadow-fox. She looked at the silver trees. She looked at her hands, and then at the coat, and then at me. Something in her expression was doing the thing I had no name for — the real version, not the managed surface, not the almost. The actual thing. “I want you to know,” she said, “that of the things I haven’t decided, the one I’m most certain about is staying.”
I went very still.
“Not because of the law,” she said. “Not because of the Claim or the Hollow or the sequence or Zora or any of it. Because—” She stopped. She was precise about words and the precision required a moment. “Because this is the most interesting place I have ever been,” she said, “and you are the most interesting person I have ever met, and I have been thinking about your cold hands for seven weeks and I am not finished thinking about them yet.”
The shadow-fox attended to the middle distance with the composed quality of something that had known this was coming and found the timing satisfactory. The silver trees produced their steady light. The Hollow’s morning sky was the deep blue of its best register, the most saturated it reached, the color that pressed against the eye like a held note.
I looked at her for a long moment. At the coat and the open face and the brown eyes that were giving me their full weight without managing any of it back.
“All right,” I said.
It was not a sufficient response to what she had just said. I was aware of that. But she smiled — the real one, the full one, the one that changed her whole face — and the insufficiency of my response was apparently, from the quality of that smile, not a problem.
“All right,” she said.
We stood at the silver forest’s edge in the morning light, and the shadow-fox sat between us and the trees, and the Hall behind us was warm in the specific way it had been warm for seven weeks, steady and present and pleased about things in the quiet way of something that had been waiting a very long time.
I thought about the words I had found in the night. About the architecture of the sequence and the specific fear underneath it and the thing she had just given back to the fear in exchange for saying it aloud.
About the character of it fabric you decided to notice it and stayed after you had stopped expecting it. The way it reminded you of things you had not thought about in a very long time.
I thought about sunlight.
About standing in it.
*   *   *
She said all right and she smiled and the smile was the real one and I said all right back and the inadequacy of my response was apparently not a problem.
I knew this because she told me so, directly, with the brown eyes and the open face. And I stood at the silver forest’s edge in the morning light and I received the telling and I said all right and the smile arrived and the moment was everything it was supposed to be.
And then I went inside and I could not get out of my own head.
Not immediately. The morning was fine. We had tea and she asked about the seventh mark’s timeline and I answered and she wrote things in her notes and the pattern continued. But underneath the pattern, running at a different register entirely, was the thought that had arrived when she said: not because of the law, not because of the Claim — and that thought was: how would she know?
The Claim was in her body. But the Claim oriented. The same way the deep current oriented toward the membrane, the same way the oldest trees’ roots oriented toward each other, the same way everything in the Hollow oriented toward restoration: the Claim in her was oriented toward this.
Toward me. Toward Nochval. Toward the work.
I was not the work. I was the territory keeper who happened to be present while she did the work. All of those things were real. But they were also, every one of them, in the same direction as what the Claim would have her do regardless.
How would she know the difference between what she chose and what the Claim had already chosen for her?
How would I?
I managed the morning. I managed the afternoon. I managed dinner with the specific precision of someone maintaining a surface that nothing was showing through, and she watched me across the table with the attentiveness she applied to everything and she did not say what she was seeing and I did not offer it and the dinner ended and I said goodnight at the foot of the stairs rather than the top and I went to the north wing rather than the north study and I sat in the reading chair with the window and the oldest trees and I thought about the Claim until the Hollow’s sky had cycled all the way to its nadir.
The binding told me she was awake for a long time before she slept. And the tone of her wakefulness was not rest. It was the composure of someone rearranging the architecture of their understanding.
*   *   *
She came to find me in the morning.
Not the north study — the north wing, the room I had gone to twice now and that she had therefore correctly identified as the place I went when I was working through something I had not yet resolved. She knocked. I told her to come in. She came in and she stood in the doorway with the coat on her shoulders and the expression that was not going to accept a surface.
“You’ve been in here since last night,” she said.
“Yes,” I said.
“You said goodnight at the foot of the stairs,” she said. “You’ve been saying goodnight at the top for three weeks.”
“Yes,” I said.
She came into the room. Not quickly — with the deliberate quality she brought to things she was going to address directly. She stood near the window, the oldest trees behind her in their morning light, and she looked at me with the brown eyes and the complete attention. “Tell me what happened,” she said.
“Nothing happened,” I said. The even voice. The surface.
“Aleksei,” she said. Not impatiently. With the particular feel she used for my name when she was not going to accept less than the truth. “Tell me.”
I looked at the oldest trees through the window. “The Claim orients you,” I said. “Everything about being in this territory, being in proximity to me, doing the work of restoration — all of it is in the same direction as what the Claim would have you do regardless of what you want. You said you’re staying because you want to. I don’t know how you distinguish between the two.”
The room was quiet for a moment.
“You’re worried I don’t actually choose you,” she said. “You’re worried I choose the Claim and you come with it.”
“Yes,” I said. It was easier than I had expected to say. The truth usually was, once the surface gave way. “I am.”
She was quiet for three seconds. Four. I watched her not-manage the thinking, watched it work across her face in the specific way it worked when she was building a sequence she intended to be accurate. Then she said: “You said the Claim was what I am, not what I must do.”
“Yes,” I said.
“Then it doesn’t choose,” she said. “It doesn’t want or prefer or orient. It is simply present. Like my eye color. Like the way I read geometry by looking at where people stand rather than what they say.” She held my gaze. “I choose what I do with what I am. The Claim doesn’t make that choice. I do.”
“You can’t know that for certain,” I said.
“Neither can you,” she said, without hesitation and without cruelty. Simply accurately. “You cannot know with certainty that anything you feel about me is free of the binding’s influence. The binding communicates my state to you constantly. It has been present since the first night. You are no more able to separate your feelings from the binding than I am able to separate my choices from the Claim.” She paused. “So we are the same. Both of us making choices inside systems we didn’t design and cannot fully step outside of. The question isn’t whether the systems influence us. The question is whether the choice still belongs to us.”
I was very still.
“I am choosing to stay,” she said. “Not the Hollow. Not the marks. Not the restoration. You. Specifically. The coat on the third day and the corrections in the courtyard and the seven weeks of the north study and the alley and all of it.” She took a step closer. “The Claim might be why the alley happened. It is not why I’m standing in this room at seven in the morning telling you that your cold hands have been in my head since the first week.” She stopped. “Is that clear enough?”
I looked at her in the north wing’s morning light with the oldest trees behind her and the seven weeks between us and the truth she had just said with the complete directness she brought to everything that mattered.
“Yes,” I said. “That is clear enough.”
“Good,” she said. “Then stop going to the north wing to think about it and come back to the north study where you belong.”
She left the doorway. I heard her moving back down the corridor toward the hall’s main wing. The shadow-fox moved past the window in its morning circuit with the feel of something that had been waiting for this and found the result satisfactory.
I stood. I went back to the north study.
She was already there, at the reading table, with her notes open. She did not look up when I came in. She was already working. The pattern continuing.
The morning was fine. Better than fine. The thing that had been underneath the surface overnight was not there anymore — not because it had been resolved in the sense of made to not matter, but because she had answered it and the answer had been accurate and I had received it and the receiving had done what receiving accurate things did: it settled the question so the question did not have to be carried anymore.
At some point she looked up from her notes and said: “Next time you need to think something through, you can do it in the north study. I don’t mind.”
“The north wing is for things I haven’t worked out yet,” I said.
“Then come get me before you go there,” she said. “The working-out goes faster with two.”
She looked at me with the almost-not-a-smile quality and went back to her notes. The binding showed me her state: steady, satisfied, the manner of someone who had done a difficult thing correctly and had arrived on the other side of it. The quality of someone who had meant what she said.
I had been afraid she was choosing the Claim.
She had told me she was choosing me.
And she was right that I could not verify it with certainty and she was right that neither could she and she was right that we were the same in this, both of us inside systems we had not designed, both of us making choices that belonged to us anyway.
I believed her.
That was, I thought, probably the point.
And then the foundation stone under the north wing — silent for a hundred and fifty years — produced a vibration so low I almost believed I had imagined it.
Almost.
The silence after it was the silence of something that had moved once and was deciding whether to move again.


Chapter Seventeen: The Beginning of the Actual Content
Kira
I had said a true thing out loud to a thousand-year-old being at the edge of a silver forest at dawn and then had to go back inside and have breakfast.
This was the practical reality of the situation, and I was a practical person, and I was handling it with the same methodical competence I applied to all situations that were simultaneously significant and required mundane continuation. The Hollow was dying. I carried something ancient and possibly critical to its survival. The person I had been living alongside for seven weeks had known this from the first night and had spent those seven weeks finding the right way to tell me, and the right way had been at the forest’s edge in the morning light with the shadow-fox sitting between us and the sky at its best blue and his voice doing the thing it did when he was saying something true rather than something managed.
And I had told him I had been thinking about his cold hands for seven weeks.
I sat at the breakfast table with both of these facts and ate, because eating was available to me and the alternative was not eating — not a productive response to any situation regardless of its scale.
The Hall was warm. The presences were present. The shadow-fox was at the east courtyard wall. He had gone back inside after we stood at the forest’s edge and the silence had done what it needed to do and we had both said all right and then had run out of morning. He had gone to the north study. I had come to breakfast.
The normal architecture of the day, with the disclosure sitting inside it like the oldest tree in the forest: everything continuing as it had been, and something entirely different at the center of it.
I finished breakfast. I went to the library. I sat in the right-side chair because Sable was in the left-side chair — my chair, which she was always in, and I pulled out a fresh notebook because the existing notes were no longer organized for where I was and I needed a new structure.
“Good morning,” Sable said, from my chair.
“Good morning,” I said.
She turned a page. She said nothing. The nothing lasted approximately four minutes — the amount of time it took me to open the new notebook, write the date, and stare at the first blank page with the full recognition that I had no idea where to start.
“You can talk about it,” she said, to her book.
“I’m aware,” I said.
“You don’t have to start with the Claim,” she said. “You can start with whatever is loudest.”
I thought about what was loudest. There were several candidates and they were not competing quietly. “I told him I’d been thinking about his cold hands,” I said.
Sable put her book down. The full putting-down was a thing I had only seen twice and both times it had indicated that the conversation had become the priority over everything else in the room. She looked at me with both membrane-colored eyes. “Word for word,” she said. “What you said.”
I told her. The sequence, the and I haven’t decided yet, the but, the staying, the most interesting place and the most interesting person and the cold hands for seven weeks.
Sable was quiet for a moment. Then she said, in the specific warm register she used when something had exceeded her expectations and she found that both delightful and deeply satisfying: “You said that. Out loud. To him.”
“I did,” I said.
“And he said—”
“All right,” I said.
“All right,” she repeated.
“Twice,” I said. “I said it back. We both said it.”
Sable picked up her book. She held it. She did not open it. When she spoke her voice was even. “The Claim,” she said. “Do you want to talk through what he told you.”
“Yes,” I said. “But not yet. Give me an hour to build the first structure and then I’ll need you to tell me where I’ve got it wrong.”
“All right,” she said the phrase of the morning.
I opened the notebook. I started to write.
*   *   *
The first structure took two hours, not one.
I wrote everything he had told me in the sequence he had told it, and then I wrote it again in the sequence that made internal sense to me, which was different from his sequence in some significant places — not because his sequence was wrong but because his sequence was built for my comprehension and mine was built for my working, and comprehension and working required different architectures. I drew maps in the margins. Not geographic maps — conceptual ones, the kind I used when information had relationships that were structural rather than linear, when you needed to see which things depended on which other things before you could understand any of them.
The Hollow dying: central. Everything else radiating from it. The dying as the consequence of the Claim going cold for a century. The dying not as catastrophe yet, not as crisis, but as slow and certain diminishment — the oldest trees slightly dimmer, the deep current slower, the membrane thinning at the boundaries. A hundred years of the thing that needed to be present not being present.
And me. I had been the person who located the center for other people, who found the thing that needed finding and returned it, who occupied the edges of other people’s significant situations and moved through the center when necessary and left when the job was done. I was not a center-of-the-diagram person.
Except I was. I had apparently always been. The diagram had been waiting for me to show up and the Hollow had known I was coming and had been patience-of-something-ancient about it, and the binding had locked into place with the ease of something finding what it had been looking for and the thing it had been looking for was me.
I wrote, in the margin next to the center of the diagram, in the medium handwriting:
Not a job. Not a retrieval. I am the thing that was lost and has been found.
I looked at this for a long time.
Then I wrote below it, in the small handwriting, in the space where I kept things that were true and tender and not ready for a larger size:
He found me. In an alley in Vreshka. On the night of the Hunt.
I closed the notebook. I looked at Sable. “Tell me where I’ve got it wrong,” I said.
*   *   *
She corrected two things and confirmed the rest.
The first correction: the Claim was not a mechanism in the sense I had drawn it — I had mapped it as a channel, a conduit, the thing through which continuity flowed. It was not a conduit. It was more fundamental than that. The Claim was not something you used to do a thing. Not caused by the Claim in the way that a lever caused a thing to move. Changed by the presence of the Claim the way sunlight changed a room — not through mechanism but through property.
I redrew the diagram.
The second correction: the dying was not reversible by the Claim’s presence alone. This was the one that landed with weight. Sable looked at this shape and said, carefully: “It’s more complicated than that. The Claim makes restoration possible. It is not sufficient on its own.”
“What else,” I said.
“That,” she said, “is what Book Five is about.”
I stared at her. “Book Five,” I said. “You’ve — are you telling me my life has chapters.”
“Everything has chapters,” she said, serenely. “Yours are just better documented than most.”
I looked at my diagram. I looked at the center where I had written Not a job. Not a retrieval. I am the thing that was lost and has been found. I looked at Sable with her membrane-colored eyes and her eight-hundred-years-in-this-hall and her not-quite-solid edges and the bearing of someone who had been waiting for this morning since considerably before I existed.
“You knew,” I said. “You knew what I was when you sat in my chair and read my notes.”
“I felt it when the mark answered you,” she said. “I knew before I came in. Yes.”
“And you didn’t tell me immediately because—”
“Because it was his to tell,” she said. Simply, without apology. “I gave you groundwork. I gave you the pieces that were mine to give, the context and the foundation. The thing itself — what you are, why the Hollow knows you, why the binding activated, why the book answered — that belonged to him. He wrote the second page.” A pause. “He was always going to be the one to tell you.”
I thought about the second page. About the mark for beginning under my finger and the concept arriving in the sub-linguistic register and the reaching-toward that I had been living with for seven weeks. About writing being an act of intention and intention belonging to the person who chose it and the second page being the beginning of the actual content, which he had said was his to begin because what was begun was his.
He had written the beginning.
I was the content.
The thought arrived so quietly and with such completeness that I sat with it for a long moment before I recognized what it was. Not a conclusion from the diagram. Something older than the diagram. Something that had been true since before I had the diagram to put it in.
“Sable,” I said.
“Mm.”
“The right question,” I said. “The one that goes first. You said it was about me.”
She picked up her book. She looked at it. She set it back down. “Yes,” she said.
“I think I know what it is,” I said.
She looked at me. Both membrane-colored eyes, steady, with the composure of someone who has been waiting for a specific moment and recognizes it when it arrives. “Tell me,” she said.
“It’s not why can I almost read that book,” I said. “That was the question I thought was foundational but it’s actually downstream. The right question is—” I stopped. I was being precise about it, because she had always said I would arrive at it and when I arrived at it I would know, and I was arriving at it and I wanted to name it correctly. “Why does the Hollow feel like somewhere I have always been going,” I said. “Not somewhere I arrived. Somewhere I have been in transit toward. The whole time.”
Sable was quiet.
“That’s the right question,” she said. Quietly. In the voice she used for things she was saying because they needed to be said rather than because she had decided the time was right. “And you already know the answer.”
“I’ve always been coming here,” I said. “The Claim has been — it’s been orienting me. My whole life. The way a compass points north without deciding to. I have been pointing here.” I looked at the diagram. At the center of it. “Not because I had no choice. Because it is where I belong. There’s a difference.”
“There is,” Sable said.
The library was very quiet around us. The presences in the upper reaches were warm with the feel of things that had been holding their breath for a considerable while and had just exhaled. Outside the narrow windows the Hollow’s sky was in its afternoon register, the blue beginning its long descent toward evening, and in the silver forest the bioluminescence was running in its steady ordinary way, the oldest trees producing their deep silver light with the patience of things that had been waiting for this answer to be found for a century.
I put my hand on the notebook. On the diagram inside it. On the center.
“All right,” I said, to the room, to the waiting, to the answer that had always been there.
*   *   *
The mirror was in the east corridor, second door from the main stair, exactly where he had said it was on day three.
I had been to it on the days it worked — once every three days, the silver surface communicating when it was available by a particular quality of reflection, something slightly more dimensional than an ordinary mirror, the glass showing the room and also something beyond the room, the edges of a different space. I had been going every three days. I had not missed one.
Tomasz was at his mirror on the appointed time. He was always at his mirror on the appointed time — the thing about Tomasz that I had been relying on since I was seventeen and that had never, in six years, failed to be true. He was a fence by profession and a person of his word by nature and the combination had made him the most reliable person in my life for longer than I had been consciously building a list of reliable people.
His face appeared in the silver surface with the dimensional quality of the mirror’s communication — not quite flat, not quite present, the quality of a face seen across a significant distance through a medium that was doing its best. He looked, as Tomasz always looked, like someone who had made peace with a complicated world and continued to find it fundamentally interesting. He had broad features and grey-shot hair that he had always had even when I first knew him and that made him look like a person who had been born at an approximate age and intended to stay there indefinitely.
“Kira,” he said. His voice through the mirror had the dimensional quality of the image: real, slightly distant, recognizably itself. “You look different.”
“I’m fine,” I said, reflexively.
“I didn’t say you weren’t fine,” he said, with the patience of someone who had been interpreting my reflexive I’m fine as deflection for six years. “I said you looked different. Those are not the same statement.”
I looked at my reflection in the mirror’s surface alongside his. Different was accurate. My eyes had an alertness in them that was different from the alertness I’d had in Vreshka — not sharpened by necessity but by interest, the manner of someone who was paying attention because they wanted to rather than because they had to. I was wearing the coat.
“How is it there,” Tomasz said.
“Strange,” I said. “And very good.” I paused. “I found out today what I actually am. What I carry. Why the Hollow—” I stopped, because explaining the Claim and the dying Hollow and the first world’s covenant through a three-day mirror to a fence in Vreshka was a project of some scale and I had twenty minutes before the mirror would lose its dimensional quality. “It’s complicated,” I said. “I’ll tell you everything when I can.”
“But you’re all right,” he said.
“I’m more than all right,” I said, and heard in my own voice the truth of it — not performed, not managed, simply the statement of a state. “I’m the most all right I’ve been in a long time.”
Tomasz looked at me with the eyes of someone who had been watching me operate since I was seventeen and had a complete and accurate model of my states and was now updating the model with new data. He looked at the coat. He looked at my face. He put something together in the way Tomasz put things together — quietly, without announcement, filing the conclusion without requiring me to confirm it.
“The Vorath,” he said. “The one who caught you.”
“His name is Aleksei,” I said.
Tomasz nodded. It was the nod of a person who has suspected something for some time and is receiving confirmation without satisfaction — something more careful than satisfaction, closer to concern that was working out whether it was warranted. “Is he—” He stopped. He tried again. “Are you—” He stopped again. Tomasz was not a person who said things imprecisely and he was apparently having trouble with the precision.
“I don’t know yet what it is,” I said. “I know that it is something. I know that I told him so this morning. I know that of all the things I’ve said out loud in my life that required courage, that one required the most.”
Tomasz was quiet for a moment. It looked smaller than I remembered. Not less real — I was not going to romanticize the loss of that realness, I knew what a privilege ordinary walls were. But smaller. The scale of it changed against what I was standing in.
“Is he good to you,” Tomasz said. The question a person asked when they had decided that the situation was what it was and the most useful thing was the simplest thing.
I thought about seven weeks. About the room assembled with research rather than assumption. About the coat given on the third day because it suited me better. About the library that had become mine without a conversation about it. About the word soon and the morning at the forest’s edge and the full architecture of the sequence he had spent the night building because the words deserved to be right before he gave them.
“Yes,” I said. “Very.”
Tomasz nodded again. Moved from active to acknowledged — his version of trust. “Tell me about the realm,” he said. “I want to know what it’s like.”
So I told him. Everything — the trees, the Hall, the book, the binding, Sable, Vasilisa. He listened without interruption, building his own model alongside my description.
“You’ll come back,” he said, when I finished. “At the year’s end.”
“Yes,” I said. And then: “I think I’ll come back differently.”
“You’re already different,” he said, with the matter-of-fact quality of an observation about the weather. “You were different in the third communication. I didn’t say anything because it wasn’t time yet.” He looked at me through the silver surface with the eyes that had known me since I was seventeen. “Come back differently. Come back at all.”
“I will,” I said.
The mirror’s dimensional quality was fading — the edges becoming flatter, the depth reducing, the quality of a communication that had used its available time. Tomasz’s face was still in the surface but less dimensional now, more the quality of a reflection than a window. “Kira,” he said, as the surface began to settle back toward ordinary silver.
“I know,” I said. “I’ll be careful.”
“That’s not what I was going to say,” he said. The mirror was nearly flat. “I was going to say don’t be too careful.”
The surface went flat. I stood in the east corridor with the ordinary silver of a not-currently-communicating mirror and the quality of the air in a corridor after a significant thing had just occurred in it, and I thought about don’t be too careful and what it meant in Tomasz’s vocabulary — the vocabulary of someone who had watched me be too careful for years and had learned not to say anything about it until the moment came when something was worth saying it for.
*   *   *
The afternoon training session was different.
Not in structure — same courtyard, same dark earth, same progression of the deep-Hollow navigation work that we had been building for three weeks. Different in the quality of the space between us, the air in the room that had shifted overnight and that morning and that was now something I did not have a previous category for.
He knew I had been to the mirror. He did not mention it. He came to the courtyard and he was the same that he always was, the stillness and the economy and the silver eyes and the controlled surface, except that the controlled surface had a quality today that it had not had before, a slight loosening at the edges, as if the management had decided that full deployment was no longer required for all situations.
We had said all right.
Both of us.
The full deployment was not currently required.
We worked for two hours. The correction came at forty minutes — my centre drifting, his hand on my hip redirecting. Brief, precise, gone. The rest of the session I held the correction and felt it settle into my body alongside the others.
I did not say anything about it. Neither did he.
We did not need to. We had said all right.
After the session, in the period that was neither session nor departure, he said: “How are you.”
Not the functional binding-check. The real question, the same gentle quality it had had after Vasilisa’s visit. Asking about the state of the person, specifically.
“Building the sequence,” I said. “It’s — large. It takes time.”
“I know,” he said.
“I talked to Tomasz,” I said. “Through the mirror.”
“Good,” he said. And then: “Was it helpful.”
“Yes,” I said. “He told me not to be too careful.”
Something in his expression moved. It did not become an actual smile. It didn’t need to. The distance between almost and actual was a very specific distance now and I knew exactly what it felt like to be in it. “Vasilisa,” he said, “says the same thing approximately once a month.”
“She seems like a person who would,” I said.
“She is,” he said. A pause. “She’s usually right.”
We stood in the courtyard with the Hollow’s sky going toward evening above us and both of us knowing what we were standing in and neither of us in a hurry about it. The shadow-fox was on the wall. The Hall was warm behind us. The silver forest was at the territory’s edge, running in its steady ordinary bioluminescence, the oldest trees their deep silver, patient as things that had been accumulating light for centuries and intended to keep accumulating.
“The training tomorrow,” I said. “The atmospheric work.”
“Yes,” he said.
“I want to try the membrane-reading in the older part of the territory,” I said. “Where the trees are tallest. The signal should be cleaner there.”
He looked at me with the recalibration look — the one I’d catalogued fourteen times, the assessment updating. “It will be,” he confirmed. “How did you know that.”
“The oldest trees respond most strongly to atmospheric shifts,” I said. “You told me that. If they respond most strongly, their relationship to the membrane surface should be clearest. Reading the membrane where the response is strongest seems like the better learning environment.”
He was quiet for a moment. The almost-actual quality in his expression deepened slightly, going somewhere I did not have a name for yet but was working on. “You’re planning my sessions now,” he said.
“I’m contributing to the planning,” I said. “It’s collaborative.”
“I’ve been teaching for a thousand years,” he said. “I’ve never had a collaborative session plan.”
“That seems like an oversight,” I said pleasantly.
The almost-actual moved the final distance it had been considering for seven weeks. He smiled. It lasted perhaps two seconds. It changed everything about his expression and then it was gone and the controlled surface returned, but the return was different from before, lighter, the management not quite as total as it had been at eight o’clock this morning.
I held it. The two seconds of it. The particular feel of an expression that had been in preparation for seven weeks and had finally arrived.
“Tomorrow,” he said. “The older territory.”
“Tomorrow,” I said.
He went inside. I stayed in the courtyard for a moment, with the shadow-fox on the wall and the evening sky above and the cold living in my ribs from the correction forty minutes ago and the two seconds of his actual smile still in my awareness with the feel of something I was going to keep.
I went inside. I went to my room. I opened the second notebook to a fresh page and I wrote at the top, in full handwriting, not small, not medium, the largest I had written anything in this building:
He smiled.
I looked at it.
I added, below it, in the medium handwriting, because full was for completed things and this was not completed:
I am not finished here. I have not started yet. The sequence says: this is the beginning of the actual content.
I put the pen down. The fox attended to the middle distance from the windowsill. The binding put Aleksei in the library — not the north study, the library itself, which the binding had not placed him in since the first weeks when I had been tracking his movements and he had been maintaining the careful distance.
He was in the library.
Not adjacent to it. In it.
I thought about going down. I thought about the right question that I had found today and the answer I already knew and the diagram in the first notebook and the smile in the second. I thought about Tomasz saying don’t be too careful and Sable saying the right direction and Vasilisa saying go home, she’s been alone all day.
I picked up the first notebook. I went downstairs.
He was in the library’s left-side chair — my chair, which Sable had apparently vacated, the library empty of everyone except him. He was reading. He looked up when I came in, and the thing in his expression when he looked up was not the controlled nothing and was not the managed surface. It was simply the expression of a person who was glad the other person had come.
I sat in the right-side chair.
I opened the first notebook to the diagram.
“I need to show you what I’ve built so far,” I said. “And find out where I’ve got it wrong.”
“All right,” he said. He set down his book. He leaned forward slightly, the angle of someone who intended to look at what was being shown rather than wait to be talked through it. Present. Fully. The way he was fully present in the courtyard and fully present in the forest and, I was understanding now, had always been fully present in any space I was in, from the very first night.
I showed him the diagram.
He looked at it for a long time.
“You’ve drawn it as property rather than mechanism,” he said.
“Sable corrected that one,” I said. “Before you — look at the second version.”
He looked at the second version. Some things wanted the night. Some things wanted the morning. Some things wanted the silver forest and the shadow-fox and the right words built across the whole dark and delivered at dawn.
I had time. We had time. The year was half gone and half remaining, and the Hollow was patient, and I was learning patience from everything in this realm that had accumulated it.
